Words: 0:
Words: 5:MEMOIR OF WILLIAM WELLS BROWN.
Words: 0:
Words: 0:
Words: 15:A narrative of the life of the author of the present work has been most
Words: 10:extensively circulated in England and America. The present memoir will,
Words: 10:therefore, simply comprise a brief sketch of the most interesting
Words: 12:portion of Mr. Brown's history while in America, together with a short
Words: 10:account of his subsequent cisatlantic career. The publication of his
Words: 13:adventures as a slave, and as a fugitive from slavery in his native
Words: 11:land, has been most valuable in sustaining a sound anti-slavery spirit
Words: 11:in Great Britain. His honourable reception in Europe may be equally
Words: 11:serviceable in America, as another added to the many practical protests
Words: 11:previously entered from this side of the Atlantic, against the absolute
Words: 13:bondage of three millions and a quarter of the human race, and the
Words: 9:semi-slavery involved in the social and political proscription of
Words: 7:600,000 free coloured people in that country.
Words: 0:
Words: 13:William Wells Brown was born at Lexington, in the state of Kentucky, as
Words: 15:nearly as he can tell in the autumn of 1814. In the Southern States of
Words: 13:America, the pedigree and age of a horse or a dog are carefully
Words: 15:preserved, but no record is kept of the birth of a slave. All that Mr.
Words: 12:Brown knows upon the subject is traditionally, that he was born "about
Words: 12:corn-cutting time" of that year. His mother was a slave named Elizabeth,
Words: 13:the property of Dr. Young, a physician. His father was George Higgins, a
Words: 4:relative of his master.
Words: 0:
Words: 13:The name given to our author at his birth, was "William"--no second or
Words: 12:surname being permitted to a slave. While William was an infant, Dr.
Words: 12:Young removed to Missouri, where, in addition to his profession as a
Words: 7:physician, he carried on the--to European notions--incongruous
Words: 11:avocations of miller, merchant, and farmer. Here William was employed as
Words: 14:a house servant, while his mother was engaged as a field hand. One of
Words: 11:his first bitter experiences of the cruelties of slavery, was his
Words: 12:witnessing the infliction of ten lashes upon the bare back of his
Words: 12:mother, for being a few minutes behind her time at the field--a
Words: 10:punishment inflicted with one of those peculiar whips in the
Words: 12:construction of which, so as to produce the greatest amount of torture,
Words: 12:those whom Lord Carlisle has designated "the chivalry of the South" find
Words: 4:scope for their ingenuity.
Words: 0:
Words: 13:Dr. Young subsequently removed to a farm near St. Louis, in the same
Words: 12:State. Having been elected a Member of the Legislature, he devolved the
Words: 12:management of his farm upon an overseer, having, what to his unhappy
Words: 12:victims must have been the ironical name of "Friend Haskall." The mother
Words: 13:and child were now separated. The boy was levied to a Virginian named
Words: 12:Freeland, who bore the military title of Major, and carried on the
Words: 12:plebeian business of a publican. This man was of an extremely brutal
Words: 10:disposition, and treated his slaves with most refined cruelty. His
Words: 9:favourite punishment, which he facetiously called "Virginian play," was
Words: 12:to flog his slaves severely, and then expose their lacerated flesh to
Words: 11:the smoke of tobacco stems, causing the most exquisite agony. William
Words: 13:complained to his owner of the treatment of Freeland, but, as in almost
Words: 13:all similar instances, the appeal was in vain. At length he was induced
Words: 12:to attempt an escape, not from that love of liberty which subsequently
Words: 11:became with him an unconquerable passion, but simply to avoid the
Words: 12:cruelty to which he was habitually subjected. He took refuge in the
Words: 12:woods, but was hunted and "traced" by the blood-hounds of a Major
Words: 10:O'Fallon, another of "the chivalry of the South," whose gallant
Words: 11:occupation was that of keeping an establishment for the hire of
Words: 11:ferocious dogs with which to hunt fugitive slaves. The young slave
Words: 11:received a severe application of "Virginia play" for his attempt to
Words: 9:escape. Happily the military publican soon afterwards failed in
Words: 11:business, and William found a better master and a more congenial
Words: 10:employment with Captain Cilvers, on board a steam-boat plying between
Words: 14:St. Louis and Galena. At the close of the sailing season he was levied
Words: 14:to an hotel-keeper, a native of a free state, but withal of a class
Words: 12:which exist north as well as south--a most inveterate negro hater. At
Words: 9:this period of William's history, a circumstance occurred, which,
Words: 14:although a common incident in the lives of slaves, is one of the keenest
Words: 12:trials they have to endure--the breaking up of his family circle. Her
Words: 12:master wanted money, and he therefore sold Elizabeth and six of her
Words: 9:children to seven different purchasers. The family relationship is
Words: 11:almost the only solace of slavery. While the mother, brothers, and
Words: 13:sisters are permitted to meet together in the negro hut after the hour
Words: 10:of labour, the slaves are comparatively content with their oppressed
Words: 12:condition; but deprive them of this, the only privilege which they as
Words: 13:human beings are possessed of, and nothing is left but the animal part
Words: 11:of their nature--the living soul is extinguished within them. With them
Words: 10:there is nothing to love--everything to hate. They feel themselves
Words: 13:degraded to the condition not only of mere animals, but of the most
Words: 5:ill-used animals in the creation.
Words: 0:
Words: 13:Not needing the services of his young relative, Dr. Young hired him to
Words: 12:the proprietor of the _St. Louis Times_, the best master William ever
Words: 12:had in slavery. Here he gained the scanty amount of education he
Words: 11:acquired at the South. This kind treatment by his editorial master
Words: 12:appears to have engendered in the heart of William a consciousness of
Words: 13:his own manhood, and led him into the commission of an offence similar
Words: 8:to that perpetrated by Frederick Douglass, under similar
Words: 9:circumstances--the assertion of the right of self-defence. He gallantly
Words: 11:defended himself against the attacks of several boys older and bigger
Words: 13:than himself, but in so doing was guilty of the unpardonable sin of
Words: 13:lifting his hand against white lads; and the father of one of them,
Words: 13:therefore, deemed it consistent with his manhood to lay in wait for the
Words: 14:young slave, and beat him over the head with a heavy cane till the
Words: 13:blood gushed from his nose and ears. From the effects of that treatment
Words: 16:the poor lad was confined to his bed for five weeks, at the end of which
Words: 11:time he found that, to his personal sufferings, were superadded the
Words: 13:calamity of the loss of the best master he ever had in slavery.
Words: 0:
Words: 13:His next employment was that of waiter on board a steam-boat plying on
Words: 10:the Mississippi. Here his occupation again was pleasant, and his
Words: 12:treatment good; but the freedom of action enjoyed by the passengers in
Words: 9:travelling whithersoever they pleased, contrasted strongly in his mind
Words: 14:with his own deprivation of will as a slave. The natural result of this
Words: 11:comparison was an intense desire for freedom--a feeling which was never
Words: 10:afterwards eradicated from his breast. This love of liberty was,
Words: 10:however, so strongly counteracted by affection for his mother and
Words: 12:sisters, that although urgently entreated by one of the latter to take
Words: 11:advantage of his present favourable opportunity for escape, he would not
Words: 15:bring himself to do so at the expense of a separation for life from his
Words: 2:beloved relatives.
Words: 0:
Words: 13:His period of living on board the steamer having expired, he was again
Words: 12:remitted to field labour, under a burning sun. From that labour, from
Words: 13:which he suffered severely, he was soon removed to the lighter and more
Words: 11:agreeable occupation of house-waiter to his master. About this time Dr.
Words: 10:Young, in the conventional phraseology of the locality, "got religion."
Words: 13:The fruit of his alleged spiritual gain, was the loss of many material
Words: 11:comforts to the slaves. Destitute of the resources of education, they
Words: 12:were in the habit of employing their otherwise unoccupied minds on the
Words: 13:Sunday in fishing and other harmless pursuits; these were now all put an
Words: 14:end to. The Sabbath became a season of dread to William: he was required
Words: 14:to drive the family to and from the church, a distance of four miles
Words: 12:either way; and while they attended to the salvation of their souls
Words: 12:within the building, he was compelled to attend to the horses without
Words: 12:it, standing by them during divine service under a burning sun, or
Words: 12:drizzling rain. Although William did not get the religion of his master,
Words: 11:he acquired a family passion which appears to have been strongly
Words: 10:intermixed with the devotional exercises of the household of Dr.
Words: 12:Young--a love of sweet julep. In the evening, the slaves were required
Words: 11:to attend family worship. Before commencing the service, it was the
Words: 13:custom to hand a pitcher of the favourite beverage to every member of
Words: 13:the family, not excepting the nephew, a child of between four and five
Words: 12:years old. William was in the habit of watching his opportunity during
Words: 13:the prayer and helping himself from the pitcher, but one day letting it
Words: 11:fall, his propensity for this intoxicating drink was discovered, and he
Words: 6:was severely punished for its indulgence.
Words: 0:
Words: 14:In 1830, being then about sixteen years of age, William was hired to a
Words: 11:slave-dealer named Walker. This change of employment led the youth away
Words: 12:south and frustrated, for a time, his plans for escape. His experience
Words: 9:while in this capacity furnishes some interesting, though painful,
Words: 12:details of the legalized traffic in human beings carried on in the
Words: 12:United States. The desperation to which the slaves are driven at their
Words: 11:forced separation from husband, wife, children, and kindred, he found to
Words: 11:be a frequent cause of suicide. Slave-dealers he discovered were as
Words: 13:great adepts at deception in the sale of their commodity as the most
Words: 9:knowing down-easter, or tricky horse dealer. William's occupation on
Words: 13:board the steamer, as they steamed south, was to prepare the stock for
Words: 13:the market, by shaving off whiskers and blacking the grey hairs with a
Words: 2:colouring composition.
Words: 0:
Words: 12:At the expiration of the period of his hiring with Walker, William
Words: 11:returned to his master rejoiced to have escaped an employment so
Words: 14:repugnant to his feelings. But this joy was not of long duration. One of
Words: 13:his sisters who, although sold to another master had been living in the
Words: 14:same city with himself and mother, was again sold to be sent away south,
Words: 11:never in all probability to meet her sorrowing relatives. Dr. Young
Words: 13:also, wanting money, intimated to his young kinsman that he was about to
Words: 10:sell him. This intimation determined William, in conjunction with his
Words: 10:mother, to attempt their escape. For ten nights they travelled
Words: 13:northwards, hiding themselves in the woods by day. The mother and son at
Words: 10:length deemed themselves safe from re-capture, and, although weary and
Words: 12:foot-sore, were laying down sanguine plans for the acquisition of a farm
Words: 14:in Canada, the purchase of the freedom of the six other members of the
Words: 11:family still in slavery, and rejoicing in the anticipated happiness of
Words: 14:their free home in Canada. At that moment three men made up to and
Words: 12:seized them, bound the son and led him, with his desponding mother,
Words: 13:back to slavery. Elizabeth was sold and sent away south, while her son
Words: 11:became the property of a merchant tailor named Willi. Mr. Brown's
Words: 11:description of the final interview between himself and his mother, is
Words: 12:one of the most touching portions of his narrative. The mother, after
Words: 12:expressing her conviction of the speedy escape from slavery by the hand
Words: 13:of death, enjoined her child to persevere in his endeavours to gain his
Words: 12:freedom by flight. Her blessing was interrupted by the kick and curse
Words: 9:bestowed by her dehumanized master upon her beloved son.
Words: 0:
Words: 13:After having been hired for a short time to the captain of the
Words: 11:steam-boat _Otto_, William was finally sold to Captain Enoch Price for
Words: 11:650 dollars. That the quickness and intelligence of William rendered him
Words: 13:very valuable as a slave, is favoured by the evidence of Enoch Price
Words: 13:himself, who states that he was offered 2000 dollars for Sanford (as he
Words: 12:was called), in New Orleans. William was strongly urged by his new
Words: 14:mistress to marry. To facilitate this object, she even went so far as to
Words: 13:purchase a girl for whom she fancied he had an affection. He himself,
Words: 11:however, had secretly resolved never to enter into such a connexion
Words: 11:while in slavery, knowing that marriage, in the true and honourable
Words: 11:sense of the term, could not exist among slaves. Notwithstanding the
Words: 12:multitude of petty offences for which a slave is severely punished, it
Words: 10:is singular that one crime--bigamy--is visited upon a white with
Words: 14:severity, while no slave has ever yet been tried for it. In fact, the
Words: 13:man is allowed to form connections with as many women, and the women
Words: 7:with as many men, as they please.
Words: 0:
Words: 13:At St. Louis, William was employed as coachman to Mr. Price; but when
Words: 11:that gentleman subsequently took his family up the river to Cincinnati,
Words: 11:Sanford acted as appointed steward. While lying off this city, the
Words: 10:long-looked-for opportunity of escape presented itself; and on the 1st
Words: 11:of January, 1834--he being then almost twenty years of age--succeeded in
Words: 14:getting from the steamer to the wharf, and thence to the woods, where he
Words: 13:lay concealed until the shades of night had set in, when he again
Words: 12:commenced his journey northwards. While with Dr. Young, a nephew of that
Words: 11:gentleman, whose christian name was William, came into the family: the
Words: 11:slave was, therefore, denuded of the name of William, and thenceforth
Words: 11:called Sanford. This deprivation of his original name he had ever
Words: 12:regarded as an indignity, and having now gained his freedom he resumed
Words: 15:his original name; and as there was no one by whom he could be addressed
Words: 12:by it, he exultingly enjoyed the first-fruits of his freedom by calling
Words: 12:himself aloud by his old name "William!" After passing through a variety
Words: 12:of painful vicissitudes, on the eighth day he found himself destitute of
Words: 11:pecuniary means, and unable, from severe illness, to pursue his journey.
Words: 13:In that condition he was discovered by a venerable member of the Society
Words: 15:of Friends, who placed him in a covered waggon and took him to his own
Words: 12:house. There he remained about fifteen days, and by the kind treatment
Words: 12:of his host and hostess, who were what in America are called
Words: 11:"Thompsonians," he was restored to health, and supplied with the means
Words: 12:of pursuing his journey. The name of this, his first kind benefactor,
Words: 13:was "Wells Brown." As William had risen from the degradation of a slave
Words: 14:to the dignity of a man, it was expedient that he should follow the
Words: 12:customs of other men, and adopt a second name. His venerable friend,
Words: 12:therefore, bestowed upon him his own name, which, prefixed by his former
Words: 12:designation, made him "William Wells Brown," a name that will live in
Words: 13:history, while those of the men who claimed him as property would, were
Words: 14:it not for his deeds, have been unknown beyond the town in which they
Words: 13:lived. In nine days from the time he left Wells Brown's house, he
Words: 13:arrived at Cleveland, in the State of Ohio, where he found he could
Words: 10:remain comparatively safe from the pursuit of the man-stealer. Having
Words: 12:obtained employment as a waiter, he remained in that city until the
Words: 11:following spring, when he procured an engagement on board a steam-boat
Words: 12:plying on Lake Erie. In that situation he was enabled, during seven
Words: 12:months, to assist no less than sixty-nine slaves to escape to Canada.
Words: 14:While a slave he had regarded the whites as the natural enemies of his
Words: 12:race. It was, therefore, with no small pleasure that he discovered the
Words: 12:existence of the salt of America, in the despised Abolitionists of the
Words: 11:Northern States. He read with assiduity the writings of Benjamin Lundy,
Words: 11:William Lloyd Garrison, and others; and after his own twenty years'
Words: 11:experience of slavery, it is not surprising that he should have
Words: 8:enthusiastically embraced the principles of "total and immediate
Words: 6:emancipation," and "no union with slaveholders."
Words: 0:
Words: 10:In proportion as his mind expanded under the more favourable
Words: 12:circumstances in which he was placed, he became anxious, not merely for
Words: 12:the redemption of his race from personal slavery, but for the moral
Words: 12:elevation of those among them who were free. Finding that habits of
Words: 10:intoxication were too prevalent amongst his coloured brethren, he, in
Words: 9:conjunction with others, commenced a temperance reformation in their
Words: 14:body. Such was the success of their efforts that in three years, in the
Words: 13:city of Buffalo alone, a society of upwards of 500 members was raised
Words: 13:out of a coloured population of 700. Of that society Mr. Brown was
Words: 3:thrice elected President.
Words: 0:
Words: 10:The intellectual powers of our author, coupled with his intimate
Words: 11:acquaintance with the workings of the slave system, recommended him to
Words: 11:the Abolitionists as a man eminently qualified to arouse the attention
Words: 13:of the people of the Northern States to the great national sin of
Words: 13:America. In 1843 he was engaged as a lecturer by the Western New-York
Words: 11:Anti-Slavery Society. From 1844 to 1847 he laboured in the anti-slavery
Words: 10:cause in connection with the American Anti-Slavery Society, and from
Words: 15:that period up to the time of his departure for Europe, in 1849, he was
Words: 11:an agent of the Massachusetts Anti-Slavery Society. The records of those
Words: 11:societies furnish abundant evidence of the success of his labours. From
Words: 9:the Massachusetts Anti-Slavery Society he early received the following
Words: 1:testimony:--
Words: 0:
Words: 10:"Since Mr. Brown became an agent of the Massachusetts Anti-Slavery
Words: 12:Society, he has lectured in very many of the towns of this
Words: 10:Commonwealth, and won for himself general respect and approbation. He
Words: 9:combines true self-respect with true humility, and rare judiciousness
Words: 10:with great moral courage. Himself a fugitive slave, he can
Words: 11:experimentally describe the situation of those in bonds as bound with
Words: 11:them; and he powerfully illustrates the diabolism of that system which
Words: 13:keeps in chains and darkness a host of minds, which, if free and
Words: 10:enlightened, would shine among men like stars in a firmament."
Words: 0:
Words: 12:Another member of that Society speaks thus of him:--"I need not attempt
Words: 10:any description of the ability and efficiency which characterized his
Words: 13:speaking throughout the meetings. To you who know him so well, it is
Words: 14:enough to say that his lectures were worthy of himself. He has left an
Words: 13:impression on the minds of the people, that few could have done. Cold,
Words: 14:indeed, must be the heart that could resist the appeals of so noble a
Words: 11:specimen of humanity, in behalf of a crushed and despised race."
Words: 0:
Words: 12:Notwithstanding the celebrity Mr. Brown had acquired in the north, as a
Words: 13:man of genius and talent, and the general respect his high character had
Words: 13:gained him, the slave spirit of America denied him the rights of a
Words: 12:citizen. By the constitution of the United States, he was every moment
Words: 16:liable to be seized and sent back to slavery. He was in daily peril of a
Words: 10:gradual legalized murder, under a system one of whose established
Words: 13:economical principles is, that it is more profitable to work up a slave
Words: 13:on a plantation in a short time, by excessive labour and cheap food,
Words: 11:than to obtain a lengthened remuneration by moderate work and humane
Words: 13:treatment. His only protection from such a fate was the anomaly of the
Words: 12:ascendancy of the public opinion over the law of the country. So
Words: 11:uncertain, however, was that tenure of liberty, that even before the
Words: 13:passage of the Fugitive Slave Law, it was deemed expedient to secure the
Words: 11:services of Frederick Douglass to the anti-slavery cause by the purchase
Words: 13:of his freedom. The same course might have been taken to secure the
Words: 11:labours of Mr. Brown, had he not entertained an unconquerable repugnance
Words: 14:to its adoption. On the 10th of January, 1848, Enoch Price wrote to Mr.
Words: 13:Edmund Quincy offering to sell Mr. Brown to himself or friends for 325
Words: 10:dollars. To this communication the fugitive returned the following pithy
Words: 3:and noble reply:--
Words: 0:
Words: 14:"I cannot accept of Mr. Price's offer to become a purchaser of my body
Words: 16:and soul. God made me as free as he did Enoch Price, and Mr. Price shall
Words: 12:never receive a dollar from me or my friends with my consent."
Words: 0:
Words: 11:There were, however, other reasons besides his personal safety which led
Words: 13:to Mr. Brown's visit to Europe. It was thought desirable always to have
Words: 15:in England some talented man of colour who should be a living lie to the
Words: 12:doctrine of the inferiority of the African race: and it was moreover
Words: 12:felt that none could so powerfully advocate the cause of "those in
Words: 13:bonds" as one who had actually been "bound with them." This had been
Words: 10:proved in the extraordinary effect produced in Great Britain by
Words: 10:Frederick Douglass in 1845 and 1846. The American Committee in
Words: 11:connection with the Peace Congress were also desirous of sending to
Words: 10:Europe coloured representatives of their Society, and Mr. Brown was
Words: 12:selected for that purpose, and duly accredited by them to the Paris
Words: 1:Congress.
Words: 0:
Words: 14:On the 18th of July, 1849, a large meeting of the coloured citizens of
Words: 13:Boston was held in Washington Hall to bid him farewell. At that meeting
Words: 6:the following resolutions were unanimously adopted:--
Words: 0:
Words: 11:"_Resolved_,--That we bid our brother, William Wells Brown, God speed in
Words: 11:      his mission to Europe, and commend him to the hospitality and
Words: 7:      encouragement of all true friends of humanity.
Words: 0:
Words: 10:"_Resolved_,--That we forward by him our renewed protest against the
Words: 10:      American Colonization Society; and invoke for him a candid hearing
Words: 12:      before the British public, in reply to the efforts put forth there
Words: 12:      by the Rev. Mr. Miller, or any other agent of said Society."
Words: 0:
Words: 12:Two days afterwards he sailed for Europe, encountering on his voyage his
Words: 7:last experience of American prejudice against colour.
Words: 0:
Words: 14:On the 28th of August he landed at Liverpool, a time and place memorable
Words: 15:in his life as the first upon which he could truly call himself a free
Words: 12:man upon God's earth. In the history of nations, as of individuals,
Words: 10:there is often singular retributive mercy as well as retributive
Words: 10:justice. In the seventeenth century the victims of monarchical tyranny
Words: 12:in Great Britain found social and political freedom when they set foot
Words: 12:upon Plymouth Rock in New England: in the nineteenth century the victims
Words: 11:of the oppressions of the American Republic find freedom and social
Words: 9:equality upon the shores of monarchical England. Liverpool, which
Words: 13:seventy years back was so steeped in the guilt of negro slavery that
Words: 14:Paine expressed his surprise that God did not sweep it from the face of
Words: 14:the earth, is now to the hunted negro the Plymouth Rock of Old England.
Words: 12:From Liverpool he proceeded to Dublin where he was warmly received by
Words: 12:Mr. Haughton, Mr. Webb, and other friends of the slave, and publicly
Words: 12:welcomed at a large meeting presided over by the first named gentleman.
Words: 0:
Words: 13:The reception of Mr. Brown at the Peace Congress in Paris was most
Words: 12:flattering. In a company, comprising a large portion of the _elite_ of
Words: 11:Europe, he admirably maintained his reputation as a public speaker. His
Words: 12:brief address, upon that "war spirit of America which holds in bondage
Words: 11:three million of his brethren," produced a profound sensation. At its
Words: 11:conclusion the speaker was warmly greeted by Victor Hugo, the Abbe
Words: 10:Duguerry, Emile de Girardin, the Pastor Coquerel, Richard Cobden, and
Words: 14:every man of note in the Assembly. At the soiree given by M. De
Words: 11:Tocqueville, the Minister for Foreign Affairs, and the other fetes given
Words: 12:to the Members of the Congress, Mr. Brown was received with marked
Words: 1:attention.
Words: 0:
Words: 10:Having finished his Peace mission in France, he commenced an
Words: 10:Anti-slavery tour in England and Scotland. With that independence of
Words: 13:feeling which those who are acquainted with him know to be his chief
Words: 9:characteristic, he rejected the idea of anything like eleemosynary
Words: 11:support. He determined to maintain himself and family by his own
Words: 10:exertions--by his literary labours, and the honourable profession of a
Words: 11:public lecturer. His first metropolitan reception in England was at a
Words: 10:large, influential, and enthusiastic meeting in the Music Hall, Stone
Words: 9:Street. The members of the Whittington Club--an institution numbering
Words: 10:nearly 2000 members, among whom are Lords Brougham, Dudley Coutts
Words: 8:Stuart, and Beaumont; Charles Dickens, Douglass Jerrold, Martin
Words: 9:Thackeray, Charles Lushington, M.P., Monckton Milnes, M.P., and several
Words: 11:other of the most distinguished legislators and literary men and women
Words: 13:in this country--elected Mr. Brown an honorary member of the Club, as a
Words: 13:mark of respect to his character; and, as the following extract from the
Words: 10:Secretary, Mr. Stundwicke, will show, as a protest against the
Words: 11:distinctions made between man and man on account of colour in
Words: 12:America:--"I have much pleasure in conveying to you the best thanks of
Words: 11:the managing committee of this institution for the excellent lecture you
Words: 13:gave here last evening on the subject of 'Slavery in America,' and also
Words: 12:in presenting you in their names with an honorary membership of the
Words: 13:Club. It is hoped that you will often avail yourself of its privileges
Words: 14:by coming amongst us. You will then see, by the cordial welcome of the
Words: 10:members, that they protest against the odious distinctions made between
Words: 13:man and man, and the abominable traffic of which you have been the
Words: 1:victim."
Words: 0:
Words: 13:For the last three years Mr. Brown has been engaged in visiting and
Words: 13:holding meetings in nearly all the large towns in the kingdom upon the
Words: 10:question of American Slavery, Temperance, and other subjects. Perhaps no
Words: 8:coloured individual, not excepting that extraordinary man, Frederick
Words: 10:Douglass, has done more good in disseminating anti-slavery principles in
Words: 4:England, Scotland, and Ireland.
Words: 0:
Words: 12:In the spring of 1851, two most interesting fugitives, William and Ellen
Words: 12:Craft, arrived in England. They had made their escape from the South,
Words: 13:the wife disguised in male attire, and the husband in the capacity of
Words: 13:her slave. William Craft was doing a thriving business in Boston, but in
Words: 14:1851 was driven with his wife from that city by the operation of the
Words: 11:Fugitive Slave Law. For several months they travelled in company with
Words: 12:Mr. Brown in this country, deepening the disgust created by Mr. Brown's
Words: 10:eloquent denunciation of slavery by their simple but touching narrative.
Words: 12:At length they were enabled to gratify their thirst for education by
Words: 12:gaining admission to Lady Byron's school at Oakham, Surrey. In the month
Words: 15:of May, Mr. Brown and Mr. and Mrs. Craft were taken by a party of
Words: 10:anti-slavery friends to the Great Exhibition. The honourable manner in
Words: 11:which they were received by distinguished persons to whom their history
Words: 11:was known, and the freedom with which they perambulated the American
Words: 11:department, was a salutary rebuke to the numerous Americans present, in
Words: 11:regard to the great sin of their country--slavery; and its great
Words: 9:folly--prejudice of colour. A curious circumstance occurred during the
Words: 12:Exhibition. Among the hosts of American visitors to this country was Mr.
Words: 11:Brown's late master, Enoch Price, who made diligent inquiry after his
Words: 11:lost piece of property--not, of course, with any view to its
Words: 10:reclamation--but, to the mutual regret of both parties, without success.
Words: 13:It is gratifying to state that the master spoke highly of, and expressed
Words: 13:a wish for the future prosperity of, his fugitive slave; a fact which
Words: 14:tends to prove that prejudice of colour is to a very great extent a
Words: 11:thing of locality and association. Had Mr. Price, however, left behind
Words: 13:him letters of manumission for Mr. Brown, enabling him, if he chose, to
Words: 14:return to his native land, he would have given a more practical proof of
Words: 13:respect, and of the sincerity of his desire for the welfare of Mr.
Words: 1:Brown.
Words: 0:
Words: 11:It would extend these pages far beyond their proposed length were
Words: 11:anything like a detailed account of Mr. Brown's anti-slavery labours in
Words: 12:this country to be attempted. Suffice it to say that they have
Words: 10:everywhere been attended with benefit and approbation. At Bolton an
Words: 12:admirable address from the ladies was presented to him, and at other
Words: 7:places he has received most honourable testimonials.
Words: 0:
Words: 12:Since Mr. Brown left America, the condition of the fugitive slaves in
Words: 12:his own country has, through the operation of the Fugitive Slave Law,
Words: 11:been rendered so perilous as to preclude the possibility of return
Words: 10:without the almost certain loss of liberty. His expatriation has,
Words: 14:however, been a gain to the cause of humanity in this country, where an
Words: 8:intelligent representative of the oppressed coloured Americans is
Words: 10:constantly needed, not only to describe, in language of fervid
Words: 12:eloquence, the wrongs inflicted upon his race in the United States, but
Words: 11:to prevent their bonds being strengthened in this country by holding
Words: 8:fellowship with slave-holding and slave-abetting ministers from America.
Words: 12:In his lectures he has clearly demonstrated the fact, that the sole
Words: 12:support of the slavery of the United States is its churches. This
Words: 11:knowledge of the standing of American ministers in reference to slavery
Words: 14:has, in the case of Dr. Dyer, and in many other instances, been most
Words: 8:serviceable, preventing their reception into communion with British
Words: 12:churches. Last year Mr. Brown succeeded in getting over to this country
Words: 11:his daughters, two interesting girls twelve and sixteen years of age
Words: 11:respectively, who are now receiving an education which will qualify them
Words: 10:hereafter to become teachers in their turn--a description of education
Words: 13:which would have been denied them in their native land. In 1834 Mr.
Words: 13:Brown married a free coloured woman, who died in January of the present
Words: 1:year.
Words: 0:
Words: 12:The condition of escaped slaves has engaged much of his attention while
Words: 11:in this country. He found that in England no anti-slavery organization
Words: 11:existed whose object was to aid fugitive slaves in obtaining an
Words: 12:honourable subsistence in the land of their exile. In most cases they
Words: 11:are thrown upon the support of a few warm-hearted anti-slavery advocates
Words: 10:in this country, pre-eminent among whom stands Mr. Brown's earliest
Words: 12:friend, Mr. George Thompson, M.P., whose house is rarely free from one
Words: 12:or more of those who have acquired the designation of his "American
Words: 10:constituents." This want has recently been attempted to be supplied,
Words: 11:partly through Mr. Brown's exertions, and partly by the establishment of
Words: 4:the Anglo-American Anti-Slavery Association.
Words: 0:
Words: 14:On the 1st of August, 1851, a meeting of the most novel character was
Words: 13:held at the Hall of Commerce, London, being a soiree given by fugitive
Words: 13:slaves in this country to Mr. George Thompson, on his return from his
Words: 12:American mission on behalf of their race. That meeting was most ably
Words: 13:presided over by Mr. Brown, and the speeches made upon the occasion by
Words: 10:fugitive slaves were of the most interesting and creditable description.
Words: 11:Although a residence in Canada is infinitely preferable to slavery in
Words: 11:America, yet the climate of that country is uncongenial to the
Words: 12:constitutions of the fugitive slaves, and their lack of education is an
Words: 11:almost insuperable barrier to their social progress. The latter evil Mr.
Words: 12:Brown attempted to remedy by the establishment of a Manual Labour School
Words: 2:in Canada.
Words: 0:
Words: 11:A public meeting, attended by between 3000 and 4000 persons, was
Words: 14:convened by Mr. Brown, on the 6th of January, 1851, in the City Hall,
Words: 12:Glasgow, presided over by Mr. Hastie, one of the representatives of that
Words: 11:city, at which meeting a resolution was unanimously passed approving of
Words: 11:Mr. Brown's scheme, which scheme, however, never received that amount of
Words: 13:support which would have enabled him to bring it into practice; and the
Words: 12:plan at present only remains as an evidence of its author's ingenuity
Words: 11:and desire for the elevation of his depressed race. Mr. Brown
Words: 10:subsequently made, through the columns of the _Times_ newspaper, a
Words: 10:proposition for the emigration of American fugitive slaves, under fair
Words: 14:and honourable terms, to the West Indies, where there is a great lack of
Words: 11:that tillage labour which they are so capable of undertaking. This
Words: 11:proposition has hitherto met with no better fate than its predecessor.
Words: 0:
Words: 11:Mr. Brown's literary abilities may be partly judged of from the
Words: 11:following pages. The amount of knowledge and education he has acquired
Words: 11:under circumstances of no ordinary difficulty, is a striking proof of
Words: 13:what can be done by combined genius and industry. His proficiency as a
Words: 12:linguist, without the aid of a master, is considerable. His present work
Words: 12:is a valuable addition to the stock of English literature. The honour
Words: 13:which has hitherto been paid, and which, so long as he resides upon
Words: 13:British soil, will no doubt continue to be paid to his character and
Words: 11:talents, must have its influence in abating the senseless prejudice of
Words: 12:colour in America, and hastening the time when the object of his
Words: 12:mission, the abolition of the slavery of his native country, shall be
Words: 9:accomplished, and that young Republic renouncing with penitence its
Words: 11:national sin, shall take its proper place amongst the most free,
Words: 7:civilized, and Christian nations of the earth.

Words: 1:PREFACE.
Words: 0:
Words: 0:
Words: 13:While I feel conscious that most of the contents of these Letters will
Words: 12:be interesting chiefly to American readers, yet I may indulge the hope,
Words: 13:that the fact of their being the first production of a Fugitive Slave,
Words: 13:as a history of travels, may carry with them novelty enough to secure
Words: 13:for them, to some extent, the attention of the reading public of Great
Words: 13:Britain. Most of the letters were written for the private perusal of a
Words: 10:few personal friends in America; some were contributed to "Frederick
Words: 11:Douglass's paper," a journal published in the United States. In a
Words: 12:printed circular sent some weeks since to some of my friends, asking
Words: 11:subscriptions to this volume, I stated the reasons for its publication:
Words: 13:these need not be repeated here. To those who so promptly and kindly
Words: 14:responded to that appeal, I tender my most sincere thanks. It is with no
Words: 13:little diffidence that I lay these letters before the public; for I am
Words: 14:not blind to the fact, that they must contain many errors; and to those
Words: 15:who shall find fault with them on that account, it may not be too much
Words: 15:for me to ask them kindly to remember, that the author was a slave in
Words: 14:one of the Southern States of America, until he had attained the age of
Words: 13:twenty years; and that the education he has acquired, was by his own
Words: 11:exertions, he never having had a day's schooling in his life.
Words: 0:
Words: 3:                              W. WELLS BROWN.
Words: 0:

Words: 1:     LONDON.

Words: 2:LETTER I.
Words: 0:
Words: 5:_Departure from Boston--the Passengers--Halifax--the Passage--First
Words: 3:Sight of Land--Liverpool._
Words: 0:
Words: 0:
Words: 3:                    LIVERPOOL, _July 28_.
Words: 0:
Words: 12:On the 18th July, 1849, I took passage in the steam-ship _Canada_,
Words: 13:Captain Judkins, bound for Liverpool. The day was a warm one; so much
Words: 14:so, that many persons on board, as well as several on shore, stood with
Words: 14:their umbrellas up, so intense was the heat of the sun. The ringing of
Words: 14:the ship's bell was a signal for us to shake hands with our friends,
Words: 14:which we did, and then stepped on the deck of the noble craft. The
Words: 12:_Canada_ quitted her moorings at half-past twelve, and we were soon in
Words: 15:motion. As we were passing out of Boston Bay, I took my stand on the
Words: 13:quarter-deck, to take a last farewell (at least for a time), of my
Words: 16:native land. A visit to the old world, up to that time had seemed but a
Words: 12:dream. As I looked back upon the receding land, recollections of the
Words: 12:past rushed through my mind in quick succession. From the treatment that
Words: 13:I had received from the Americans as a victim of slavery, and the
Words: 14:knowledge that I was at that time liable to be seized and again reduced
Words: 13:to whips and chains, I had supposed that I would leave the country
Words: 15:without any regret; but in this I was mistaken, for when I saw the last
Words: 13:thread of communication cut off between me and the land, and the dim
Words: 14:shores dying away in the distance, I almost regretted that I was not on
Words: 1:shore.
Words: 0:
Words: 11:An anticipated trip to a foreign country appears pleasant when talking
Words: 12:about it, especially when surrounded by friends whom we love; but when
Words: 14:we have left them all behind, it does not seem so pleasant. Whatever may
Words: 14:be the fault of the government under which we live, and no matter how
Words: 13:oppressive her laws may appear, yet we leave our native land (if such
Words: 12:it be) with feelings akin to sorrow. With the steamer's powerful engine
Words: 15:at work, and with a fair wind, we were speedily on the bosom of the
Words: 14:Atlantic, which was as calm and as smooth as our own Hudson in its
Words: 12:calmest aspect. We had on board above one hundred passengers, forty of
Words: 10:whom were the "Viennese children"--a troop of dancers. The passengers
Words: 7:represented several different nations, English, French, Spaniards,
Words: 12:Africans, and Americans. One man who had the longest pair of mustaches
Words: 11:that mortal man was ever doomed to wear, especially attracted my
Words: 12:attention. He appeared to belong to no country in particular, but was
Words: 13:yet the busiest man on board. After viewing for some time the many
Words: 13:strange faces around me, I descended to the cabin to look after my
Words: 14:luggage, which had been put hurriedly on board. I hope that all who take
Words: 16:a trip of so great a distance may be as fortunate as I was, in being
Words: 13:supplied with books to read on the voyage. My friends had furnished me
Words: 11:with literature, from "Macaulay's History of England" to "Jane Eyre," so
Words: 11:that I did not want for books to occupy my time.
Words: 0:
Words: 13:A pleasant passage of about thirty hours, brought us to Halifax, at six
Words: 13:o'clock in the evening. In company with my friend the President of the
Words: 12:Oberlin Institute, I took a stroll through the town; and from what
Words: 16:little I saw of the people in the streets, I am sure that the taking of
Words: 13:the Temperance pledge would do them no injury. Our stay at Halifax was
Words: 14:short. Having taken in a few sacks of coals, the mails, and a limited
Words: 13:number of passengers, we were again out, and soon at sea. After a
Words: 16:pleasant run of seven days more, and as I was lying in my bed, I heard
Words: 11:the cry of "Land a-head." Although our passage had been unprecedentedly
Words: 15:short, yet I need not inform you that this news was hailed with joy by
Words: 16:all on board. For my own part, I was soon on deck. Away in the distance,
Words: 13:and on our larboard quarter, were the grey hills of Ireland. Yes! we
Words: 14:were in sight of the land of Emmett and O'Connell. While I rejoiced with
Words: 14:the other passengers at the sight of land, and the near approach to the
Words: 14:end of the voyage, I felt low spirited, because it reminded me of the
Words: 12:great distance I was from home. But the experience of above twenty
Words: 12:years' travelling, had prepared me to undergo what most persons must lay
Words: 13:their account with, in visiting a strange country. This was the last day
Words: 17:but one that we were to be on board; and as if moved by the sight of
Words: 9:land, all seemed to be gathering their different things
Words: 11:together--brushing up their old clothes and putting on their new ones,
Words: 14:as if this would bring them any sooner to the end of their journey.
Words: 0:
Words: 13:The last night on board was the most pleasant, apparently, that we had
Words: 12:experienced; probably, because it was the last. The moon was in her
Words: 11:meridian splendour, pouring her broad light over the calm sea; while
Words: 14:near to us, on our starboard side, was a ship with her snow-white sails
Words: 13:spread aloft, and stealing through the water like a thing of life. What
Words: 13:can present a more picturesque view, than two vessels at sea on a
Words: 13:moonlight night, and within a few rods of each other? With a gentle
Words: 14:breeze, and the powerful engine at work, we seemed to be flying to the
Words: 5:embrace of our British neighbours.
Words: 0:
Words: 15:The next morning I was up before the sun, and found that we were within
Words: 14:a few miles of Liverpool. The taking of a pilot on board at eleven
Words: 13:o'clock, warned us to prepare to quit our ocean palace and seek other
Words: 13:quarters. At a little past three o'clock, the ship cast anchor, and we
Words: 14:were all tumbled, bag and baggage, into a small steamer, and in a few
Words: 13:moments were at the door of the Custom-House. The passage had only been
Words: 13:nine days and twenty-two hours, the quickest on record at that time, yet
Words: 13:it was long enough. I waited nearly three hours before my name was
Words: 14:called, and when it was, I unlocked my trunks and handed them over to
Words: 13:one of the officers, whose dirty hands made no improvement on the work
Words: 12:of the laundress. First one article was taken out, and then another,
Words: 15:till an _Iron Collar_ that had been worn by a female slave on the banks
Words: 11:of the Mississippi, was hauled out, and this democratic instrument of
Words: 12:torture became the centre of attraction; so much so, that instead of
Words: 13:going on with the examination, all hands stopped to look at the "Negro
Words: 1:Collar."
Words: 0:
Words: 12:Several of my countrymen who were standing by, were not a little
Words: 12:displeased at answers which I gave to questions on the subject of
Words: 11:Slavery; but they held their peace. The interest created by the
Words: 12:appearance of the Iron Collar, closed the examination of my luggage. As
Words: 12:if afraid that they would find something more hideous, they put the
Words: 13:Custom-House mark on each piece, and passed them out, and I was soon
Words: 8:comfortably installed at Brown's Temperance Hotel, Clayton Square.
Words: 0:
Words: 0:
Words: 12:No person of my complexion can visit this country without being struck
Words: 11:with the marked difference between the English and the Americans. The
Words: 12:prejudice which I have experienced on all and every occasion in the
Words: 12:United States, and to some extent on board the _Canada_, vanished as
Words: 16:soon as I set foot on the soil of Britain. In America I had been bought
Words: 13:and sold as a slave, in the Southern States. In the so-called free
Words: 12:States, I had been treated as one born to occupy an inferior
Words: 11:position,--in steamers, compelled to take my fare on the deck; in
Words: 14:hotels, to take my meals in the kitchen; in coaches, to ride on the
Words: 13:outside; in railways, to ride in the "negro car;" and in churches, to
Words: 16:sit in the "negro pew." But no sooner was I on British soil, than I was
Words: 13:recognised as a man, and an equal. The very dogs in the streets
Words: 12:appeared conscious of my manhood. Such is the difference, and such is
Words: 15:the change that is brought about by a trip of nine days in an Atlantic
Words: 1:steamer.
Words: 0:
Words: 14:I was not more struck with the treatment of the people, than with the
Words: 13:appearance of the great seaport of the world. The grey appearance of the
Words: 12:stone piers and docks, the dark look of the magnificent warehouses, the
Words: 10:substantial appearance of every thing around, causes one to think
Words: 13:himself in a new world instead of the old. Every thing in Liverpool
Words: 13:looks old, yet nothing is worn out. The beautiful villas on the opposite
Words: 12:side of the river, in the vicinity of Birkenhead, together with the
Words: 14:countless number of vessels in the river, and the great ships to be seen
Words: 11:in the stream, give life and animation to the whole scene.
Words: 0:
Words: 14:Every thing in and about Liverpool seems to be built for the future as
Words: 14:well as the present. We had time to examine but few of the public
Words: 11:buildings, the first of which was the Custom-House, an edifice that
Words: 10:would be an ornament to any city in the world.
Words: 0:
Words: 16:For the first time in my life, I can say "I am truly free." My old
Words: 12:master may make his appearance here, with the Constitution of the United
Words: 14:States in his pocket, the Fugitive Slave Law in one hand and the chains
Words: 14:in the other, and claim me as his property, but all will avail him
Words: 15:nothing. I can here stand and look the tyrant in the face, and tell him
Words: 15:that I am his equal! England is, indeed, the "land of the free, and the
Words: 4:home of the brave."
Words: 0:

Words: 2:LETTER II.
Words: 0:
Words: 7:_Trip to Ireland--Dublin--Her Majesty's Visit--Illumination of the
Words: 6:City--the Birth-Place of Thomas Moore--a Reception._
Words: 0:
Words: 0:
Words: 3:                    DUBLIN, _August 6_.
Words: 0:
Words: 12:After remaining in Liverpool two days, I took passage in the little
Words: 13:steamer _Adelaide_ for this city. The wind being high on the night of
Words: 14:our voyage, the vessel had scarcely got to sea ere we were driven to
Words: 12:our berths; and though the distance from Liverpool to Dublin is short,
Words: 14:yet, strange to say, I witnessed more effects of the sea and rolling of
Words: 14:the steamer upon the passengers, than was to be seen during the whole of
Words: 11:our voyage from America. We reached Kingstown, five miles below Dublin,
Words: 14:after a passage of nearly fifteen hours, and were soon seated on a car,
Words: 16:and on our way to the city. While coming into the bay, one gets a fine
Words: 13:view of Dublin and the surrounding country. Few sheets of water make a
Words: 12:more beautiful appearance than Dublin Bay. We found it as still and
Words: 13:smooth as a mirror, with a soft mist on its surface--a strange contrast
Words: 11:to the boisterous sea that we had left a moment before.
Words: 0:
Words: 12:The curious phrases of the Irish sounded harshly upon my ear, probably,
Words: 15:because they were strange to me. I lost no time on reaching the city in
Words: 14:seeking out some to whom I had letters of introduction, one of whom gave
Words: 14:me an invitation to make his house my home during my stay, an invitation
Words: 8:which I did not think fit to decline.
Words: 0:
Words: 12:Dublin, the Metropolis of Ireland, is a city of above two hundred
Words: 12:thousand inhabitants, and is considered by the people of Ireland to be
Words: 12:the second city in the British Empire. The Liffey, which falls into
Words: 12:Dublin Bay a little below the Custom-House, divides the town into two
Words: 10:nearly equal parts. The streets are--some of them--very fine, especially
Words: 12:upper Sackville Street, in the centre of which stands a pillar erected
Words: 10:to Nelson, England's most distinguished Naval Commander. The Bank of
Words: 13:Ireland, to which I paid a visit, is a splendid building, and was
Words: 9:formerly the Parliament House. This magnificent edifice fronts College
Words: 14:Green, and near at hand stands a bronze statue of William III. The Bank
Words: 12:and the Custom-House are two of the finest monuments of architecture in
Words: 13:the city; the latter of which stands near the river Liffey, and its
Words: 10:front makes an imposing appearance, extending to three hundred and
Words: 13:seventy-five feet. It is built of Portland stone, and is adorned with a
Words: 10:beautiful portico in the centre, consisting of four Doric columns
Words: 11:supporting an enriched entablature, decorated with a group of figures in
Words: 8:alto-relievo, representing Hibernia and Britannia presenting emblems of
Words: 11:peace and liberty. A magnificent dome, supporting a cupola, on whose
Words: 13:apex stands a colossal figure of Hope, rises nobly from the centre of
Words: 13:the building to a height of one hundred and twenty-five feet. It is,
Words: 9:withal, a fine specimen of what man can do.
Words: 0:
Words: 14:From this noble edifice, we bent our steps to another part of the city,
Words: 13:and soon found ourselves in the vicinity of St. Patrick's, where we had
Words: 12:a heart-sickening view of the poorest of the poor. All the recollections
Words: 12:of poverty which I had ever beheld, seemed to disappear in comparison
Words: 14:with what was then before me. We passed a filthy and noisy market, where
Words: 12:fruit and vegetable women were screaming and begging those passing by to
Words: 10:purchase their commodities; while in and about the market-place were
Words: 11:throngs of beggars fighting for rotten fruit, cabbage stocks, and even
Words: 12:the very trimmings of vegetables. On the side walks, were great numbers
Words: 11:hovering about the doors of the more wealthy, and following strangers,
Words: 10:importuning them for "pence to buy bread." Sickly and emaciated-looking
Words: 12:creatures, half naked, were at our heels at every turn. After passing
Words: 13:through a half dozen, or more, of narrow and dirty streets, we returned
Words: 14:to our lodgings, impressed with the idea that we had seen enough of the
Words: 4:poor for one day.
Words: 0:
Words: 12:In our return home, we passed through a respectable looking street, in
Words: 12:which stands a small three storey brick building, which was pointed out
Words: 13:to us as the birth-place of Thomas Moore, the poet. The following verse
Words: 13:from one of Moore's poems was continually in my mind while viewing this
Words: 1:house:--
Words: 0:
Words: 6:    "Where is the slave, so lowly,
Words: 4:    Condemn'd to chains unholy,
Words: 4:    Who, could he burst
Words: 4:    His bonds at first,
Words: 5:    Would pine beneath them slowly?"

Words: 13:Yesterday was the Sabbath, but it had more the appearance of a holiday
Words: 15:than a day of rest. It had been announced the day before, that the Royal
Words: 13:fleet was expected, and at an early hour on Sunday, the entire town
Words: 14:seemed to be on the move towards Kingstown, and as the family with whom
Words: 12:I was staying followed the multitude, I was not inclined to remain
Words: 13:behind, and so went with them. On reaching the station we found it
Words: 13:utterly impossible to get standing room in any of the trains, much less
Words: 13:a seat, and therefore determined to reach Kingstown under the plea of a
Words: 14:morning's walk; and in this we were not alone, for during the walk of
Words: 12:five miles the road was filled with thousands of pedestrians and a
Words: 11:countless number of carriages, phaetons, and vehicles of a more humble
Words: 1:order.
Words: 0:
Words: 14:We reached the lower town in time to get a good dinner, and rest
Words: 11:ourselves before going to make further searches for Her Majesty's fleet.
Words: 12:At a little past four o'clock, we observed the multitude going towards
Words: 14:the pier, a number of whom were yelling at the top of their voices,
Words: 14:"It's coming, it's coming;" but on going to the quay, we found that a
Words: 14:false alarm had been given. However, we had been on the look-out but a
Words: 15:short time, when a column of smoke rising as it were out of the sea,
Words: 13:announced that the Royal fleet was near at hand. The concourse in the
Words: 12:vicinity of the pier was variously estimated at from eighty to one
Words: 2:hundred thousand.
Words: 0:
Words: 13:It was not long before the five steamers were entering the harbour, the
Words: 14:one bearing Her Majesty leading the way. As each vessel had a number of
Words: 13:distinguished persons on board, the people appeared to be at a loss to
Words: 14:know which was the Queen; and as each party made its appearance on the
Words: 10:promenade deck, they were received with great enthusiasm, the party
Words: 11:having the best looking lady being received with the greatest applause.
Words: 12:The Prince of Wales, and Prince Alfred, while crossing the deck were
Words: 12:recognised and greeted with three cheers; the former taking off his hat
Words: 14:and bowing to the people, showed that he had had some training as a
Words: 14:public man although not ten years of age. But not so with Prince Alfred;
Words: 15:for, when his brother turned to him and asked him to take off his hat
Words: 16:and make a bow to the people, he shook his head and said, "No." This was
Words: 13:received with hearty laughter by those on board, and was responded to by
Words: 13:the thousands on shore. But greater applause was yet in store for the
Words: 13:young prince; for the captain of the steamer being near by, and seeing
Words: 14:that the Prince of Wales could not prevail on his brother to take off
Words: 15:his hat, stepped up to him and undertook to take it off for him, when,
Words: 14:seemingly to the delight of all, the prince put both hands to his head
Words: 15:and held his hat fast. This was regarded as a sign of courage and future
Words: 10:renown, and was received with the greatest enthusiasm--many crying out,
Words: 12:"Good, good: he will make a brave king when his day comes."
Words: 0:
Words: 12:After the greetings and applause had been wasted on many who had
Words: 15:appeared on deck, all at once, as if by some magic power, we beheld a
Words: 13:lady rather small in stature, with auburn or reddish hair, attired in a
Words: 11:plain dress, and wearing a sky-blue bonnet, standing on the larboard
Words: 12:paddle-box, by the side of a tall good-looking man, with mustaches. The

Words: 14:a few moments were allowed to the people to look at the Queen, before
Words: 13:she again disappeared; and it was understood that she would not be seen
Words: 14:again that evening. A rush was then made for the railway, to return to
Words: 1:Dublin.

Words: 2:                    _August 8_.
Words: 0:
Words: 14:Yesterday was a great day in Dublin. At an early hour the bells began
Words: 13:their merry peals, and the people were soon seen in groups in the
Words: 13:streets and public squares. The hour of ten was fixed for the procession
Words: 14:to leave Kingstown, and it was expected to enter the city at eleven. The
Words: 13:windows of the houses in the streets through which the Royal train was
Words: 12:to pass, were at a premium, and seemed to find ready occupants.
Words: 0:
Words: 13:Being invited the day previous to occupy part of a window in Upper
Words: 12:Sackville Street, I was stationed at my allotted place, at an early
Words: 12:hour, with an out-stretched neck and open eyes. My own colour differing
Words: 13:from those about me, I attracted not a little attention from many; and
Words: 14:often, when gazing down the street to see if the Royal procession was in
Words: 13:sight, would find myself eyed by all around. But neither while at the
Words: 14:window, or in the streets, was I once insulted. This was so unlike the
Words: 12:American prejudice, that it seemed strange to me. It was near twelve
Words: 11:o'clock before the procession entered Sackville Street, and when it did
Words: 12:all eyes seemed to beam with delight. The first carriage contained only
Words: 12:Her Majesty and the Prince Consort; the second, the Royal children; and
Words: 12:the third, the Lords in Waiting. Fifteen carriages were used by those
Words: 17:that made up the Royal party. I had a full view of the Queen and all who
Words: 11:followed in the train. Her Majesty--whether from actual love for her
Words: 12:person, or the novelty of the occasion, I know not which--was received
Words: 10:everywhere with the greatest enthusiasm. One thing, however, is certain,
Words: 9:and that is--Queen Victoria is beloved by her subjects.
Words: 0:
Words: 11:But the grand _fete_ was reserved for the evening. Great preparations
Words: 13:had been made to have a grand illumination on the occasion, and hints
Words: 12:were thrown out that it would surpass anything ever witnessed in London.
Words: 15:In this they were not far out of the way; for all who witnessed the
Words: 12:scene admitted that it could scarcely have been surpassed. My own idea
Words: 15:of an illumination, as I had seen it in the backwoods of my own native
Words: 10:land, dwindled into nothing when compared with this magnificent affair.
Words: 0:
Words: 14:In company with a few friends, and a lady under my charge, I undertook
Words: 12:to pass through Sackville and one or two other streets, about eight
Words: 12:o'clock in the evening, but we found it utterly impossible to proceed.
Words: 10:Masses thronged the streets, and the wildest enthusiasm seemed to
Words: 14:prevail. In our attempt to cross the bridge, we were wedged in and lost
Words: 13:our companions; and on one occasion I was separated from the lady, and
Words: 13:took shelter under a cart standing in the street. After being jammed and
Words: 13:pulled about for nearly two hours, I returned to my lodgings, where I
Words: 14:found part of my company, who had come in one after another. At eleven
Words: 11:o'clock we had all assembled, and each told his adventures and
Words: 10:"hairbreadth escapes;" and nearly every one had lost a pocket
Words: 12:handkerchief or something of the kind: my own was among the missing.
Words: 13:However, I lost nothing; for a benevolent lady, who happened to be one
Words: 14:of the company, presented me with one which was of far more value than
Words: 5:the one I had lost.
Words: 0:
Words: 12:Every one appeared to enjoy the holiday which the Royal visit had
Words: 12:caused. But the Irish are indeed a strange people. How varied their
Words: 9:aspect--how contradictory their character. Ireland, the land of genius
Words: 7:and degradation--of great resources and unparalleled poverty--noble
Words: 10:deeds and the most revolting crimes--the land of distinguished poets,
Words: 11:splendid orators, and the bravest of soldiers--the land of ignorance and
Words: 12:beggary! Dublin is a splendid city, but its splendour is that of
Words: 10:chiselled marble rather than real life. One cannot behold these
Words: 10:architectural monuments without thinking of the great men that Ireland
Words: 10:has produced. The names of Burke, Sheridan, Flood, Grattan, O'Connell,
Words: 12:and Shiel, have become as familiar to the Americans as household words.
Words: 13:Burke is known as the statesman; Sheridan for his great speech on the
Words: 11:trial of Warren Hastings; Grattan for his eloquence; O'Connell as the
Words: 7:agitator; and Shiel as the accomplished orator.
Words: 0:
Words: 12:But of Ireland's sons, none stands higher in America than Thomas Moore,
Words: 13:the Poet. The vigour of his sarcasm, the glow of his enthusiasm, the
Words: 14:coruscations of his fancy, and the flashing of his wit, seem to be as
Words: 14:well understood in the new world as the old; and the support which his
Words: 11:pen has given to civil and religious liberty throughout the world,
Words: 9:entitled the Minstrel of Erin to this elevated position.
Words: 0:
Words: 13:Before leaving America I had heard much of the friends of my enslaved
Words: 12:countrymen residing in Ireland; and the reception I met with on all
Words: 14:hands while in public, satisfied me that what I had heard had not been
Words: 11:exaggerated. To the Webbs, Allens, and Haughtons, of Dublin, the cause
Words: 7:of the American slave is much indebted.
Words: 0:
Words: 12:I quitted Dublin with a feeling akin to leaving my native land.

Words: 3:                    PARIS, _August 23_.
Words: 0:
Words: 13:After a pleasant sojourn of three weeks in Ireland, I took passage in
Words: 13:one of the mail steamers for Liverpool, and arriving there was soon on
Words: 13:the road to the metropolis. The passage from Dublin to Liverpool was an
Words: 13:agreeable one. The rough sea that we passed through on going to Ireland
Words: 14:had given way to a dead calm, and our noble little steamer, on quitting
Words: 14:the Dublin wharf, seemed to understand that she was to have it all her
Words: 14:own way. During the first part of the evening, the boat appeared to feel
Words: 10:her importance, and, darting through the water with majestic strides,
Words: 15:she left behind her a dark cloud of smoke suspended in the air like a
Words: 14:banner; while, far astern in the wake of the vessel, could be seen the
Words: 12:rippled waves sparkling in the rays of the moon, giving strength and
Words: 7:beauty to the splendour of the evening.
Words: 0:
Words: 12:On reaching Liverpool, and partaking of a good breakfast, for which we
Words: 12:paid double price, we proceeded to the railway station, and were soon
Words: 14:going at a rate unknown to those accustomed to travel on one of our
Words: 13:American railways. At a little past two o'clock in the afternoon, we saw
Words: 13:in the distance the out-skirts of London. We could get but an indistinct
Words: 11:view, which had the appearance of one architectural mass, extending all
Words: 13:round to the horizon, and enveloped in a combination of fog and smoke;
Words: 14:and towering above every other object to be seen, was the dome of St.
Words: 2:Paul's Cathedral.
Words: 0:
Words: 13:A few moments more, and we were safely seated in a "Hansom's Patent,"
Words: 14:and on our way to Hughes's--one of the politest men of the George Fox
Words: 14:stamp we have ever met. Here we found forty or fifty persons, who, like
Words: 11:ourselves, were bound for the Peace Congress. The Sturges, the Wighams,
Words: 13:the Richardsons, the Allens, the Thomases, and a host of others not less
Words: 12:distinguished as friends of peace, were of the company--many of whom I
Words: 17:had heard of, but none of whom I had ever seen; yet I was not an entire
Words: 11:stranger to many, especially to the abolitionists. In company with a
Words: 14:friend, I sallied forth after tea to take a view of the city. The
Words: 12:evening was fine--the dense fog and smoke having to some extent passed
Words: 12:away, left the stars shining brightly, while the gas light from the
Words: 12:street lamps and the brilliant shop windows gave it the appearance of
Words: 12:day-light in a new form. "What street is this?" we asked. "Cheapside,"
Words: 13:was the reply. The street was thronged, and every body seemed to be
Words: 14:going at a rapid rate, as if there was something of importance at the
Words: 11:end of the journey. Flying vehicles of every description passing each
Words: 12:other with a dangerous rapidity, men with lovely women at their sides,
Words: 12:children running about as if they had lost their parents--all gave a
Words: 13:brilliancy to the scene scarcely to be excelled. If one wished to get
Words: 13:jammed and pushed about, he need go no farther than Cheapside. But every
Words: 14:thing of the kind is done with a degree of propriety in London, that
Words: 15:would put the New Yorkers to blush. If you are run over in London, they
Words: 14:"beg your pardon;" if they run over you in New York, you are "laughed
Words: 16:at:" in London, if your hat is knocked off it is picked up and handed to
Words: 16:you; if, in New York, you must pick it up yourself. There is a lack of
Words: 11:good manners among Americans that is scarcely known or understood in
Words: 12:Europe. Our stay in the great metropolis gave us but little opportunity
Words: 13:of seeing much of the place; for in twenty-four hours after our arrival
Words: 14:we joined the rest of the delegates, and started on our visit to our
Words: 2:Gallic neighbours.
Words: 0:
Words: 12:We assembled at the London Bridge Railway Station on Tuesday morning the
Words: 15:21st, a few minutes past nine, to the number of 600. The day was fine,
Words: 11:and every eye seemed to glow with enthusiasm. Besides the delegates,
Words: 14:there were probably not less than 600 more, who had come to see the
Words: 13:company start. We took our seats and appeared to be waiting for nothing
Words: 14:but the iron-horse to be fastened to the train, when all at once, we
Words: 12:were informed that we must go to the booking-office and change our
Words: 13:tickets. At this news every one appeared to be vexed. This caused great
Words: 13:trouble; for on returning to the train many persons got into the wrong
Words: 10:carriages; and several parties were separated from their friends, while
Words: 15:not a few were calling out at the top of their voices, "Where is my
Words: 14:wife? Where is my husband? Where is my luggage? Who's got my boy? Is
Words: 14:this the right train?" "What is that lady going to do with all these
Words: 12:children?" asked the guard. "Is she a delegate: are all the children
Words: 12:delegates?" In the carriage where I had taken my seat was a
Words: 13:good-looking lady who gave signs of being very much annoyed. "It is just
Words: 16:so when I am going anywhere: I never saw the like in my life," said she.
Words: 8:"I really wish I was at home again."
Words: 0:
Words: 14:An hour had now elapsed, and we were still at the station. However, we
Words: 13:were soon on our way, and going at express speed. In passing through
Words: 11:Kent we enjoyed the scenery exceedingly, as the weather was altogether
Words: 14:in our favour; and the drapery which nature hung on the trees, in the
Words: 14:part through which we passed, was in all its gaiety. On our arrival at
Words: 12:Folkstone, we found three steamers in readiness to convey the party to
Words: 14:Boulogne. As soon as the train stopped, a general rush was made for the
Words: 15:steamers; and in a very short time the one in which I had embarked was
Words: 13:passing out of the harbour. The boat appeared to be conscious that we
Words: 15:were going on a holy mission, and seemed to be proud of her load. There
Words: 16:is nothing in this wide world so like a thing of life as a steamer, from
Words: 14:the breathing of her steam and smoke, the energy of her motion, and the
Words: 14:beauty of her shape; while the ease with which she is managed by the
Words: 14:command of a single voice, makes her appear as obedient as the horse is
Words: 3:to the rein.
Words: 0:
Words: 13:When we were about half way between the two great European Powers, the
Words: 13:officers began to gather the tickets. The first to whom he applied, and
Words: 13:who handed out his "Excursion Ticket," was informed that we were all in
Words: 14:the wrong boat. "Is this not one of the boats to take over the
Words: 11:delegates?" asked a pretty little lady, with a whining voice. "No,
Words: 13:Madam," said the captain. "You must look to the committee for your pay,"
Words: 14:said one of the company to the captain. "I have nothing to do with
Words: 10:committees," the captain replied. "Your fare, Gentlemen, if you please."
Words: 0:
Words: 12:Here the whole party were again thrown into confusion. "Do you hear
Words: 16:that? We are in the wrong boat." "I knew it would be so," said the Rev.
Words: 14:Dr. Ritchie, of Edinburgh. "It is indeed a pretty piece of work," said a
Words: 11:plain-looking lady in a handsome bonnet. "When I go travelling again,"
Words: 14:said an elderly looking gent with an eye-glass to his face, "I will take
Words: 14:the phaeton and old Dobbin." Every one seemed to lay the blame on the
Words: 11:committee, and not, too, without some just grounds. However, Mr. Sturge,
Words: 13:one of the committee, being in the boat with us, an arrangement was
Words: 14:entered into, by which we were not compelled to pay our fare the second
Words: 1:time.
Words: 0:
Words: 12:As we neared the French coast, the first object that attracted our
Words: 14:attention was the Napoleon Pillar, on the top of which is a statue of
Words: 11:the Emperor in the Imperial robes. We landed, partook of refreshment
Words: 12:that had been prepared for us, and again repaired to the railway
Words: 11:station. The arrangements for leaving Boulogne were no better than those
Words: 14:at London. But after the delay of another hour, we were again in motion.
Words: 0:
Words: 12:It was a beautiful country through which we passed from Boulogne to
Words: 10:Amiens. Straggling cottages which bespeak neatness and comfort abound on
Words: 11:every side. The eye wanders over the diversified views with unabated
