Words: 13:the eye cannot but be gratified at viewing the entire country from the
Words: 12:coast to the metropolis. Sparkling hamlets spring up as the steam horse
Words: 10:speeds his way, at almost every point--showing the progress of
Words: 8:civilization, and the refinement of the nineteenth century.
Words: 0:
Words: 13:We arrived at Paris a few minutes past twelve o'clock at night, when,
Words: 12:according to our tickets, we should have been there at nine. Elihu
Words: 13:Burritt, who had been in Paris some days, and who had the arrangements
Words: 14:there pretty much his own way, was at the station waiting the arrival of
Words: 13:the train, and we had demonstrated to us, the best evidence that he
Words: 13:understood his business. In no other place on the whole route had the
Words: 12:affairs been so well managed; for we were seated in our respective
Words: 14:carriages and our luggage placed on the top, and away we went to our
Words: 11:hotels without the least difficulty or inconvenience. The champion of an
Words: 11:"Ocean Penny Postage" received, as he deserved, thanks from the whole
Words: 5:company for his admirable management.
Words: 0:
Words: 14:The silence of the night was only disturbed by the rolling of the wheels
Words: 13:of the omnibus, as we passed through the dimly lighted streets. Where, a
Words: 14:few months before was to be seen the flash from the cannon and the
Words: 12:musket, and the hearing of the cries and groans behind the barricades,
Words: 12:was now the stillness of death--nothing save here and there a _gens
Words: 10:d'arme_ was to be seen going his rounds in silence.
Words: 0:
Words: 13:The omnibus set us down at the hotel Bedford, Rue de L'Arend, where,
Words: 13:although near one o'clock, we found a good supper waiting for us; and,
Words: 15:as I was not devoid of an appetite, I did my share towards putting it
Words: 4:out of the way.
Words: 0:
Words: 16:The next morning I was up at an early hour, and out on the Boulevards to
Words: 15:see what might be seen. As I was passing from the Bedford to the Place
Words: 15:de La Concord, all at once, and as if by some magic power, I found
Words: 12:myself in front of the most splendid edifice imaginable, situated at the
Words: 13:end of the Rue Nationale. Seeing a number of persons entering the church
Words: 12:at that early hour, and recognising among them my friend the President
Words: 13:of the Oberlin (Ohio) Institute, and wishing not to stray too far from
Words: 11:my hotel before breakfast, I followed the crowd and entered the
Words: 11:building. The church itself consisted of a vast nave, interrupted by
Words: 11:four pews on each side, fronted with lofty fluted Corinthian columns
Words: 9:standing on pedestals, supporting colossal arches, bearing up cupolas,
Words: 9:pierced with skylights and adorned with compartments gorgeously gilt;
Words: 11:their corners supported with saints and apostles in _alto relievo_. The
Words: 12:walls of the church were lined with rich marble. The different paintings
Words: 11:and figures, gave the interior an imposing appearance. On inquiry, I
Words: 15:found that I was in the Church of the Madeleine. It was near this spot
Words: 11:that some of the most interesting scenes occurred during the Revolution
Words: 12:of 1848, which dethroned Louis Philippe. Behind the Madeleine is a small
Words: 13:but well supplied market; and on an esplanade east of the edifice, a
Words: 8:flower market is held on Tuesdays and Fridays.
Words: 0:
Words: 5:       *       *       *       *       *
Words: 0:
Words: 9:The first session of the Peace Congress is over.
Words: 0:
Words: 0:
Words: 13:The Congress met this morning at 11 o'clock, in the Salle St. Cecile,
Words: 13:Rue de la St. Lazare. The Parisians have no "Exeter Hall:" in fact,
Words: 15:there is no private hall in the city of any size, save this, where such
Words: 14:a meeting could be held. This hall has been fitted up for the occasion.
Words: 15:The room is long, and at one end has a raised platform; and at the
Words: 13:opposite end is a gallery, with seats raised one above another. On one
Words: 13:side of the hall was a balcony with sofas, which were evidently the
Words: 2:"reserved seats."
Words: 0:
Words: 13:The hall was filled at an early hour with the delegates, their friends,
Words: 11:and a good sprinkling of the French. Occasionally, small groups of
Words: 11:gentlemen would make their appearance on the platform, until it soon
Words: 12:appeared that there was little room left for others; and yet the
Words: 11:officers of the Convention had not come in. The different countries
Words: 10:were, many of them, represented here. England, France, Belgium, Germany,
Words: 10:Switzerland, Greece, Spain, and the United States, had each their
Words: 11:delegates. The Assembly began to give signs of impatience, when very
Words: 11:soon the train of officials made their appearance amid great applause.
Words: 13:Victor Hugo led the way, followed by M. Duguerry, curé of the Madeleine,
Words: 14:Elihu Burritt, and a host of others of less note. Victor Hugo took the
Words: 10:chair as President of the Congress, supported by Vice-presidents from
Words: 11:the several nations represented. Mr. Richard, the Secretary, read a dry
Words: 12:report of the names of societies, committees, &c., which was deemed the
Words: 4:opening of the Convention.
Words: 0:
Words: 12:The President then arose, and delivered one of the most impressive and
Words: 12:eloquent appeals in favour of peace that could possibly be imagined. The
Words: 14:effect produced upon the minds of all present was such as to make the
Words: 12:author of "_Notre Dame de Paris_" a great favourite with the Congress.
Words: 13:An English gentleman near me said to his friend, "I can't understand a
Words: 14:word of what he says, but is it not good?" Victor Hugo concluded his
Words: 13:speech amid the greatest enthusiasm on the part of the French, which was
Words: 11:followed by hurrahs in the old English style. The Convention was
Words: 10:successively addressed by the President of the Brussels Peace Society;
Words: 10:President Mahan of the Oberlin (Ohio) Institute, U.S.; Henry Vincent;
Words: 13:and Richard Cobden. The latter was not only the _lion_ of the English
Words: 12:delegation, but the great man of the Convention. When Mr. Cobden speaks,
Words: 14:there is no want of hearers. The great power of this gentleman lies in
Words: 12:his facts and his earnestness, for he cannot be called an eloquent
Words: 11:speaker. Mr. Cobden addressed the Congress first in French, then in
Words: 13:English; and, with the single exception of Mr. Ewart, M.P., was the only
Words: 13:one of the English delegation that could speak to the French in their
Words: 2:own language.
Words: 0:
Words: 13:The Congress was brought to a close at five o'clock, when the numerous
Words: 10:audience dispersed--the citizens to their homes, and the delegates to
Words: 3:see the sights.
Words: 0:
Words: 15:I was not a little amused at an incident that occurred at the close of
Words: 13:the first session. On the passage from America, there were in the same
Words: 11:steamer with me, several Americans, and among these, three or four
Words: 14:appeared to be much annoyed at the fact that I was a passenger, and
Words: 12:enjoying the company of white persons; and although I was not openly
Words: 13:insulted, I very often heard the remark, that "That nigger had better be
Words: 12:on his master's farm," and "What could the American Peace Society be
Words: 15:thinking about to send a black man as a delegate to Paris." Well, at the
Words: 14:close of the first sitting of the Convention, and just as I was leaving
Words: 14:Victor Hugo, to whom I had been introduced by an M.P., I observed near
Words: 15:me a gentleman with his hat in hand, whom I recognized as one of the
Words: 13:passengers who had crossed the Atlantic with me in the _Canada_, and who
Words: 13:appeared to be the most horrified at having a negro for a fellow
Words: 14:passenger. This gentleman, as I left M. Hugo, stepped up to me and said,
Words: 15:"How do you do, Mr. Brown?" "You have the advantage of me," said I. "Oh,
Words: 14:don't you know me; I was a fellow passenger with you from America; I
Words: 14:wish you would give me an introduction to Victor Hugo and Mr. Cobden." I
Words: 10:need not inform you that I declined introducing this pro-slavery
Words: 13:American to these distinguished men. I only allude to this, to show what
Words: 13:a change comes over the dreams of my white American brother, by crossing
Words: 15:the ocean. The man who would not have been seen walking with me in the
Words: 15:streets of New York, and who would not have shaken hands with me with a
Words: 13:pair of tongs while on the passage from the United States, could come
Words: 13:with hat in hand in Paris, and say, "I was your fellow-passenger." From
Words: 14:the Salle de St. Cecile, I visited the Column Vendome, from the top of
Words: 14:which I obtained a fine view of Paris and its environs. This is the
Words: 15:Bunker Hill Monument of Paris. On the top of this pillar is a statue of
Words: 11:the Emperor Napoleon, eleven feet high. The monument is built with
Words: 11:stone, and the outside covered with a metallic composition, made of
Words: 10:cannons, guns, spikes, and other warlike implements taken from the
Words: 11:Russians and Austrians by Napoleon. Above 1200 cannons were melted down
Words: 13:to help to create this monument of folly, to commemorate the success of
Words: 13:the French arms in the German Campaign. The column is in imitation of
Words: 14:the Trajan pillar at Rome, and is twelve feet in diameter at the base.
Words: 13:The door at the bottom of the pillar, and where we entered, was
Words: 11:decorated above with crowns of oak, surmounted by eagles, each weighing
Words: 13:500 lbs. The bas-relief of the shaft pursues a spiral direction to the
Words: 10:capitol, and displays, in a chronological order, the principal actions
Words: 13:of the French army, from the departure of the troops from Boulogne to
Words: 12:the battle of Austerlitz. The figures are near three feet high, and
Words: 12:their number said to be two thousand. This sumptuous monument stands on
Words: 12:a plinth of polished granite, surmounted by an iron railing; and, from
Words: 12:its size and position, has an imposing appearance when seen from any
Words: 4:part of the city.
Words: 0:
Words: 10:Everything here appears strange and peculiar--the people not less so
Words: 10:than their speech. The horses, carriages, furniture, dress, and manners,
Words: 12:are in keeping with their language. The appearance of the labourers in
Words: 10:caps, resembling nightcaps, seemed particularly strange to me. The women
Words: 11:without bonnets, and their caps turned the right side behind, had
Words: 13:nothing of the look of our American women. The prettiest woman I ever
Words: 12:saw was without a bonnet, walking on the Boulevards. While in Ireland,
Words: 15:and during the few days I was in England, I was struck with the marked
Words: 12:difference between the appearance of the women from those of my own
Words: 11:country. The American women are too tall, too sallow, and too
Words: 12:long-featured to be called pretty. This is most probably owing to the
Words: 13:fact that in America the people come to maturity earlier than in most
Words: 2:other countries.
Words: 0:
Words: 13:My first night in Paris was spent with interest. No place can present
Words: 10:greater street attractions than the Boulevards of Paris. The countless
Words: 12:number of cafés, with tables before the doors, and these surrounded by
Words: 12:men with long moustaches, with ladies at their sides, whose very smiles
Words: 12:give indication of happiness, together with the sound of music from the
Words: 14:gardens in the rear, tell the stranger that he is in a different country
Words: 3:from his own.

Words: 3:                    VERSAILLES, _August 24_.
Words: 0:
Words: 10:After the Convention had finished its sittings yesterday, I accompanied
Words: 12:Mrs. M. C---- and sisters to Versailles, where they are residing during
Words: 13:the summer. It was really pleasing to see among the hundreds of strange
Words: 11:faces in the Convention, those distinguished friends of the slave from
Words: 1:Boston.
Words: 0:
Words: 12:Mrs. C----'s residence is directly in front of the great palace where
Words: 14:so many kings have made their homes, the prince of whom was Louis XIV.
Words: 13:The palace is now unoccupied. No ruler has dared to take up his
Words: 12:residence here since Louis XVI. and Marie Antoinette were driven from it
Words: 15:by the mob from Paris on the 8th of October, 1789. The town looks like
Words: 13:the wreck of what it once was. At the commencement of the first
Words: 10:revolution, it contained one hundred thousand inhabitants; now it has
Words: 15:only about thirty thousand. It seems to be going back to what it was in
Words: 14:the time of Louis XIII., when in 1624 he built a small brick chateau,
Words: 12:and from it arose the magnificent palace which now stands here, and
Words: 11:which attracts strangers to it from all parts of the world.
Words: 0:
Words: 14:I arose this morning before the sun, and took a walk through the grounds
Words: 12:of the Palace, and remained three hours among the fountains and statuary
Words: 13:of this more than splendid place. But as I intend spending some days
Words: 12:here, and shall have better opportunities of seeing and judging, I will
Words: 8:defer my remarks upon Versailles for the present.
Words: 0:
Words: 14:Yesterday was a great day in the Congress. The session was opened by a
Words: 11:speech from M. Coquerel, the Protestant clergyman in Paris. His speech
Words: 12:was received with much applause, and seemed to create great sensation in
Words: 13:the Congress, especially at the close of his remarks, when he was seized
Words: 12:by the hand by the Abbe Duguerry, amid the most deafening and
Words: 10:enthusiastic applause of the entire multitude. The meeting was then
Words: 11:addressed in English by a short gentleman, of florid complexion. His
Words: 11:words seemed to come without the least difficulty, and his jestures,
Words: 10:though somewhat violent, were evidently studied; and the applause with
Words: 14:which he was greeted by the English delegation, showed that he was a man
Words: 12:of no little distinction among them. His speech was one continuous flow
Words: 11:of rapid, fervid eloquence, that seemed to fire every heart; and
Words: 12:although I disliked his style, I was prepossessed in his favour. This
Words: 11:was Henry Vincent, and his speech was in favour of disarmament.
Words: 0:
Words: 13:Mr. Vincent was followed by M. Emile de Girardin, the editor of _La
Words: 14:Presse_, in one of the most eloquent speeches that I ever heard; and his
Words: 11:exclamation of "Soldiers of Peace," drew thunders of applause from his
Words: 13:own countrymen. M. Girardin is not only the leader of the French press,
Words: 14:but is a writer on politics of great distinction, and a leader of no
Words: 10:inconsiderable party in the National Assembly; although still a young
Words: 11:man, apparently not more than thirty-eight or forty years of age.
Words: 0:
Words: 13:After a speech from Mr. Ewart, M.P., in French, and another from Mr.
Words: 13:Cobden in the same language, the Convention was brought to a close for
Words: 14:the day. I spent the morning yesterday, in visiting some of the lions of
Words: 11:the French capital, among which was the Louvre. The French Government
Words: 12:having kindly ordered, that the members of the Peace Congress should be
Words: 12:admitted free, and without ticket, to all the public works, I had
Words: 12:nothing to do but present my card of membership, and was immediately
Words: 1:admitted.
Words: 0:
Words: 15:The first room I entered, was nearly a quarter of a mile in length; is
Words: 12:known as the "Long Gallery," and contains some of the finest paintings
Words: 12:in the world. On entering this superb palace, my first impression was,
Words: 12:that all Christendom had been robbed, that the Louvre might make a
Words: 10:splendid appearance. This is the Italian department, and one would
Words: 13:suppose by its appearance that but few paintings had been left in Italy.
Words: 14:The entrance end of the Louvre was for a long time in an unfinished
Words: 11:state, but was afterwards completed by that master workman, the Emperor
Words: 11:Napoleon. It was long thought that the building would crumble into
Words: 12:decay, but the genius of the great Corsican rescued it from ruin.
Words: 0:
Words: 13:During our walk through the Louvre, we saw some twenty or thirty artists
Words: 11:copying paintings; some had their copies finished and were going out,
Words: 12:others half done, while many had just commenced. I remained some minutes
Words: 14:near a pretty French girl, who was copying a painting of a dog rescuing
Words: 12:a child from a stream of water into which it had fallen.
Words: 0:
Words: 15:I walked down one side of the hall and up the other, and was about
Words: 14:leaving, when I was informed that this was only one room, and that a
Words: 13:half-dozen more were at my service; but a clock on a neighbouring church
Words: 14:reminded me that I must quit the Louvre for the Salle de St. Cecile.
Words: 0:
Words: 5:       *       *       *       *       *
Words: 0:
Words: 14:This morning the Hall was filled at an early hour with rather a more
Words: 10:fashionable looking audience than on any former occasion, and all
Words: 12:appeared anxious for the Congress to commence its session, as it was
Words: 13:understood to be the last day. After the reading of several letters from
Words: 12:gentlemen, apologising for their not being able to attend, the speech of
Words: 13:Elihu Burritt was read by a son of M. Coquerel. I felt somewhat
Words: 12:astonished that my countryman, who was said to be master of fifty
Words: 12:languages, had to get some one to read his speech in French.
Words: 0:
Words: 12:The Abbe Duguerry now came forward amid great cheering, and said that
Words: 10:"the eminent journalist, Girardin, and the great English logician, Mr.
Words: 11:Cobden, had made it unnecessary for any further advocacy in that
Words: 12:assembly of the Peace cause--that if the principles laid down in the
Words: 13:resolutions were carried out, the work would be done. He said that the
Words: 9:question of general pacification was built on truth--truth which
Words: 13:emanated from God--and it were as vain to undertake to prevent air from
Words: 12:expanding as to check the progress of truth. It must and would
Words: 1:prevail."
Words: 0:
Words: 11:A pale, thin-faced gentleman next ascended the platform (or tribune, as
Words: 13:it was called) amid shouts of applause from the English, and began his
Words: 13:speech in rather a low tone, when compared with the sharp voice of
Words: 14:Vincent, or the thunder of the Abbe Duguerry. An audience is not apt to
Words: 11:be pleased or even contented with an inferior speaker, when surrounded
Words: 11:by eloquent men, and I looked every moment for manifestations of
Words: 12:disapprobation, as I felt certain that the English delegation had made a
Words: 11:mistake in applauding this gentleman who seemed to make such an
Words: 12:unpromising beginning. But the speaker soon began to get warm on the
Words: 14:subject, and even at times appeared as if he had spoken before. In a
Words: 13:very short time, with the exception of his own voice, the stillness of
Words: 11:death prevailed throughout the building. The speaker, in the delivery of
Words: 13:one of the most logical speeches made in the Congress, and despite of
Words: 13:his thin, sallow look, interested me much more than any whom I had
Words: 12:before heard. Towards the close of his remarks, he was several times
Words: 9:interrupted by manifestations of approbation; and finally concluded amid
Words: 11:great cheering. I inquired the gentleman's name, and was informed that
Words: 8:it was Edward Miall, editor of the _Nonconformist_.
Words: 0:
Words: 11:After speeches from several others, the great Peace Congress of 1849,
Words: 11:which had brought men together from nearly all the governments of
Words: 14:Europe, and many from America, was brought to a final close by a speech
Words: 11:from the President, returning thanks for the honour that had been
Words: 14:conferred upon him. He said, "My address shall be short, and yet I have
Words: 14:to bid you adieu! How resolve to do so? Here, during three days, have
Words: 11:questions of the deepest import been discussed, examined, probed to the
Words: 10:bottom; and during these discussions, counsels have been given to
Words: 12:governments which they will do well to profit by. If these days'
Words: 13:sittings are attended with no other result, they will be the means of
Words: 12:sowing in the minds of those present, gems of cordiality which must
Words: 10:ripen into good fruit. England, France, Belgium, Europe, and America,
Words: 14:would all be drawn closer by these sittings. Yet the moment to part has
Words: 14:arrived, but I can feel that we are strongly united in heart. But before
Words: 12:parting I may congratulate you and myself on the result of our
Words: 10:proceedings. We have been all joined together without distinction of
Words: 13:country; we have all been united in one common feeling during our three
Words: 13:days' communion. The good work cannot go back, it must advance, it must
Words: 14:be accomplished. The course of the future may be judged of by the sound
Words: 14:of the footsteps of the past. In the course of that day's discussion, a
Words: 13:reminiscence had been handed up to one of the speakers, that this was
Words: 11:the anniversary of the dreadful massacre of St. Bartholomew: the rev.
Words: 11:gentleman who was speaking turned away from the thought of that
Words: 11:sanguinary scene with pious horror, natural to his sacred calling. But
Words: 15:I, who may boast of firmer nerve, I take up the remembrance. Yes, it was
Words: 12:on this day, two hundred and seventy-seven years ago, that Paris was
Words: 14:roused from slumber by the sound of that bell which bore the name of
Words: 12:_cloche d'argent_. Massacre was on foot, seeking with keen eye for its
Words: 12:victim--man was busy in slaying man. That slaughter was called forth by
Words: 12:mingled passions of the worst description. Hatred of all kinds was there
Words: 11:urging on the slayer--hatred of a religious, a political, a personal
Words: 14:character. And yet on the anniversary of that same day of horror, and in
Words: 13:that very city whose blood was flowing like water, has God this day
Words: 12:given a rendezvous to men of peace, whose wild tumult is transformed
Words: 13:into order, and animosity into love. The stain of blood is blotted out,
Words: 14:and in its place beams forth a ray of holy light. All distinctions are
Words: 10:removed, and Papist and Huguenot meet together in friendly communion.
Words: 13:(Loud cheers.) Who that thinks of these amazing changes can doubt of the
Words: 12:progress that has been made? But whoever denies the force of progress
Words: 12:must deny God, since progress is the boon of Providence, and emanated
Words: 13:from the great Being above. I feel gratified for the change that has
Words: 14:been effected, and, pointing solemnly to the past, I say let this day be
Words: 11:ever held memorable--let the 24th of August, 1572, be remembered only
Words: 13:for the purpose of being compared with the 24th of August, 1849; and
Words: 14:when we think of the latter, and ponder over the high purpose to which
Words: 12:it has been devoted--the advocacy of the principles of peace--let us not
Words: 14:be so wanting in reliance on Providence as to doubt for one moment of
Words: 7:the eventful success of our holy cause."
Words: 0:
Words: 11:The most enthusiastic cheers followed this interesting speech. A vote of
Words: 12:thanks to the government, and three times three cheers, with Mr. Cobden
Words: 9:as "fugleman," ended the great Peace Congress of 1849.
Words: 0:
Words: 12:Time for separating had arrived, yet all seemed unwilling to leave the
Words: 15:place, where for three days men of all creeds and of no creed had met
Words: 12:upon one common platform. In one sense the meeting was a glorious
Words: 12:one--in another, it was mere child's play; for the Congress had been
Words: 11:restricted to the discussion of certain topics. They were permitted to
Words: 13:dwell on the blessings of peace, but were not allowed to say anything
Words: 12:about the very subjects above all others that should have been brought
Words: 13:before the Congress. A French army had invaded Rome and put down the
Words: 13:friends of political and religious freedom, yet not a word was said in
Words: 12:reference to it. The fact is, the Committee permitted the Congress to
Words: 13:be _gagged_, before it had met. They put padlocks upon their own mouths,
Words: 14:and handed the keys to the government. And this was sorely felt by many
Words: 11:of the speakers. Richard Cobden, who had thundered his anathemas against
Words: 14:the Corn Laws of his own country, and against wars in every clime, had
Words: 14:to sit quiet in his fetters. Henry Vincent, who can make a louder speech
Words: 12:in favour of peace, than almost any other man, and whose denunciations
Words: 14:of "all war," have gained him no little celebrity with peace men, had to
Words: 13:confine himself to the blessings of peace. Oh! how I wished for a
Words: 8:Massachusetts atmosphere, a New England Convention platform, with
Words: 10:Wendell Phillips as the speaker, before that assembled multitude from
Words: 5:all parts of the world.
Words: 0:
Words: 15:But the Congress is over, and cannot now be made different; yet it is to
Words: 12:be hoped that neither the London Peace Committee, nor any other men
Words: 12:having the charge of getting up such another great meeting, will commit
Words: 4:such an error again.

Words: 2:LETTER V.
Words: 0:
Words: 9:_M. de Tocqueville's Grand Soiree--Madame de Tocqueville--Visit of the
Words: 5:Peace Delegates to Versailles--The Breakfast--Speechmaking--The
Words: 3:Trianons--Waterworks--St. Cloud--The Fête._
Words: 0:
Words: 0:
Words: 3:                    VERSAILLES, _August 24_.
Words: 0:
Words: 13:The day after the close of the Congress, the delegates and their friends
Words: 12:were invited to a soirée by M. de Tocqueville, Minister for Foreign
Words: 12:Affairs, to take place on the next evening (Saturday); and, as my
Words: 13:coloured face and curly hair did not prevent my getting an invitation, I
Words: 9:was present with the rest of my peace brethren.
Words: 0:
Words: 14:Had I been in America, where colour is considered a crime, I would not
Words: 14:have been seen at such a gathering, unless as a servant. In company with
Words: 12:several delegates, we left the Bedford Hotel for the mansion of the
Words: 12:Minister of Foreign Affairs; and, on arriving, we found a file of
Words: 13:soldiers drawn up before the gate. This did not seem much like peace:
Words: 13:however, it was merely done in honour of the company. We entered the
Words: 11:building through massive doors and resigned ourselves into the hands of
Words: 11:good-looking waiters in white wigs; and, after our names were duly
Words: 13:announced, were passed from room to room till I was presented to Madame
Words: 11:de Tocqueville, who was standing near the centre of the large
Words: 13:drawing-room, with a bouquet in her hand. I was about passing on, when
Words: 11:the gentleman who introduced me intimated that I was an "American
Words: 11:slave." At the announcement of this fact the distinguished lady extended
Words: 14:her hand and gave me a cordial welcome--at the same time saying, "I hope
Words: 12:you feel yourself free in Paris." Having accepted an invitation to a
Words: 16:seat by the lady's side, who seated herself on a sofa, I was soon what I
Words: 11:most dislike, "the observed of all observers." I recognised among many
Words: 13:of my own countrymen, who were gazing at me, the American Consul, Mr.
Words: 12:Walsh. My position did not improve his looks. The company present on
Words: 11:this occasion were variously estimated at from one thousand to fifteen
Words: 10:hundred. Among these were the Ambassadors from the different countries
Words: 12:represented at the French metropolis, and many of the _elite_ of Paris.
Words: 13:One could not but be interested with the difference in dress, looks, and
Words: 11:manners of this assemblage of strangers whose language was as different
Words: 11:as their general appearance. Delight seemed to beam in every countenance
Words: 13:as the living stream floated from one room to another. The house and
Words: 11:gardens were illuminated in the most gorgeous manner. Red, yellow, blue,
Words: 12:green, and many other coloured lamps, suspended from the branches of the
Words: 14:trees in the gardens, gave life and animation to the whole scene out of
Words: 11:doors. The soirée passed off satisfactorily to all parties; and by
Words: 8:twelve o'clock I was again at my Hotel.
Words: 0:
Words: 5:       *       *       *       *       *
Words: 0:
Words: 11:Through the politeness of the government the members of the Congress
Words: 14:have not only had the pleasure of seeing all the public works free, and
Words: 11:without special ticket, but the palaces of Versailles and St. Cloud,
Words: 11:together with their splendid grounds, have been thrown open, and the
Words: 13:water-works set to playing in both places. This mark of respect for the
Words: 13:Peace movement is commendable in the French; and were I not such a
Words: 13:strenuous friend of free speech, this act would cause me to overlook the
Words: 11:padlocks that the government put upon our lips in the Congress.
Words: 0:
Words: 13:Two long trains left Paris at nine o'clock for Versailles; and at each
Words: 13:of the stations the company were loudly cheered by the people who had
Words: 12:assembled to see them pass. At Versailles, we found thousands at the
Words: 13:station, who gave us a most enthusiastic welcome. We were blessed with a
Words: 14:goodly number of the fair sex, who always give life and vigour to such
Words: 11:scenes. The train had scarcely stopped, ere the great throng were
Words: 12:wending their ways in different directions, some to the cafés to get
Words: 11:what an early start prevented their getting before leaving Paris, and
Words: 14:others to see the soldiers who were on review. But most bent their steps
Words: 4:towards the great palace.
Words: 0:
Words: 12:At eleven o'clock we were summoned to the _dejeuner_, which had been
Words: 11:prepared by the English delegates in honour of their American friends.
Words: 12:About six hundred sat down at the tables. Breakfast being ended, Mr.
Words: 12:Cobden was called to the chair, and several speeches were made. Many
Words: 13:who had not an opportunity to speak at the Congress, thought this a
Words: 12:good chance; and the written addresses which had been studied during the
Words: 11:passage from America, with the hope that they would immortalize their
Words: 11:authors before the great Congress, were produced at the breakfast table.
Words: 12:But speech-making was not the order of the day. Too many thundering
Words: 13:addresses had been delivered in the Salle de St. Cecile, to allow the
Words: 12:company to sit and hear dryly written and worse delivered speeches in
Words: 2:the Teniscourt.
Words: 0:
Words: 15:There was no limited time given to the speakers, yet no one had been on
Words: 14:his feet five minutes, before the cry was heard from all parts of the
Words: 12:house, "Time, time." One American was hissed down, another took his seat
Words: 14:with a red face, and a third opened his bundle of paper, looked around
Words: 14:at the audience, made a bow, and took his seat amid great applause. Yet
Words: 14:some speeches were made, and to good effect, the best of which was by
Words: 12:Elihu Burritt, who was followed by the Rev. James Freeman Clark. I
Words: 12:regretted very much that the latter did not deliver his address before
Words: 13:the Congress, for he is a man of no inconsiderable talent, and an
Words: 5:acknowledged friend of the slave.
Words: 0:
Words: 13:The cry of "The water-works are playing," "The water is on," broke up
Words: 13:the meeting, without even a vote of thanks to the Chairman; and the
Words: 12:whole party were soon revelling among the fountains and statues of Louis
Words: 13:XIV. Description would fail to give a just idea of the grandeur and
Words: 14:beauty of this splendid place. I do not think that any thing can surpass
Words: 12:the fountain of Neptune, which stands near the Grand Trianon. One may
Words: 12:easily get lost in wandering through the grounds of Versailles, but he
Words: 11:will always be in sight of some life-like statue. These monuments,
Words: 12:erected to gratify the fancy of a licentious king, make their appearance
Words: 14:at every turn. Two lions, the one overturning a wild boar, the other a
Words: 13:wolf, both the production of Fillen, pointed out to us the fountain of
Words: 14:Diana. But I will not attempt to describe to you any of the very
Words: 6:beautiful sculptured gods and goddesses here.
Words: 0:
Words: 15:With a single friend I paid a visit to the two Trianons. The larger was,
Words: 13:we were told, just as king Louis Philippe left it. One room was
Words: 11:splendidly fitted up for the reception of Her Majesty Queen Victoria;
Words: 13:who, it appeared, had promised a visit to the French Court; but the
Words: 12:French Monarch ran away from his throne before the time arrived. The
Words: 13:Grand Trianon is not larger than many noblemen's seats that may be seen
Words: 14:in a day's ride through any part of the British empire. The building has
Words: 10:only a ground floor, but its proportions are very elegant.
Words: 0:
Words: 14:We next paid our respects to the Little Trianon. This appears to be the
Words: 12:most Republican of any of the French palaces. I inspected this little
Words: 13:palace with much interest, not more for its beauty than because of its
Words: 11:having been the favourite residence of that purest of Princesses, best
Words: 10:of Queens, and most affectionate of mothers, Marie Antoinette. The
Words: 14:grounds and building may be said to be only a palace in miniature, and
Words: 13:this makes it still a more lovely spot. The building consists of a
Words: 10:square pavilion two stories high, and separated entirely from the
Words: 12:accessory buildings, which are on the left, and among them a pretty
Words: 14:chapel. But a wish to be with the multitude, who were roving among the
Words: 8:fountains, cut short my visit to the trianons.
Words: 0:
Words: 15:The day was very fine, and the whole party seemed to enjoy it. It was
Words: 11:said that there were more than one hundred thousand persons at
Words: 11:Versailles during the day. The company appeared to lose themselves with
Words: 11:the pleasure of walking among the trees, flower beds, fountains, and
Words: 13:statues. I met more than one wife seeking a lost husband, and _vice
Words: 12:versa_. Many persons were separated from their friends and did not meet
Words: 14:them again till at the hotels in Paris. In the train returning to Paris,
Words: 14:an old gentleman who was seated near me said, "I would rest contented if
Words: 9:I thought I should ever see my wife again!"
Words: 0:
Words: 14:At four o'clock we were _en route_ to St. Cloud, the much loved and
Words: 11:favourite residence of the Emperor Napoleon. It seemed that all Paris
Words: 14:had come out to St. Cloud to see how the English and Americans would
Words: 12:enjoy the playing of the water-works. Many kings and rulers of the
Words: 11:French have made St. Cloud their residence, but none have impressed
Words: 14:their images so indelibly upon it as Napoleon. It was here he was first
Words: 11:elevated to power, and here Josephine spent her most happy hours.
Words: 0:
Words: 11:The apartments where Napoleon was married to Marie Louise; the private
Words: 13:rooms of Josephine and Marie Antoinette, were all in turn shown to us.
Words: 12:While standing on the balcony looking at Paris one cannot wonder that
Words: 13:the Emperor should have selected this place as his residence, for a more
Words: 8:lovely spot cannot be found than St. Cloud.
Words: 0:
Words: 15:The palace is on the side of a hill, two leagues from Paris, and so
Words: 12:situated that it looks down upon the French capital. Standing, as we
Words: 12:did, viewing Paris from St. Cloud, and the setting sun reflecting upon
Words: 14:the domes, spires, and towers of the city of fashion, made us feel that
Words: 12:this was the place from which the monarch should watch his subjects.
Words: 14:From the hour of arrival at St. Cloud till near eight o'clock, we were
Words: 10:either inspecting the splendid palace or roaming the grounds and
Words: 12:gardens, whose beautiful walks and sweet flowers made it appear a very
Words: 3:Paradise on earth.
Words: 0:
Words: 12:At eight o'clock the water-works were put in motion, and the variagated
Words: 10:lamps with their many devices, displaying flowers, stars, and wheels,
Words: 12:all with a brilliancy that can scarcely be described, seemed to throw
Words: 13:everything in the shade we had seen at Versailles. At nine o'clock the
Words: 13:train was announced, and after a good deal of jamming and pushing about,
Words: 8:we were again on the way to Paris.

Words: 2:LETTER VI.
Words: 0:
Words: 7:_The Tuileries--Place de la Concorde--The Egyptian Obelisk--Palais
Words: 10:Royal--Residence of Robespierre--A Visit to the Room in which Charlotte
Words: 9:Corday killed Marat--Church de Notre Dame--Palais de Justice--Hotel des
Words: 3:Invalids--National Assembly--The Elysee._
Words: 0:
Words: 0:
Words: 3:                    PARIS, _August 28_.
Words: 0:
Words: 13:Yesterday morning I started at an early hour for the Palace of the
Words: 13:Tuileries. A show of my card of membership of the Congress (which had
Words: 13:carried me through so many of the public buildings) was enough to gain
Words: 14:me immediate admission. The attack of the mob on the palace, on the 20th
Words: 15:of June, 1792, the massacre of the Swiss guard on the 10th of August of
Words: 14:the same year, the attack by the people in July 1830, together with the
Words: 12:recent flight of king Louis Philippe and family, made me anxious to
Words: 4:visit the old pile.
Words: 0:
Words: 13:We were taken from room to room, until the entire building had been
Words: 12:inspected. In front of the Tuileries, are a most magnificent garden and
Words: 14:grounds. These were all laid out by Louis XIV., and are left nearly as
Words: 11:they were during that monarch's reign. Above fifty acres surrounded by
Words: 14:an iron rail fence, fronts the Place de la Concorde, and affords a place
Words: 12:of promenade for the Parisians. I walked the pleasing grounds, and saw
Words: 12:hundreds of well dressed persons walking under the shade of the great
Words: 14:chestnuts, or sitting on chairs which were kept to let at two sous a
Words: 12:piece. Near by is the Place de Carrousel, noted for its historical
Words: 8:remembrances. Many incidents connected with the several revolutions
Words: 12:occurred here, and it is pointed out as the place where Napoleon
Words: 11:reviewed that formidable army of his before its departure for Russia.
Words: 0:
Words: 13:From the Tuileries, I took a stroll through the Place de la Concorde,
Words: 14:which has connected with it so many acts of cruelty, that it made me
Words: 15:shudder as I passed over its grounds. As if to take from one's mind the
Words: 13:old associations of this place, the French have erected on it, or rather
Words: 13:given a place to, the celebrated obelisk of Luxor, which now is the
Words: 12:chief attraction on the grounds. The obelisk was brought from Egypt at
Words: 12:an enormous expense; for which purpose a ship was built, and several
Words: 13:hundred men employed above three years in its removal. It is formed of
Words: 13:the finest red syenite, and covered on each side with three lines of
Words: 7:hieroglyphic inscriptions, commemorative of Sesostris--the middle lines
Words: 11:being the most deeply cut and most carefully finished; and the
Words: 12:characters altogether number more than 1600. The obelisk is of a single
Words: 14:stone, is 72 feet in height, weighs 500,000 lbs., and stands on a block
Words: 14:of granite that weighs 250,000 lbs. He who can read Latin will see that
Words: 13:the monument tells its own story, but to me its characters were all
Words: 1:blank.
Words: 0:
Words: 13:It would be tedious to follow the history of this old and venerated
Words: 13:stone, which was taken from the quarry 1550 years before the birth of
Words: 12:Christ; placed in Thebes; its removal; the journey to the Nile, and
Words: 13:down the Nile; thence to Cherbourg, and lastly its arrival in Paris on
Words: 12:the 23d of December, 1833--just one year before I escaped from slavery.
Words: 15:The obelisk was raised on the spot where it now stands, on the 25th of
Words: 12:October, 1836, in the presence of Louis Philippe and amid the greetings
Words: 3:of 160,000 persons.
Words: 0:
Words: 14:Having missed my dinner, I crossed over to the Palais Royal, to a dining
Words: 14:saloon, and can assure you that a better dinner may be had there for
Words: 14:five francs, than can be got in New York for twice that sum, and
Words: 14:especially if the person who wants the dinner is a coloured man. I found
Words: 9:no prejudice against my complexion in the Palais Royal.
Words: 0:
Words: 13:Many of the rooms in this once abode of Royalty, are most splendidly
Words: 9:furnished, and decorated with valuable pictures. The likenesses of
Words: 11:Madame de Stael, J.J. Rousseau, Cromwell, and Francis I., are among
Words: 1:them.
Words: 0:
Words: 5:       *       *       *       *       *
Words: 0:
Words: 10:After several unsuccessful attempts to-day, in company with R.D. Webb,
Words: 11:Esq., to seek out the house where once resided the notorious
Words: 13:Robespierre, I was fortunate enough to find it, but not until I had
Words: 14:lost the company of my friend. The house is No. 396, Rue St. Honore,
Words: 13:opposite the Church of the Assumption. It stands back, and is reached by
Words: 12:entering a court. During the first revolution it was occupied by M.
Words: 13:Duplay, with whom Robespierre lodged. The room used by the great man of
Words: 14:the revolution, was pointed out to me. It is small, and the ceiling low,
Words: 14:with two windows looking out upon the court. The pin upon which the blue
Words: 13:coat once hung, is still in the wall. While standing there, I could
Words: 11:almost imagine that I saw the great "Incorruptible," sitting at the
Words: 12:small table composing those speeches which gave him so much power and
Words: 7:influence in the Convention and the Clubs.
Words: 0:
Words: 13:Here, the disciple of Rousseau sat and planned how he should outdo his
Words: 13:enemies and hold on to his friends. From this room he went forth,
Words: 14:followed by his dog Brunt, to take his solitary walk in a favourite and
Words: 10:neighbouring field, or to the fiery discussions of the National
Words: 12:Convention. In the same street, is the house in which Madame Roland--one
Words: 3:of Robespierre's victims--resided.
Words: 0:
Words: 14:A view of the residence of one of the master spirits of the French
Words: 12:revolution inclined me to search out more, and therefore I proceeded to
Words: 11:the old town, and after winding through several small streets--some of
Words: 15:them so narrow as not to admit more than one cab at a time--I found
Words: 14:myself in the Rue de L'Ecole de Medecine, and standing in front of house
Words: 13:No. 20. This was the residence, during the early days of the revolution,
Words: 8:of that bloodthirsty demon in human form, Marat.
Words: 0:
Words: 15:I said to a butcher, whose shop was underneath, that I wanted to see La
Words: 16:Chambre de Marat. He called out to the woman of the house to know if I
Words: 14:could be admitted, and the reply was, that the room was used as a
Words: 7:sleeping apartment, and could not be seen.
Words: 0:
Words: 13:As this was private property, my blue card of membership to the Congress
Words: 13:was not available. But after slipping a franc into the old lady's hand,
Words: 14:I was informed that the room was now ready. We entered a court and
Words: 14:ascended a flight of stairs, the entrance to which is on the right; then
Words: 13:crossing to the left, we were shown into a moderate-sized room on the
Words: 14:first floor, with two windows looking out upon a yard. Here it was where
Words: 13:the "Friend of the People" (as he styled himself,) sat and wrote those
Words: 12:articles that appeared daily in his journal, urging the people to "hang
Words: 14:the rich upon lamp posts." The place where the bath stood, in which he
Words: 13:was bathing at the time he was killed by Charlotte Corday, was pointed
Words: 13:out to us; and even something representing an old stain of blood was
Words: 15:shown as the place where he was laid when taken out of the bath. The
Words: 12:window, behind whose curtains the heroine hid, after she had plunged the
Words: 15:dagger into the heart of the man whom she thought was the cause of the
Words: 13:shedding of so much blood by the guillotine, was pointed out with a
Words: 8:seeming degree of pride by the old woman.
Words: 0:
Words: 14:With my Guide Book in hand, I again went forth to "hunt after new
Words: 1:fancies."
Words: 0:
Words: 5:       *       *       *       *       *
Words: 0:
Words: 11:After walking over the ground where the guillotine once stood, cutting
Words: 13:off its hundred and fifty heads per day, and then visiting the place
Words: 11:where some of the chief movers in that sanguinary revolution once
Words: 13:lived, I felt little disposed to sleep, when the time for it had
Words: 14:arrived. However, I was out this morning at an early hour, and on the
Words: 12:Champs Elysees; and again took a walk over the place where the
Words: 11:guillotine stood, when its fatal blade was sending so many unprepared
Words: 12:spirits into eternity. When standing here, you have the Palace of the
Words: 14:Tuileries on one side, the arch on the other; on a third, the classic
Words: 12:Madeleine; and on the fourth, the National Assembly. It caused my blood
Words: 14:to chill, the idea of being on the identical spot where the heads of
Words: 14:Louis XVI. and his Queen, after being cut off, were held up to satisfy
Words: 10:the blood-thirsty curiosity of the two hundred thousand persons that
Words: 13:were assembled on the Place de la Revolution. Here Royal blood flowed as
Words: 13:it never did before or since. The heads of patricians and plebians, were
Words: 12:thrown into the same basket, without any regard to birth or station.
Words: 12:Here Robespierre and Danton had stood again and again, and looked their
Words: 13:victims in the face as they ascended the scaffold; and here, these same
Words: 14:men had to mount the very scaffold that they had erected for others. I
Words: 13:wandered up the Seine, till I found myself looking at the statue of
Words: 14:Henry the IV. over the principal entrance of the Hotel de Ville. When we
Words: 12:take into account the connection of the Hotel de Ville with the
Words: 13:different revolutions, we must come to the conclusion, that it is one of
Words: 12:the most remarkable buildings in Paris. The room was pointed out where
Words: 12:Robespierre held his counsels, and from the windows of which he could
Words: 13:look out upon the Place de Greve, where the guillotine stood before its
Words: 13:removal to the Place de la Concorde. The room is large, with gilded
Words: 8:hangings, splendid old-fashioned chandeliers, and a chimney-piece with
Words: 10:fine antiquated carvings, that give it a venerable appearance. Here
Words: 12:Robespierre not only presided at the counsels that sent hundreds to the
Words: 13:guillotine; but from this same spot, he, with his brother St. Just and
Words: 11:others, were dragged before the Committee of Public Safety, and thence
Words: 8:to the guillotine, and justice and revenge satisfied.
Words: 0:
Words: 11:The window from which Lafayette addressed the people in 1830, and
Words: 14:presented to them Louis Philippe, as the king, was shown to us. Here the
Words: 9:poet, statesman, philosopher and orator, Lamartine, stood in February
Words: 12:1848, and, by the power of his eloquence, succeeded in keeping the
Words: 12:people quiet. Here he forced the mob, braved the bayonets presented to
Words: 12:his breast, and, by his good reasoning, induced them to retain the
Words: 11:tri-coloured flag, instead of adopting the red flag, which he considered
Words: 4:the emblem of blood.
Words: 0:
Words: 13:Lamartine is a great heroic genius, dear to liberty and to France; and
Words: 11:successive generations, as they look back upon the revolution of 1848,
Words: 12:will recall to memory the many dangers which nothing but his dauntless
Words: 10:courage warded off. The difficulties which his wisdom surmounted, and
Words: 12:the good service that he rendered to France, can never be adequately
Words: 13:estimated or too highly appreciated. It was at the Hotel de Ville that
Words: 9:the Republic of 1848 was proclaimed to the people.
Words: 0:
Words: 15:I next paid my respects to the Column of July that stands on the spot
Words: 14:formerly occupied by the Bastile. It is 163 feet in height, and on the
Words: 16:top is the Genius of Liberty, with a torch in his right hand, and in the
Words: 13:left a broken chain. After a fatiguing walk up a winding stair, I
Words: 12:obtained a splendid view of Paris from the top of the column.
Words: 0:
Words: 13:I thought I should not lose the opportunity of seeing the Church de
Words: 14:Notre Dame while so near to it, and, therefore, made it my next rallying
Words: 10:point. No edifice connected with religion has had more interesting
Words: 12:incidents occurring in it than this old church. Here Pope Pius VII.
Words: 11:placed the Imperial Crown on the head of the Corsican--or rather
Words: 14:Napoleon took the Crown from his hands and placed it on his own head.
Words: 14:Satan dragging the wicked to ----; the rider on the red horse at the
Words: 12:opening of the second seal; the blessedness of the saints; and several
Words: 10:other striking sculptured figures were among the many curiosities in
Words: 13:this splendid place. A hasty view from the gallery concluded my visit to
Words: 3:the Notre Dame.
Words: 0:
Words: 13:Leaving the old church I strayed off in a direction towards the Seine,
Words: 11:and passed by an old looking building of stately appearance, and
Words: 13:recognised, among a throng passing in and out, a number of the members
Words: 14:of the Peace Congress. I joined a party entering, and was soon in the
Words: 13:presence of men with gowns on, and men with long staffs in their
Words: 12:hands--and on inquiry found that I was in the Palais de Justice;
Words: 12:beneath which is the Conciergerie, a noted prison. Louis XVI. and Marie
Words: 8:Antoinette were tried and condemned to death here.
Words: 0:
Words: 10:A bas-relief, by Cortat, representing Louis in conference with his
Words: 13:Counsel, is here seen. But I had visited too many places of interest
Words: 13:during the day to remain long in a building surrounded by officers of
Words: 8:justice, and took a stroll upon the Boulevards.
Words: 0:
Words: 14:The Boulevards may be termed the Regent Street of Paris, or a New Yorker
Words: 12:would call them Broadway. While passing a café, my German friend Faigo,
Words: 11:whose company I had enjoyed during the passage from America, recognised
Words: 16:me, and I sat down and took a cup of delicious coffee for the first time
Words: 15:on the side walk, in sight of hundreds who were passing up and down the
Words: 11:street every hour. From three till eleven o'clock, P.M., the Boulevards
Words: 13:are lined with men and women sitting before the doors of the saloons
Words: 14:drinking their coffee or wines, or both at the same time, as fancy may
Words: 13:dictate. All Paris appeared to be on the Boulevards, and looking as if
Words: 8:the great end of this life was enjoyment.
Words: 0:
Words: 5:       *       *       *       *       *
Words: 0:
Words: 16:Anxious to see as much as possible of Paris in the limited time I had to
Words: 14:stay in it, I hired a cab yesterday morning and commenced with the Hotel
Words: 12:des Invalids, a magnificent building, within a few minutes' walk of the
Words: 11:National Assembly. On each side of the entrance gate are figures
Words: 10:representing nations conquered by Louis XIV., with colossal statues of
Words: 13:Mars and Minerva. The dome on the edifice is the loftiest in Paris--the
Words: 7:height from the ground being 323 feet.
Words: 0:
Words: 14:Immediately below the dome is the tomb of the man at whose word the
Words: 13:world turned pale. A statue of the Emperor Napoleon stands in the second
Words: 7:piazza, and is of the finest bronze.
Words: 0:
Words: 13:This building is the home of the pensioned soldiers of France. It was
Words: 15:enough to make one sick at the idea of war, to look upon the mangled
Words: 14:bodies of these old soldiers. Men with arms and no legs; others had legs
Words: 12:but no arms; some with canes and crutches, and some wheeling themselves
Words: 12:about in little hand carts. About three thousand of the decayed soldiers
Words: 14:were lodged in the Hotel des Invalids, at the time of my visit. Passing
Words: 15:the National Assembly on my return, I spent a moment or two in it. The
Words: 10:interior of this building resembles an amphitheatre. It is constructed
Words: 12:to accommodate 900 members, each having a separate desk. The seat upon
Words: 13:which the Duchesse of Orleans, and her son, the Comte de Paris, sat,
Words: 11:when they visited the National Assembly after the flight of Louis
Words: 11:Philippe, was shown with considerable alacrity. As I left the building,
Words: 14:I heard that the President of the Republic was on the point of leaving
Words: 14:the Elysee for St. Cloud, and with the hope of seeing the "Prisoner of
Words: 11:Ham," I directed my cabman to drive me to the Elysee.
Words: 0:
Words: 14:In a few moments we were between two files of soldiers, and entering the
Words: 15:gates of the palace. I called out to the driver and told him to stop;
Words: 17:but I was too late, for we were now in front of the massive doors of the
Words: 13:palace, and a liveried servant opened the cab door, bowed, and asked if
Words: 12:I had an engagement with the President. You may easily "guess" his
Words: 15:surprise when I told him no. In my best French, I asked the cabman why
Words: 15:he had come to the palace, and was answered, "You told me to." By this
Words: 13:time a number had gathered round, all making inquiries as to what I
Words: 14:wanted. I told the driver to retrace his steps, and, amid the shrugs of
Words: 12:their shoulders, the nods of their heads, and the laughter of the
Words: 12:soldiers, I left the Elysee without even a sight of the President's
Words: 14:mustaches for my trouble. This was only one of the many mistakes I made
Words: 3:while in Paris.

Words: 2:LETTER VII.
Words: 0:
Words: 8:_The Chateau at Versailles--Private apartments of Marie Antoinette--The
Words: 8:Secret Door--Paintings of Raphael and David--Arc de Triomphe--Beranger
Words: 2:the Poet._
Words: 0:
Words: 0:
Words: 3:                    VERSAILLES, _August 31_.
Words: 0:
Words: 13:Here I am, within ten leagues of Paris, spending the time pleasantly in
Words: 12:viewing the palace and grounds of the great Chateau of Louis XIV.
Words: 12:Fifty-seven years ago, a mob, composed of men, women, and boys, from
Words: 13:Paris, stood in front of this palace and demanded that the king should
Words: 14:go with them to the capital. I have walked over the same ground where
Words: 12:the one hundred thousand stood on that interesting occasion. I have been
Words: 12:upon the same balcony, and stood by the window from which Maria
Words: 11:Antoinette looked out upon the mob that were seeking her life.
Words: 0:
Words: 16:Anxious to see as much of the palace as I could, and having an offer of
Words: 13:the company of my young friend, Henry G. Chapman, to go through the
Words: 14:palace with me, I set out early yesterday morning, and was soon in the
Words: 13:halls that had often been trod by Royal feet. We passed through the
Words: 12:private, as well as the public, apartments, through the secret door by
Words: 13:which Marie Antoinette had escaped from the mob of 1792, and viewed the
Words: 12:room in which her faithful guards were killed, while attempting to save
Words: 13:their Royal mistress. I took my seat in one of the little parlour
Words: 13:carriages that had been used in days of yore for the Royal children;
Words: 12:while my friend, H.G. Chapman, drew me across the room. The superb
Words: 12:apartments are not now in use. Silence is written upon these walls,
Words: 13:although upon them are suspended the portraits of men of whom the world
Words: 2:has heard.
Words: 0:
Words: 10:Paintings, representing Napoleon in nearly all his battles, are here
Words: 13:seen; and wherever you see the Emperor, there you will also find Murat,
Words: 12:with his white plume waving above. Callot's painting of the battle of
Words: 12:Marengo, Hue's of the retaking of Genoa, and Bouchat's of the 18th
Words: 12:Brumaire, are of the highest order; while David has transmitted his fame
Words: 12:to posterity, by his splendid painting of the Coronation of Napoleon and
Words: 12:Josephine in Notre Dame. When I looked upon the many beautiful paintings
Words: 14:of the last named artist, that adorn the halls of Versailles, I did not
Words: 13:wonder that his fame should have saved his life, when once condemned and
Words: 12:sentenced to death during the reign of terror. The guillotine was robbed
Words: 13:of its intended victim, but the world gained a great painter. As Boswell
Words: 13:transmitted his own name to posterity with his life of Johnson, so has
Words: 11:David left his, with the magnificent paintings that are now suspended
Words: 13:upon the walls of the palaces of the Louvre, the Tuileries, St. Cloud,
Words: 6:Versailles, and even the little Elysee.
Words: 0:
Words: 13:After strolling from room to room, we found ourselves in the Salle du
Words: 13:Sacre, Diane, Salon de Mars, de Mercure, and d'Apollon. I gazed with my
Words: 15:eyes turned to the ceiling till I was dizzy. The Salon de la Guerre is
Words: 11:covered with the most beautiful representations that the mind of man
Words: 13:could conceive, or the hand accomplish. Louis XIV. is here in all his
Words: 12:glory. No Marie Antoinette will ever do the honours in these halls
Words: 1:again.
Words: 0:
Words: 13:After spending a whole day in the Palace and several mornings in the
Words: 13:Gardens, I finally bid adieu to the bronze statue of Louis XIV. that
Words: 12:stands in front of the Palace, and left Versailles, probably for ever.
Words: 0:
Words: 5:       *       *       *       *       *
Words: 0:
Words: 0:
Words: 3:                    PARIS, _September 2_.
Words: 0:
Words: 14:I am now on the point of quitting the French Metropolis. I have occupied
Words: 15:the last two days in visiting places of note in the city. I could not
Words: 13:resist the inclination to pay a second visit to the Louvre. Another hour
Words: 11:was spent in strolling through the Italian Hall and viewing the
Words: 10:master-workmanship of Raphael, the prince of painters. Time flies, even
Words: 15:in such a place as the Louvre with all its attractions; and before I had
Words: 14:seen half that I wished, a ponderous clock near by reminded me of an
Words: 11:engagement, and I reluctantly tore myself from the splendours of the
Words: 1:place.
Words: 0:
Words: 14:During the rest of the day I visited the Jardin des Plantes, and spent
Words: 13:an hour and a half pleasantly in walking among plants, flowers, and in
Words: 13:fact everything that could be found in any garden in France. From this
Words: 14:place we passed by the column of the Bastile, and paid our respects to
Words: 13:the Bourse, or Exchange, one of the most superb buildings in the city.
Words: 14:The ground floor and sides of the Bourse, are of fine marble, and the
Words: 13:names of the chief cities in the world are inscribed on the medallions,
Words: 13:which are under the upper cornice. The interior of the edifice has a
Words: 7:most splendid appearance as you enter it.
Words: 0:
Words: 15:The Cemetery of Père la Chaise was too much talked of by many of our
Words: 17:party at the Hotel for me to pass it by, so I took it after the Bourse.
Words: 13:Here lie many of the great marshals of France--the resting place of each
Words: 13:marked by the monument that stands over it, except one, which is marked
Words: 15:only by a weeping willow and a plain stone at its head. This is the
Words: 15:grave of Marshal Ney. I should not have known that it was his, but some
Words: 12:unknown hand had written with black paint, "Bravest of the Brave," on
Words: 13:the unlettered stone that stands at the head of the man who followed
Words: 12:Napoleon through nearly all his battles, and who was shot after the
Words: 13:occupation of Paris by the allied army. Peace to his ashes. During my
Words: 12:ramble through this noted place, I saw several who were hanging fresh
Words: 10:wreaths of everlasting flowers on the tombs of the departed.
Words: 0:
Words: 13:A ride in an omnibus down the Boulevards, and away up the Champs
Words: 13:Elysees, brought me to the Arc de Triomphe; and after ascending a flight
Words: 12:of one hundred and sixty-one steps, I was overlooking the city of
Words: 10:statuary. This stupendous monument was commenced by Napoleon in 1806;
Words: 14:and in 1811 it had only reached the cornice of the base, where it
Words: 13:stopped, and it was left for Louis Philippe to finish. The first stone
Words: 14:of this monument was laid on the 15th of August, 1806, the birth-day of
Words: 13:the man whose battles it was intended to commemorate. A model of the
Words: 13:arch was erected for Napoleon to pass through as he was entering the
Words: 11:city with Maria Louisa, after their marriage. The inscriptions on the
Words: 12:monument are many, and the different scenes here represented are all of
Words: 11:the most exquisite workmanship. The genius of War is summoning the
Words: 11:obedient nations to battle. Victory is here crowning Napoleon after his
Words: 12:great success in 1810. Fame stands here recording the exploits of the
Words: 12:warrior, while conquered cities lie beneath the whole. But it would take
Words: 14:more time than I have at command to give anything like a description of
Words: 5:this magnificent piece of architecture.
Words: 0:
Words: 12:That which seems to take most with Peace Friends, is the portion
Words: 12:representing an old man taming a bull for agricultural labour; while a
Words: 12:young warrior is sheathing his sword, a mother and children sitting at
Words: 10:his feet, and Minerva crowned with laurels, stands shedding her
Words: 11:protecting influence over them. The erection of this regal monument is
Words: 13:wonderful, to hand down to posterity the triumphs of the man whom we
Words: 14:first hear of as a student in the military school at Brienne, whom in
Words: 14:1784 we see in the Ecole Militaire, founded by Louis XV. in 1751; whom
Words: 17:again we find at No. 5, Quai de Court, near Rue de Mail; and in 1794 as
Words: 16:a lodger at No. 19, Rue de la Michandère. From this he goes to the Hotel
Words: 12:Mirabeau, Rue du Dauphin, where he resided when he defeated his enemies
Words: 12:on the 13th Vendimaire. The Hotel de la Colonade, Rue Neuve des
Words: 12:Capuchins is his next residence, and where he was married to Josephine.
Words: 12:From this hotel he removed to his wife's dwelling in the Rue
Words: 12:Chanteriene, No. 52. In 1796 the young general started for Italy, where
Words: 12:his conquests paved the way for the ever memorable 18th Brumaire, that
Words: 13:made him dictator of France. Napoleon was too great now to be satisfied
Words: 13:with private dwellings, and we next trace him to the Elysee, St. Cloud,
Words: 9:Versailles, the Tuileries, Fontainbleau, and finally, came his decline,
Words: 7:which I need not relate to you.
Words: 0:
Words: 11:After visiting the Gobelins, passing through its many rooms, seeing here
Words: 13:and there a half-finished piece of tapestry; and meeting a number of the
Words: 12:members of the late Peace Congress, who, like myself had remained behind
Words: 13:to see more of the beauties of the French capital than could be
Words: 11:overtaken during the Convention week. I accepted an invitation to dine
Words: 13:with a German gentleman at the Palais Royal, and was soon revelling amid
Words: 15:the luxuries of the table. I was glad that I had gone to the Palais
Words: 14:Royal, for here I had the honour of an introduction to M. Beranger, the
Words: 13:poet; and although I had to converse with him through an interpreter, I
Words: 13:enjoyed his company very much. "The people's poet," as he is called, is
Words: 14:apparently about seventy years of age, bald on the top of the head, and
Words: 12:rather corpulent, but of active look, and in the enjoyment of good
Words: 13:health. Few writers in France have done better service to the cause of
Words: 12:political and religious freedom, than Pierre Jean de Beranger. He is the
Words: 10:dauntless friend and advocate of the down-trodden poor and oppressed,
Words: 12:and has often incurred the displeasure of the Government by the arrows
Words: 14:that he has thrown into their camp. He felt what he wrote; it came
Words: 13:straight from his heart, and went directly to the hearts of the people.
Words: 11:He expressed himself strongly opposed to slavery, and said, "I don't
Words: 12:see how the Americans can reconcile slavery with their professed love of
Words: 11:freedom." Dinner out of the way, a walk through the different
Words: 14:apartments, and a stroll over the court, and I bade adieu to the Palais
Words: 13:Royal, satisfied that I should partake of many worse dinners than I had
Words: 5:helped to devour that day.
Words: 0:
Words: 12:Few nations are more courteous than the French. Here the stranger, let
Words: 14:him come from what country he may, and be ever so unacquainted with the
Words: 15:people and language, he is sure of a civil reply to any question that he
Words: 13:may ask. With the exception of the egregious blunder I have mentioned of
Words: 15:the cabman driving me to the Elysee, I was not laughed at once while in
Words: 1:France.

Words: 3:                    LONDON, _Sept. 8th_.
Words: 0:
Words: 14:The sun had just appeared from behind a cloud and was setting, and its
Words: 12:reflection upon the domes and spires of the great buildings in Paris
Words: 11:made everything appear lovely and sublime, as the train, with almost
Words: 11:lightning speed, was bringing me from the French metropolis. I gazed
Words: 14:with eager eyes to catch a farewell glance of the tops of the regal
Words: 14:palaces through which I had passed, during a stay of fifteen days in the
Words: 2:French capital.
Words: 0:
Words: 13:A pleasant ride of four hours brought us to Boulogne, where we rested
Words: 15:for the night. The next morning I was up at an early hour, and out
Words: 11:viewing the town. Boulogne could present but little attraction, after a
Words: 14:fortnight spent in seeing the lions of Paris. A return to the hotel, and
Words: 12:breakfast over, we stepped on board the steamer, and were soon crossing
Words: 13:the channel. Two hours more, and I was safely seated in a railway
Words: 11:carriage, _en route_ to the English metropolis. We reached London at
Words: 11:mid-day, where I was soon comfortably lodged at 22, Cecil Street,
Words: 11:Strand. As the London lodging-houses seldom furnish dinners, I lost no
Words: 12:time in seeking out a dining-saloon, which I had no difficulty in
Words: 14:finding in the Strand. It being the first house of the kind I had
Words: 14:entered in London, I was not a little annoyed at the politeness of the
Words: 13:waiter. The first salutation I had, after seating myself in one of the
Words: 12:stalls, was, "Ox tail, Sir; gravy soup; carrot soup, Sir; roast beef;
Words: 12:roast pork; boiled beef; roast lamb; boiled leg of mutton, Sir, with
Words: 12:caper sauce; jugged hare, Sir; boiled knuckle of veal and bacon; roast
Words: 10:turkey and oyster sauce; sucking pig, Sir; curried chicken; harrico
Words: 12:mutton, Sir." These, and many other dishes which I have forgotten, were
Words: 14:called over with a rapidity that would have done credit to one of our
Words: 14:Yankee pedlars, in crying his wares in a New England village. I was so
Words: 13:completely taken by surprise, that I asked for a "bill of fare," and
Words: 14:told him to leave me. No city in the world furnishes a cheaper, better,
Words: 11:and quicker meal for the weary traveller, than a London eating-house.
Words: 0:
Words: 5:       *       *       *       *       *
Words: 0:
Words: 11:After spending a day in looking about through this great thoroughfare,

Words: 16:in; but met me at the door himself, and gave me a hearty shake of the

Words: 14:course in Great Britain; and how I could best serve the cause of my

Words: 13:the negroes of the West Indies, and the wronged millions of India, are

Words: 11:comment from me. With the single exception of the illustrious Garrison,
Words: 12:no individual is more loved and honoured by the coloured people of
Words: 7:America, and their friends than Mr. Thompson.
Words: 0:
Words: 13:A few days after my arrival in London, I received an invitation from

Words: 0:

Words: 5:      Reminds us of your shame.

Words: 13:forming a museum that would be considered a sight in the Western States
Words: 2:of America.
Words: 0:
Words: 14:The morning after my arrival at Hartwell I was up at an early hour--in
Words: 10:fact, before any of the servants--wandering about through the vast
Words: 13:halls, and trying to find my way out, in which I eventually succeeded,

Words: 13:listening to the sweet voices of the birds that were fluttering in the
Words: 13:tops of the trees, and trimming their wings for a morning flight. The

Words: 11:beautiful little pond of water, where the gold fish were flouncing
Words: 12:about, and the gentle ripples glittering in the sunshine looked like so
Words: 7:many silver minnows playing on the surface.
Words: 0:
Words: 11:While strolling about with pleasure, and only regretting that my dear
Words: 13:daughters were not with me to enjoy the morning's walk, I saw the
Words: 14:gardener on his way to the garden. I followed him, and was soon feasting

Words: 13:garden. I have seldom seen a more splendid variety of fruits and flowers
Words: 12:in the southern States of America, than I saw at Hartwell House.
Words: 0:
Words: 13:I should, however, state that I was not the only guest at Hartwell
Words: 12:during my stay. Dr. Lee had invited several others of the American
Words: 12:delegation to the Peace Congress, and two or three of the French
Words: 12:delegates who were on a visit to England, were enjoying the Doctor's

Words: 13:the cause of universal freedom. Every year he treats his tenantry to a
Words: 13:dinner, and I need not add that these are always conducted on the
Words: 4:principle of total abstinence.
Words: 0:
Words: 13:During the second day we visited several of the cottages of the work
Words: 14:people, and in these I took no little interest. The people of the United
Words: 12:States know nothing of the real condition of the labouring classes of
Words: 12:England. The peasants of Great Britain are always spoken of as belonging
Words: 14:to the soil. I was taught in America that the English labourer was no
Words: 13:better off than the slave upon a Carolina rice-field. I had seen the
Words: 11:slaves in Missouri huddled together, three, four, and even five families
Words: 16:in a single room not more than 15 by 25 feet square, and I expected to
Words: 13:see the same in England. But in this I was disappointed. After visiting
Words: 15:a new house that the Doctor was building, he took us into one of the
Words: 12:cottages that stood near the road, and gave us an opportunity, of
Words: 13:seeing, for the first time, an English peasant's cot. We entered a low
Words: 12:whitewashed room, with a stone floor that showed an admirable degree of

Words: 14:the baker's boy, lay upon an oaken table which had been much worn away
Words: 13:with the scrubbing brush; while just above lay the old family bible that
Words: 12:had been handed down from father to son, until its possession was

Words: 11:fastidious taste could wish to see. No occupant was present, and
Words: 11:therefore I inspected everything with a greater degree of freedom. In

Words: 14:of flowers, cheated out of its share of sunshine by the tall holly that
Words: 15:had been planted near it. As I looked upon the home of the labourer, my
Words: 11:thoughts were with my enslaved countrymen. What a difference, thought I,
Words: 13:there is between the tillers of the soil in England and America. There
Words: 12:could not be a more complete refutation of the assertion that the
Words: 12:English labourer is no better off than the American slave, than the
Words: 14:scenes that were then before me. I called the attention of one of my
Words: 14:American friends to a beautiful rose near the door of the cot, and said
Words: 13:to him, "The law that will protect that flower will also guard and
Words: 15:protect the hand that planted it." He knew that I had drank deep of the
Words: 15:cup of slavery, was aware of what I meant, and merely nodded his head in
Words: 10:reply. I never experienced hospitality more genuine, and yet more
Words: 12:unpretending, than was meted out to me while at Hartwell. And the
Words: 10:favourable impression made on my own mind, of the distinguished
Words: 12:proprietor of Hartwell Park, was nearly as indelible as my humble name
Words: 13:that the Doctor had engraven in a brick, in the vault beneath the
Words: 4:Observatory in Hartwell House.
Words: 0:
Words: 15:On my return to London I accepted an invitation to join a party on a
Words: 12:visit to Windsor Castle; and taking the train at the Waterloo Bridge

Words: 12:Here James the First of Scotland was detained a prisoner for eighteen
Words: 12:years. I viewed the window through which the young prince had often
Words: 12:looked to catch a glimpse of the young and beautiful Lady Jane,
Words: 11:daughter of the Earl of Somerset, with whom he was enamoured.
Words: 0:
Words: 15:From the top of the Round Tower I had a fine view of the surrounding
Words: 12:country. Stoke Park, once the residence of that great friend of humanity
Words: 12:and civilization, William Penn, was among the scenes that I viewed with

Words: 2:Henry Labouchere.
Words: 0:
Words: 13:The castle, standing as it does on an eminence, and surrounded by a

Words: 14:Having met with John Morland, Esq., of Heath Lodge, at Paris, he gave me

Words: 14:The same party with whom I visited Windsor being supplied with a card of
Words: 14:admission to the Bank of England, I accepted an invitation to be one of
Words: 12:the company. We entered the vast building at a little past twelve

Words: 12:nearly all my countrymen who visit London pay their respects to this
