Words: 13:surpassed my highest idea of a bank. But a stroll through this monster
Words: 13:building of gold and silver brought to my mind an incident that occurred
Words: 9:to me a year after my escape from slavery.
Words: 0:
Words: 13:In the autumn of 1835, having been cheated out of the previous summer's
Words: 13:earnings, by the captain of the steamer in which I had been employed
Words: 14:running away with the money, I was, like the rest of the men, left
Words: 12:without any means of support during the winter, and therefore had to
Words: 12:seek employment in the neighbouring towns. I went to the town of
Words: 12:Monroe, in the state of Michigan, and while going through the principal
Words: 14:streets looking for work, I passed the door of the only barber in the
Words: 12:town, whose shop appeared to be filled with persons waiting to be
Words: 15:shaved. As there was but one man at work, and as I had, while employed
Words: 11:in the steamer, occasionally shaved a gentleman who could not perform
Words: 13:that office himself, it occurred to me that I might get employment here
Words: 11:as a journeyman barber. I therefore made immediate application for work,
Words: 18:but the barber told me he did not need a hand. But I was not to be put
Words: 13:off so easily, and after making several offers to work cheap, I frankly
Words: 17:told him, that if he would not employ me I would get a room near to him,
Words: 11:and set up an opposition establishment. This threat, however, made no
Words: 15:impression on the barber; and as I was leaving, one of the men who were
Words: 14:waiting to be shaved said, "If you want a room in which to commence
Words: 13:business, I have one on the opposite side of the street." This man
Words: 14:followed me out; we went over, and I looked at the room. He strongly
Words: 14:urged me to set up, at the same time promising to give me his
Words: 13:influence. I took the room, purchased an old table, two chairs, got a
Words: 14:pole with a red stripe painted around it, and the next day opened, with
Words: 11:a sign over the door, "Fashionable Hair-dresser from New York, Emperor
Words: 15:of the West." I need not add that my enterprise was very annoying to the
Words: 12:"shop over the way"--especially my sign, which happened to be the most
Words: 15:expensive part of the concern. Of course, I had to tell all who came in
Words: 13:that my neighbour on the opposite side did not keep clean towels, that
Words: 15:his razors were dull, and, above all, he had never been to New York to
Words: 14:see the fashions. Neither had I. In a few weeks I had the entire
Words: 12:business of the town, to the great discomfiture of the other barber.
Words: 0:
Words: 13:At this time, money matters in the Western States were in a sad
Words: 12:condition. Any person who could raise a small amount of money was
Words: 13:permitted to establish a bank, and allowed to issue notes for four times
Words: 12:the sum raised. This being the case, many persons borrowed money merely
Words: 12:long enough to exhibit to the bank inspectors, and the borrowed money
Words: 13:was returned, and the bank left without a dollar in its vaults, if,
Words: 13:indeed, it had a vault about its premises. The result was, that banks
Words: 12:were started all over the Western States, and the country flooded with
Words: 12:worthless paper. These were known as the "Wild Cat Banks." Silver coin
Words: 14:being very scarce, and the banks not being allowed to issue notes for a
Words: 13:smaller amount than one dollar, several persons put out notes from 6 to
Words: 11:75 cents in value; these were called "Shinplasters." The Shinplaster was
Words: 14:in the shape of a promissory note, made payable on demand. I have often
Words: 13:seen persons with large rolls of these bills, the whole not amounting to
Words: 13:more than five dollars. Some weeks after I had commenced business on my
Words: 12:"own hook," I was one evening very much crowded with customers; and
Words: 15:while they were talking over the events of the day, one of them said to
Words: 14:me, "Emperor, you seem to be doing a thriving business. You should do as
Words: 12:other business men, issue your Shinplasters." This, of course, as it was
Words: 13:intended, created a laugh; but with me it was no laughing matter, for
Words: 13:from that moment I began to think seriously of becoming a banker. I
Words: 14:accordingly went a few days after to a printer, and he, wishing to get
Words: 15:the job of printing, urged me to put out my notes, and showed me some
Words: 12:specimens of engravings that he had just received from Detroit. My head
Words: 13:being already filled with the idea of a bank, I needed but little
Words: 12:persuasion to set the thing finally afloat. Before I left the printer
Words: 14:the notes were partly in type, and I studying how I should keep the
Words: 10:public from counterfeiting them. The next day my Shinplasters were
Words: 12:handed to me, the whole amount being twenty dollars, and after being
Words: 13:duly signed were ready for circulation. At first my notes did not take
Words: 13:well; they were too new, and viewed with a suspicious eye. But through
Words: 14:the assistance of my customers, and a good deal of exertion on my own
Words: 12:part, my bills were soon in circulation; and nearly all the money
Words: 13:received in return for my notes was spent in fitting up and decorating
Words: 2:my shop.
Words: 0:
Words: 12:Few bankers get through this world without their difficulties, and I was
Words: 15:not to be an exception. A short time after my money had been out, a
Words: 14:party of young men, either wishing to pull down my vanity, or to try
Words: 12:the soundness of my bank, determined to give it "a run." After
Words: 14:collecting together a number of my bills, they came one at a time to
Words: 14:demand other money for them, and I, not being aware of what was going
Words: 14:on, was taken by surprise. One day as I was sitting at my table,
Words: 13:strapping some new razors I had just got with the avails of my
Words: 11:"Shinplasters," one of the men entered and said, "Emperor, you will
Words: 14:oblige me if you will give me some other money for these notes of
Words: 12:yours." I immediately cashed the notes with the most worthless of the
Words: 15:Wild Cat money that I had on hand, but which was a lawful tender. The
Words: 12:young man had scarcely left when a second appeared with a similar
Words: 12:amount, and demanded payment. These were cashed, and soon a third came
Words: 15:with his roll of notes. I paid these with an air of triumph, although I
Words: 15:had but half a dollar left. I began now to think seriously what I should
Words: 14:do, or how to act, provided another demand should be made. While I was
Words: 13:thus engaged in thought, I saw the fourth man crossing the street, with
Words: 10:a handful of notes, evidently my "Shinplasters." I instantaneously shut
Words: 13:the door, and looking out of the window, said, "I have closed business
Words: 14:for the day: come to-morrow and I will see you." In looking across the
Words: 12:street, I saw my rival standing in his shop-door, grinning and clapping
Words: 12:his hands at my apparent downfall. I was completely "done _Brown_" for
Words: 17:the day. However, I was not to be "used up" in this way; so I escaped by
Words: 14:the back door, and went in search of my friend who had first suggested
Words: 15:to me the idea of issuing notes. I found him, told him of the difficulty
Words: 16:I was in, and wished him to point out a way by which I might extricate
Words: 13:myself. He laughed heartily, and then said, "You must act as all bankers
Words: 15:do in this part of the country." I inquired how they did, and he said,
Words: 14:"When your notes are brought to you, you must redeem them, and then send
Words: 14:them out and get other money for them; and, with the latter, you can
Words: 14:keep cashing your own Shinplasters." This was indeed a new job to me. I
Words: 11:immediately commenced putting in circulation the notes which I had just
Words: 12:redeemed, and my efforts were crowned with so much success, that before
Words: 12:I slept that night my "Shinplasters" were again in circulation, and my
Words: 7:bank once more on a sound basis.
Words: 0:
Words: 14:As I saw the clerks shovelling out the yellow coin upon the counters of
Words: 14:the Bank of England, and men coming in and going out with weighty bags
Words: 14:of the precious metal in their hands, or on their shoulders, I could not
Words: 11:but think of the great contrast between the monster Institution, within
Words: 13:whose walls I was then standing, and the Wild Cat Banks of America!

Words: 3:                    LONDON, _Sept. 24_.
Words: 0:
Words: 12:I have devoted the past ten days to sight-seeing in the Metropolis--the
Words: 13:first two of which were spent in the British Museum. After procuring a
Words: 14:guide-book at the door as I entered, I seated myself on the first seat
Words: 15:that caught my eye, arranged as well as I could in my mind the different
Words: 13:rooms, and then commenced in good earnest. The first part I visited was
Words: 11:the Gallery of Antiquities, through to the north gallery, and thence
Words: 11:to the Lycian Room. This place is filled with tombs, bas-reliefs,
Words: 11:statues, and other productions of the same art. Venus, seated, and
Words: 13:smelling a lotus flower which she held in her hand, and attended by
Words: 14:three graces, put a stop to the rapid strides that I was making through
Words: 13:this part of the hall. This is really one of the most precious
Words: 14:productions of the art that I have ever seen. Many of the figures in
Words: 13:this room are very much mutilated, yet one can linger here for hours
Words: 13:with interest. A good number of the statues are of uncertain date; they
Words: 15:are of great value as works of art, and more so as a means of
Words: 11:enlightening much that has been obscure with respect to Lycia, an
Words: 7:ancient and celebrated country of Asia Minor.
Words: 0:
Words: 11:In passing through the eastern Zoological Gallery, I was surrounded on
Words: 13:every side by an army of portraits suspended upon the walls; and among
Words: 12:these was the Protector. The people of one century kicks his bones
Words: 12:through the streets of London, another puts his portrait in the British
Words: 12:Museum, and a future generation may possibly give him a place in
Words: 10:Westminster Abbey. Such is the uncertainty of the human character.
Words: 9:Yesterday, a common soldier--to-day, the ruler of an empire--to-morrow,
Words: 13:suspended upon the gallows. In an adjoining room I saw a portrait of
Words: 12:Baxter, which gives one a pretty good idea of the great Nonconformist.
Words: 12:In the same room hung a splendid modern portrait, without any intimation
Words: 14:in the guide-book of who it represented, or when it was painted. It was
Words: 15:so much like one whom I had seen, and on whom my affections were placed
Words: 14:in my younger days, that I obtained a seat from an adjoining room and
Words: 13:rested myself before it. After sitting half an hour or more, I wandered
Words: 14:to another part of the building, but only to return again to my "first
Words: 11:love," where I remained till the throng had disappeared one after
Words: 12:another, and the officer reminding me that it was time to close.
Words: 0:
Words: 12:It was eight o'clock before I reached my lodgings. Although fatigued by
Words: 12:the day's exertions, I again resumed the reading of Roscoe's "Leo X.,"
Words: 11:and had nearly finished seventy-three pages, when the clock on St.
Words: 13:Martin's Church apprised me that it was two. He who escapes from slavery
Words: 14:at the age of twenty years, without any education, as did the writer of
Words: 14:this letter, must read when others are asleep, if he would catch up with
Words: 15:the rest of the world. "To be wise," says Pope, "is but to know how
Words: 12:little can be known." The true searcher after truth and knowledge is
Words: 12:always like a child; although gaining strength from year to year, he
Words: 13:still "learns to labour and to wait." The field of labour is ever
Words: 12:expanding before him, reminding him that he has yet more to learn;
Words: 13:teaching him that he is nothing more than a child in knowledge, and
Words: 12:inviting him onward with a thousand varied charms. The son may take
Words: 12:possession of the father's goods at his death, but he cannot inherit
Words: 12:with the property the father's cultivated mind. He may put on the
Words: 13:father's old coat, but that is all: the immortal mind of the first
Words: 6:wearer has gone to the tomb.
Words: 0:
Words: 11:Property may be bequeathed, but knowledge cannot. Then let him who
Words: 14:would be useful in his day and generation be up and doing. Like the
Words: 11:Chinese student who learned perseverance from the woman whom he saw
Words: 14:trying to rub a crow-bar into a needle, so should we take the experience
Words: 13:of the past to lighten our feet through the paths of the future.
Words: 0:
Words: 15:The next morning at ten, I was again at the door of the great building;
Words: 13:was soon within its walls seeing what time would not allow of the
Words: 12:previous day. I spent some hours in looking through glass cases, viewing
Words: 14:specimens of minerals, such as can scarcely be found in any place out of
Words: 14:the British Museum. During this day I did not fail to visit the great
Words: 12:Library. It is a spacious room, surrounded with large glass cases filled
Words: 13:with volumes, whose very look tells you that they are of age. Around,
Words: 10:under the cornice, were arranged a number of old black-looking
Words: 13:portraits, in all probability the authors of some of the works in the
Words: 12:glass cases beneath. About the room were placed long tables, with stands
Words: 13:for reading and writing, and around these were a number of men busily
Words: 12:engaged in looking over some chosen author. Old men with grey hairs,
Words: 10:young men with mustaches--some in cloth, others in fustian, indicating
Words: 14:that men of different rank can meet here. Not a single word was spoken
Words: 11:during my stay, all appearing to enjoy the silence that reigned
Words: 13:throughout the great room. This is indeed a retreat from the world. No
Words: 15:one inquires who the man is who is at his side, and each pursues in
Words: 13:silence his own researches. The racing of pens over the sheets of paper
Words: 9:was all that disturbed the stillness of the occasion.
Words: 0:
Words: 14:From the Library I strolled to other rooms, and feasted my eyes on what
Words: 13:I had never before seen. He who goes over this immense building, cannot
Words: 13:do so without a feeling of admiration for the men whose energy has
Words: 11:brought together this vast and wonderful collection of things, the like
Words: 13:of which cannot be found in any other museum in the world. The
Words: 14:reflection of the setting sun against a mirror in one of the rooms, told
Words: 15:me that night was approaching, and I had but a moment in which to take
Words: 14:another look at the portrait that I had seen the previous day, and then
Words: 5:bade adieu to the Museum.
Words: 0:
Words: 12:Having published the narrative of my life and escape from slavery, and
Words: 12:put it into the booksellers' hands--and seeing a prospect of a fair
Words: 15:sale, I ventured to take from my purse the last sovereign to make up a
Words: 14:small sum to remit to the United States, for the support of my daughter,
Words: 12:who is at school there. Before doing this, however, I had made
Words: 12:arrangements to attend a public meeting in the city of Worcester, at
Words: 13:which the mayor was to preside. Being informed by the friends of the
Words: 13:slave there, that I would, in all probability, sell a number of copies
Words: 13:of my book, and being told that Worcester was only ten miles from
Words: 13:London, I felt safe in parting with all but a few shillings, feeling
Words: 14:sure that my purse would soon be again replenished. But you may guess my
Words: 12:surprise when I learned that Worcester was above a hundred miles from
Words: 13:London, and that I had not retained money enough to defray my expenses
Words: 17:to the place. In my haste and wish to make up the ten pounds to send to
Words: 13:my children, I had forgotten that the payment for my lodgings would be
Words: 12:demanded before I should leave town. Saturday morning came; I paid my
Words: 12:lodging bill, and had three shillings and fourpence left; and out of
Words: 15:this sum I was to get three dinners, as I was only served with breakfast
Words: 13:and tea at my lodgings. Nowhere in the British empire do the people
Words: 15:witness as dark days as in London. It was on Monday morning, in the fore
Words: 13:part of October, as the clock on St. Martin's Church was striking ten,

Words: 15:were yet burning, and the shops were all lighted as if day had not made
Words: 12:its appearance. This great thoroughfare, as usual at this time of the
Words: 11:day, was thronged with business men going their way, and women

Words: 12:Turning, I retraced my steps, and was soon passing through the massive
Words: 16:gates of Temple Bar, wending my way to the city, when a beggar boy at my

Words: 13:was an American. He eyed me attentively as I passed him, and seemed

Words: 11:to speak with him, I returned, and commencing conversation with him,

Words: 12:escaped from slavery in Maryland, and reached New York; but not feeling
Words: 13:himself secure there, he had, through the kindness of the captain of an
Words: 13:English ship, made his way to Liverpool; and not being able to get
Words: 14:employment there, he had come up to London. Here he had met with no
Words: 12:better success; and having been employed in the growing of tobacco, and
Words: 13:being unaccustomed to any other work, he could not get to labour in
Words: 16:England. I told him he had better try to get to the West Indies; but he
Words: 15:informed me that he had not a single penny, and that he had nothing to
Words: 16:eat that day. By this man's story, I was moved to tears; and going to a
Words: 12:neighbouring shop, I took from my purse my last shilling, changed it,
Words: 12:and gave this poor brother fugitive one-half. The poor man burst into
Words: 14:tears as I placed the sixpence in his hand, and said--"You are the first
Words: 15:friend I have met in London." I bade him farewell, and left him with a
Words: 15:feeling of regret that I could not place him beyond the reach of want. I
Words: 14:went on my way to the city, and while going through Cheapside, a streak
Words: 15:of light appeared in the east that reminded me that it was not night. In

Words: 14:some one who would lend me money enough to get to Worcester. Hungry and
Words: 13:fatigued I was returning to my lodgings, when the great clock of St

Words: 13:stroll through Fleet Street and the Strand, and I was again pacing my
Words: 14:room. On my return, I found a letter from Worcester had arrived in my

Words: 13:day on my reaching that place; and saying, "Bring plenty of books, as
Words: 12:you will doubtless sell a large number." The last sixpence had been
Words: 13:spent for postage stamps, in order to send off some letters to other
Words: 15:places, and I could not even stamp a letter in answer to the one last

Words: 14:"This is for the slaves." I was three thousand miles from home, with but
Words: 14:a single farthing in my pocket! Where on earth is a man without money

Words: 13:whom I could apply for temporary aid. My friend, Mr. Thompson, was out

Words: 11:previous; and this young gentleman, with more than ordinary zeal and
Words: 12:enthusiasm for the cause of bleeding humanity, and respect for me, had
Words: 13:gone amongst his father's congregation and sold a number of copies of my
Words: 15:book, and had come to bring me the money. I wiped the silent tear from

Words: 14:did not let him know under what obligation I was to him for this

Words: 16:has rendered to a stranger in a strange land, and I feel that I am but
Words: 12:discharging in a trifling degree, my debt of gratitude to this young
Words: 12:gentleman, in acknowledging my obligation to him. As the man who called
Words: 13:for bread and cheese, when feeling in his pocket for the last threepence

Words: 13:countermanded the order for the lunch, and bade them bring him the best
Words: 14:dinner they could get; so I told the servant when she brought the tea,
Words: 16:that I had changed my mind, and should go out to dine. With the means in
Words: 14:my pocket of reaching Worcester the next day, I sat down to dinner at
Words: 14:the Adelphi with a good cut of roast beef before me, and felt myself
Words: 11:once more at home. Thus ended a dark day in London.

Words: 3:                    LONDON, _October 10_.
Words: 0:
Words: 14:For some days past, Sol has not shown his face, clouds have obscured the
Words: 13:sky, and the rain has fallen in torrents, which has contributed much to
Words: 13:the general gloom. However, I have spent the time in as agreeable a
Words: 13:manner as I well could. Yesterday I fulfilled an engagement to dine with
Words: 12:a gentleman at the Whittington Club. One who is unacquainted with the
Words: 11:Club system as carried on in London, can scarcely imagine the
Words: 12:conveniences they present. Every member appears to be at home, and all
Words: 15:seem to own a share in the Club. There is a free-and-easy way with those
Words: 13:who frequent Clubs, and a licence given there that is unknown in the
Words: 13:drawing-room of the private mansion. I met the gentleman at the Club, at
Words: 12:the appointed hour, and after his writing my name in the visitors'
Words: 12:book, we proceeded to the dining-room, where we partook of a good
Words: 1:dinner.
Words: 0:
Words: 16:We had been in the room but a short time, when a small man, dressed in
Words: 13:black, with his coat buttoned up to the chin, entered the saloon, and
Words: 16:took a seat at the table hard by. My friend in a low whisper informed me
Words: 13:that this person was one of the French refugees. He was apparently not
Words: 11:more than thirty years of age, and exceedingly good looking--his person
Words: 12:being slight, his feet and hands very small and well shaped, especially
Words: 13:his hands, which were covered with kid gloves, so tightly drawn on, that
Words: 13:the points of the finger nails were visible through them. His face was
Words: 14:mild and almost womanly in its beauty, his eyes soft and full, his brow
Words: 12:open and ample, his features well defined, and approaching to the ideal
Words: 12:Greek in contour; the lines about his mouth were exquisitely sweet, and
Words: 11:yet resolute in expression; his hair was short--his having no mustaches

Words: 12:surprised when informed that the person before me was Louis Blanc. I
Words: 12:could scarcely be persuaded to believe that one so small, so child-like
Words: 13:in stature, had taken a prominent part in the Revolution of 1848. He
Words: 16:held in his hand a copy of _La Presse_, and as soon as he was seated,
Words: 13:opened it and began to devour its contents. The gentleman with whom I
Words: 14:was dining was not acquainted with him, but at the close of our dinner
Words: 8:he procured me an introduction through another gentleman.
Words: 0:
Words: 13:As we were returning to our lodgings, we saw in Exeter Street, Strand,
Words: 14:one of those exhibitions that can be seen in almost any of the streets
Words: 14:in the suburbs of the Metropolis, but which is something of a novelty to
Words: 13:those from the other side of the Atlantic. This was an exhibition of
Words: 12:"Punch and Judy." Everything was in full operation when we reached the
Words: 13:spot. A puppet appeared eight or ten inches from the waist upwards, with
Words: 10:an enormous face, huge nose, mouth widely grinning, projecting chin,
Words: 11:cheeks covered with grog blossoms, a large protuberance on his back,
Words: 11:another on his chest; yet with these deformities he appeared uncommonly
Words: 13:happy. This was Mr. Punch. He held in his right hand a tremendous
Words: 13:bludgeon, with which he amused himself by rapping on the head every one
Words: 12:who came within his reach. This exhibition seems very absurd, yet not
Words: 11:less than one hundred were present--children, boys, old men, and even
Words: 10:gentlemen and ladies, were standing by, and occasionally greeting the
Words: 12:performer with the smile of approbation. Mr. Punch, however, was not to
Words: 13:have it all his own way, for another and better sort of Punch-like
Words: 11:exhibition appeared a few yards off, that took away Mr. Punch's
Words: 11:audience, to the great dissatisfaction of that gentleman. This was an
Words: 12:exhibition called the Fantoccini, and far superior to any of the street
Words: 12:performances which I have yet seen. The curtain rose and displayed a
Words: 10:beautiful theatre in miniature, and most gorgeously painted. The organ
Words: 13:which accompanied it struck up a hornpipe, and a sailor, dressed in his
Words: 11:blue jacket, made his appearance and commenced keeping time with the
Words: 13:utmost correctness. This figure was not so long as Mr. Punch, but much
Words: 13:better looking. At the close of the hornpipe the little sailor made a
Words: 10:bow, and tripped off, apparently conscious of having deserved the
Words: 11:undivided applause of the bystanders. The curtain dropped; but in two
Words: 14:or three minutes it was again up, and a rope was discovered, extended on
Words: 14:two cross pieces, for dancing upon. The tune was changed to an air, in
Words: 12:which the time was marked, a graceful figure appeared, jumped upon the
Words: 12:rope with its balance pole, and displayed all the manoeuvres of an
Words: 13:expert performer on the tight rope. Many who would turn away in disgust
Words: 14:from Mr. Punch, will stand for hours and look at the performances of the
Words: 10:Fantoccini. If people, like the Vicar of Wakefield, will sometimes
Words: 13:"allow themselves to be happy," they can hardly fail to have a hearty
Words: 12:laugh at the drolleries of the Fantoccini. There may be degrees of
Words: 13:absurdity in the manner of wasting our time, but there is an evident
Words: 10:affectation in decrying these humble and innocent exhibitions, by those
Words: 15:who will sit till two or three in the morning to witness a pantomime at
Words: 2:a theatre-royal.
Words: 0:
Words: 5:       *       *       *       *       *
Words: 0:
Words: 10:An autumn sun shone brightly through a remarkably transparent atmosphere
Words: 13:this morning, which was a most striking contrast to the weather we have
Words: 16:had during the past three days; and I again set out to see some of the
Words: 12:lions of the city, commencing with the Tower of London. Every American,
Words: 14:on returning home from a visit to the old world, speaks with pride of
Words: 14:the places he saw while in Europe; and of the many resorts of interest
Words: 14:he has read of, few have made a more lasting impression upon his memory
Words: 13:than the Tower of London. The stories of the imprisoning of kings, and
Words: 11:queens, the murdering of princes, the torturing of men and women,
Words: 12:without regard to birth, education, or station, and of the burning and
Words: 15:rebuilding of the old pile, have all sunk deep into his heart. A walk of
Words: 13:twenty minutes, after being set down at the Bank by an omnibus, brought
Words: 16:me to the gate of the Tower. A party of friends who were to meet me
Words: 13:there had not arrived, so I had an opportunity of inspecting the grounds
Words: 13:and taking a good view of the external appearance of the old and
Words: 12:celebrated building. The Tower is surrounded by a high wall, and around
Words: 12:this a deep ditch partly filled with stagnated water. The wall incloses
Words: 11:twelve acres of ground on which stand the several towers, occupying,
Words: 13:with their walks and avenues, the whole space. The most ancient part of
Words: 13:the building is called the "White Tower," so as to distinguish it from
Words: 11:the parts more recently built. Its walls are seventeen feet in
Words: 11:thickness, and ninety-two in height, exclusive of the turrets, of which
Words: 12:there are four. My company arrived, and we entered the tower through
Words: 12:four massive gates, the innermost one being pointed out as the "Water,
Words: 13:or Traitors' Gate"--so called from the fact that it opened to the river,
Words: 12:and through it the criminals were usually brought to the prison within.
Words: 13:But this passage is now closed up. We visited the various apartments in
Words: 13:the old building. The room in the Bloody Tower, where the infant princes
Words: 14:were put to death by the command of their uncle, Richard III.; also, the
Words: 12:recess behind the gate where the bones of the young princes were
Words: 13:concealed, were shown to us. The warden of the prison who showed us
Words: 13:through, seemed to have little or no veneration for Henry VIII.; for he
Words: 15:often cracked a joke, or told a story at the expense of the murderer of
Words: 15:Anne Boleyn. The old man wiped the tear from his eye, as he pointed out
Words: 15:the grave of Lady Jane Grey. This was doubtless one of the best as well
Words: 13:as most innocent of those who lost their lives in the Tower; young,
Words: 13:virtuous, and handsome, she became a victim to the ambition of her own
Words: 14:and her husband's relations. I tried to count the names on the wall in
Words: 10:"Beauchamp's Tower," but they were too numerous. Anne Boleyn was
Words: 13:imprisoned here. The room in the "Brick Tower," where Lady Jane Grey was
Words: 13:imprisoned, was pointed out as a place of interest. We were next shown
Words: 13:into the "White Tower." We passed through a long room filled with many
Words: 11:things having a warlike appearance; and among them a number of
Words: 11:equestrian figures, as large as life, and clothed in armour and
Words: 13:trappings of the various reigns from Edward I. to James II., or from
Words: 12:1272 to 1685. Elizabeth, or the "Maiden Queen," as the warden called
Words: 14:her, was the most imposing of the group; she was on a cream coloured
Words: 12:charger. We left the Maiden Queen to examine the cloak upon which
Words: 13:General Wolf died, at the storming of Quebec. In this room Sir Walter
Words: 11:Raleigh was imprisoned, and here was written his "History of the
Words: 13:World." In his own hand, upon the wall, is written, "Be thou faithful
Words: 15:unto death, and I will give thee a crown of life." His Bible is still
Words: 13:shown, with these memorable lines written in it by himself a short time
Words: 3:before his death:--
Words: 0:
Words: 8:   "Even such is Time that takes on trust,
Words: 8:    Our youth, our joy, our all we have,
Words: 8:    And pays us but with age and dust;
Words: 7:    Who in the dark and silent grave,
Words: 7:    When we have wandered all our ways,
Words: 7:    Shuts up the story of our days."
Words: 0:
Words: 0:
Words: 10:Spears, battle-axes, pikes, helmets, targets, bows and arrows, and many
Words: 13:instruments of torture, whose names I did not learn, grace the walls of
Words: 14:this room. The block on which the Earl of Essex and Anne Boleyn were
Words: 12:beheaded, was shown among other objects of interest. A view of the
Words: 13:"Queen's Jewels" closed our visit to the Tower. The Gold Staff of St.
Words: 12:Edward, and the Baptismal Font used at the Royal christenings, made of
Words: 13:solid silver, and more than four feet high, were among the jewels here
Words: 14:exhibited. The Sword of Justice was there, as if to watch the rest of
Words: 12:the valuables. However, this was not the sword that Peter used. Our
Words: 10:acquaintance with De Foe, Sir Walter Raleigh, Chaucer, and James
Words: 11:Montgomery, through their writings, and the knowledge that they had been
Words: 12:incarcerated within the walls of the bastile that we were just leaving,
Words: 13:caused us to look back again and again upon its dark grey turrets.
Words: 0:
Words: 15:I closed the day with a look at the interior of St. Paul's Cathedral. A
Words: 15:service was just over, and we met a crowd coming out as we entered the
Words: 13:great building. "Service is over, and two pence for all that wants to
Words: 13:stay," was the first sound that caught our ears. In the Burlesque of
Words: 15:"Esmeralda," a man is met in the belfry of the Notre Dame at Paris, and
Words: 10:being asked for money by one of the vergers says:--
Words: 0:
Words: 7:   "I paid three pence at the door,
Words: 9:    And since I came in a great deal more:
Words: 8:    Upon my honour you have emptied my purse,
Words: 7:    St. Paul's Cathedral could not do worse."
Words: 0:
Words: 0:
Words: 14:I felt inclined to join in this sentiment before I left the church. A
Words: 13:fine statue of "Surly Sam" Johnson was one of the first things that
Words: 12:caught our eyes on looking around. A statue of Sir Edward Packenham,
Words: 15:who fell at the Battle of New Orleans, was on the opposite side of the
Words: 14:great hall. As we had walked over the ground where this General fell, we
Words: 12:viewed his statue with more than ordinary interest. We were taken from
Words: 12:one scene of interest to another, until we found ourselves in the
Words: 12:"Whispering Gallery." From the dome we had a splendid view of the
Words: 13:Metropolis of the world. A scaffold was erected up here to enable an
Words: 13:artist to take sketches from which a panorama of London was painted. The
Words: 13:artist was three years at work. The painting is now exhibited at the
Words: 13:Colosseum; but the brain of the artist was turned, and he died insane!
Words: 12:Indeed, one can scarcely conceive how it could be otherwise. You in
Words: 12:America have no idea of the immensity of this building. Pile together
Words: 13:half-a-dozen of the largest churches in New York or Boston, and you will
Words: 9:have but a faint representation of St. Paul's Cathedral.
Words: 0:
Words: 5:       *       *       *       *       *
Words: 0:
Words: 12:I have just returned from a stroll of two hours through Westminster
Words: 12:Abbey. We entered the building at a door near Poets' Corner, and,
Words: 11:naturally enough, looked around for the monuments of the men whose
Words: 10:imaginative powers have contributed so much to instruct and amuse
Words: 15:mankind. I was not a little disappointed in the few I saw. In almost any
Words: 12:church-yard you may see monuments and tombs far superior to anything in
Words: 12:the Poets' Corner. A few only have monuments. Shakspere, who wrote of
Words: 15:man to man, and for man to the end of time, is honoured with one.
Words: 11:Addison's monument is also there; but the greater number have nothing
Words: 11:more erected to their memories than busts or medallions. Poets' Corner
Words: 11:is not splendid in appearance, yet I observed visiters lingering about
Words: 15:it, as if they were tied to the spot by love and veneration for some
Words: 12:departed friend. All seemed to regard it as classic ground. No sound
Words: 13:louder than a whisper was heard during the whole time, except the verger
Words: 12:treading over the marble floor with a light step. There is great
Words: 12:pleasure in sauntering about the tombs of those with whom we are
Words: 11:familiar through their writings; and we tear ourselves from their ashes,
Words: 14:as we would from those of a bosom friend. The genius of these men
Words: 13:spreads itself over the whole panorama of Nature, giving us one vast and
Words: 14:varied picture, the colour of which will endure to the end of time. None
Words: 14:can portray like the poet the passions of the human soul. The statue of
Words: 12:Addison, clad in his dressing-gown, is not far from that of Shakspere.
Words: 14:He looks as if he had just left the study, after finishing some chosen
Words: 14:paper for the _Spectator_. This memento of a great man, was the work of
Words: 13:the British public. Such a mark of national respect was but justice to
Words: 13:one who has contributed more to purify and raise the standard of English
Words: 14:literature, than any man of his day. We next visited the other end of
Words: 11:the same transept, near the northern door. Here lie Mansfield, Chatham,
Words: 11:Fox, the second William Pitt, Grattan, Wilberforce, and a few other
Words: 12:statesmen. But, above all, is the stately monument to the Earl of
Words: 14:Chatham. In no other place so small, do so many great men lie together.
Words: 14:To these men, whose graves strangers from all parts of the world wish to
Words: 12:view, the British public are in a great measure indebted for England's
Words: 11:fame. The high pre-eminence which England has so long enjoyed and
Words: 12:maintained in the scale of empire, has constantly been the boast and
Words: 12:pride of the English people. The warm panegyrics that have been lavished
Words: 10:on her constitution and laws--the songs chaunted to celebrate her
Words: 12:glory--the lustre of her arms, as the glowing theme of her warriors--the
Words: 11:thunder of her artillery in proclaiming her moral prowess, her flag
Words: 12:being unfurled to every breeze and ocean, rolling to her shores the
Words: 12:tribute of a thousand realms--show England to be the greatest nation in
Words: 13:the world, and speak volumes for the great departed, as well as for
Words: 12:those of the living present. One requires no company, no amusements, no
Words: 14:books in such a place as this. Time and death have placed within those
Words: 13:walls sufficient to occupy the mind, if one should stay here a week.
Words: 0:
Words: 13:On my return, I spent an hour very pleasantly in the National Academy,
Words: 13:in the same building as the National Gallery. Many of the paintings here
Words: 13:are of a fine order. Oliver Cromwell looking upon the headless corpse of
Words: 12:King Charles I., appeared to draw the greatest number of spectators. A
Words: 15:scene from "As You Like it," was one of the best executed pieces we saw.
Words: 12:This was "Rosalind, Celia, and Orlando." The artist did himself and the
Words: 11:subject great credit. Kemble, in Hamlet, with that ever memorable skull
Words: 14:in his hand, was one of the pieces which we viewed with no little
Words: 12:interest. It is strange that Hamlet is always represented as a thin,
Words: 14:lean man, when the Hamlet of Shakspere was a fat, John Bull-kind of a
Words: 13:man. But the best piece in the Gallery was "Dante meditating the episode
Words: 11:of Francesca da Rimini and Paolo Malatesta, S'Inferno, Canto V." Our
Words: 12:first interest for the great Italian poet was created by reading Lord
Words: 12:Byron's poem, "The Lament of Dante." From that hour we felt like
Words: 10:examining everything connected with the great Italian poet. The history
Words: 13:of poets, as well as painters, is written in their works. The best
Words: 14:written life of Goldsmith is to be found in his poem of "The Traveller,"
Words: 12:and his novel of "The Vicar of Wakefield." Boswell could not have
Words: 13:written a better life of himself than he has done in giving the
Words: 15:Biography of Dr. Johnson. It seems clear that no one can be a great poet
Words: 12:without having been sometime during life a lover, and having lost the
Words: 12:object of his affection in some mysterious way. Burns had his Highland
Words: 12:Mary, Byron his Mary, and Dante was not without his Beatrice. Whether
Words: 14:there ever lived such a person as Beatrice seems to be a question upon
Words: 11:which neither of his biographers have thrown much light. However, a
Words: 13:Beatrice existed in the poet's mind, if not on earth. His attachment to
Words: 12:Beatrice Portinari, and the linking of her name with the immortality of
Words: 11:his great poem, left an indelible impression upon his future character.
Words: 12:The marriage of the object of his affections to another, and her
Words: 10:subsequent death, and the poet's exile from his beloved Florence,
Words: 11:together with his death amongst strangers--all give an interest to the
Words: 11:poet's writings, which could not be heightened by romance itself. When
Words: 13:exiled and in poverty, Dante found a friend in the father of Francesca.
Words: 14:And here, under the roof of his protector, he wrote his great poem. The
Words: 13:time the painter has chosen is evening. Day and night meet in mid-air:
Words: 13:one star is alone visible. Sailing in vacancy are the shadows of the
Words: 10:lovers. The countenance of Francesca is expressive of hopeless agony.
Words: 11:The delineations are sublime, the conception is of the highest order,
Words: 13:and the execution admirable. Dante is seated in a marble vestibule, in a
Words: 11:meditating attitude, the face partly concealed by the right hand upon
Words: 13:which it is resting. On the whole, it is an excellently painted piece,
Words: 13:and causes one to go back with a fresh relish to the Italian's
Words: 13:celebrated poem. In coming out, we stopped a short while in the upper
Words: 12:room of the Gallery, and spent a few minutes over a painting
Words: 11:representing Mrs. Siddons in one of Shakspere's characters. This is by
Words: 14:Sir Joshua Reynolds, and is only one of the many pieces that we have
Words: 13:seen of this great artist. His genius was vast, and powerful in its
Words: 11:grasp. His fancy fertile, and his inventive faculty inexhaustible in its
Words: 11:resources. He displayed the very highest powers of genius by the
Words: 11:thorough originality of his conceptions, and by the entirely new path
Words: 15:that he struck out in art. Well may Englishmen be proud of his name. And
Words: 14:as time shall step between his day and those that follow after him, the
Words: 12:more will his works be appreciated. We have since visited his grave,
Words: 8:and stood over his monument in St. Paul's.

Words: 2:LETTER XI.
Words: 0:
Words: 5:_York Minster--The Great Organ--Newcastle-on-Tyne--The Labouring
Words: 4:Classes--The American Slave--Sheffield--James Montgomery._
Words: 0:
Words: 0:
Words: 2:                    _January, 1850_.
Words: 0:
Words: 13:Some days since, I left the Metropolis to fulfil a few engagements to
Words: 13:visit provincial towns; and after a ride of nearly eight hours, we were
Words: 16:in sight of the ancient city of York. It was night, the moon was in her
Words: 11:zenith, and there seemed nothing between her and the earth but
Words: 10:glittering gold. The moon, the stars, and the innumerable gas-lights,
Words: 13:gave the city a panoramic appearance. Like a mountain starting out of a
Words: 13:plain, there stood the Cathedral in all its glory, looking down upon the
Words: 10:surrounding buildings, with all the appearance of a Gulliver standing
Words: 11:over the Lilliputians. Night gave us no opportunity to view the
Words: 12:Minster. However, we were up the next morning before the sun, and
Words: 11:walking round the Cathedral with a degree of curiosity seldom excited
Words: 13:within us. It is thought that a building of the same dimensions would
Words: 14:take fifty years to complete it at the present time, even with all the
Words: 12:improvements of the nineteenth century, and would cost no less than the
Words: 13:enormous sum of two millions of pounds sterling. From what I had heard
Words: 11:of this famous Cathedral, my expectations were raised to the highest
Words: 14:point; but it surpassed all the idea that I had formed of it. On
Words: 12:entering the building, we lost all thought of the external appearance by
Words: 13:the matchless beauty of the interior. The echo produced by the tread of
Words: 12:our feet upon the floor as we entered, resounding through the aisles,
Words: 14:seemed to say "Put off your shoes, for the place whereon you tread is
Words: 13:holy ground." We stood with hat in hand, and gazed with wonder and
Words: 11:astonishment down the incomparable vista of more than five hundred feet.
Words: 14:The organ, which stands near the centre of the building, is said to be
Words: 16:one of the finest in the world. A wall, in front of which is a screen
Words: 11:of the most gorgeous and florid architecture and executed in solid
Words: 10:stone, separates the nave from the service choir. The beautiful
Words: 13:workmanship of this makes it appear so perfect, as almost to produce the
Words: 13:belief that it is tracery work of wood. We ascended the rough stone
Words: 14:steps through a winding stair to the turrets, where we had such a view
Words: 13:of the surrounding country, as can be obtained from no other place. On
Words: 14:the top of the centre and highest turret, is a grotesque figure of a
Words: 12:fiddler; rather a strange looking object, we thought, to occupy the most
Words: 11:elevated pinnacle on the house of God. All dwellings in the
Words: 12:neighbourhood appear like so many dwarfs couching at the feet of the
Words: 11:Minster; while its own vastness and beauty impress the observer with
Words: 13:feelings of awe and sublimity. As we stood upon the top of this
Words: 8:stupendous mountain of ecclesiastical architecture, and surveyed the
Words: 10:picturesque hills and valleys around, imagination recalled the tumult of
Words: 12:the sanguinary battles fought in sight of the edifice. The rebellion of
Words: 12:Octavius near three thousand years ago, his defeat and flight to the
Words: 12:Scots, his return and triumph over the Romans, and being crowned king
Words: 11:of all Britain; the assassination of Oswald king of the Northumbrians;
Words: 13:the flaying alive of Osbert; the crowning of Richard III; the siege by
Words: 12:William the Conqueror; the siege by Cromwell, and the pomp and splendour
Words: 11:with which the different monarchs had been received in York, all
Words: 14:appeared to be vividly before me. While we were thus calling to our aid
Words: 13:our knowledge of history, a sweet peal from the lungs of the ponderous
Words: 13:organ below cut short our stay among the turrets, and we descended to
Words: 14:have our organ of tune gratified, as well as to finish the inspection of
Words: 2:the interior.
Words: 0:
Words: 11:I have heard the sublime melodies of Handel, Hayden, and Mozart,
Words: 11:performed by the most skilful musicians; I have listened with delight
Words: 12:and awe to the soul-moving compositions of those masters, as they have
Words: 12:been chaunted in the most magnificent churches; but never did I hear
Words: 13:such music, and played upon such an instrument, as that sent forth by
Words: 13:the great organ in the Cathedral of York. The verger took much delight
Words: 14:in showing us the Horn that was once mounted with gold, but is now
Words: 12:garnished with brass. We viewed the monuments and tombs of the departed,
Words: 14:and then spent an hour before the great north window. The designs on the
Words: 12:painted glass, which tradition states was given to the church by five
Words: 14:virgin sisters, is the finest thing of the kind in Great Britain. I felt
Words: 13:a relief on once more coming into the open air and again beholding
Words: 12:Nature's own sun-light. The splendid ruins of St. Mary's Abbey, with its
Words: 10:eight beautiful light gothic windows, next attracted our attention. A
Words: 14:visit to the Castle finished our stay in York; and as we were leaving
Words: 14:the old city we almost imagined that we heard the chiming of the bells
Words: 11:for the celebration of the first Christian Sabbath, with Prince Arthur
Words: 4:as the presiding genius.
Words: 0:
Words: 5:       *       *       *       *       *
Words: 0:
Words: 10:England stands pre-eminently the first government in the world for
Words: 14:freedom of speech and of the press. Not even in our own beloved America,
Words: 13:can the man who feels himself oppressed speak as he can in Great
Words: 12:Britain. In some parts of England, however, the freedom of thought is
Words: 12:tolerated to a greater extent than in others; and of the places
Words: 11:favourable to reforms of all kinds, calculated to elevate and benefit
Words: 8:mankind, Newcastle-on-Tyne doubtless takes the lead. Surrounded by
Words: 10:innumerable coal mines, it furnishes employment for a large labouring
Words: 13:population, many of whom take a deep interest in the passing events of
Words: 12:the day, and, consequently, are a reading class. The public debater or
Words: 14:speaker, no matter what may be his subject, who fails to get an audience
Words: 14:in other towns, is sure of a gathering in the Music Hall, or Lecture
Words: 13:Room in Newcastle. Here I first had an opportunity of coming in contact
Words: 12:with a portion of the labouring people of Britain. I have addressed
Words: 10:large and influential meetings in Newcastle and the neighbouring towns,
Words: 14:and the more I see and learn of the condition of the working-classes of
Words: 13:England the more I am satisfied of the utter fallacy of the statements
Words: 12:often made that their condition approximates to that of the slaves of
Words: 10:America. Whatever may be the disadvantages that the British peasant
Words: 14:labours under, he is free; and if he is not satisfied with his employer
Words: 14:he can make choice of another. He also has the right to educate his
Words: 13:children; and he is the equal of the most wealthy person before an
Words: 14:English Court of Justice. But how is it with the American Slave? He has
Words: 15:no right to himself, no right to protect his wife, his child, or his own
Words: 13:person. He is nothing more than a living tool. Beyond his field or
Words: 13:workshop he knows nothing. There is no amount of ignorance he is not
Words: 16:capable of. He has not the least idea of the face of this earth, nor of
Words: 13:the history or constitution of the country in which he dwells. To him
Words: 9:the literature, science, and art--the progressive history, and the
Words: 12:accumulated discoveries of bygone ages, are as if they had never been.
Words: 13:The past is to him as yesterday, and the future scarcely more than
Words: 9:to-morrow. Ancestral monuments, he has none; written documents fraught
Words: 11:with cogitations of other times, he has none; and any instrumentality
Words: 9:calculated to awaken and expound the intellectual activity and
Words: 11:comprehension of a present or approaching generation, he has none. His
Words: 14:condition is that of the leopard of his own native Africa. It lives, it
Words: 12:propagates its kind; but never does it indicate a movement towards that
Words: 11:all but angelic intelligence of man. The slave eats, drinks, and
Words: 13:sleeps--all for the benefit of the man who claims his body as his
Words: 14:property. Before the tribunals of his country he has no voice. He has no
Words: 14:higher appeal than the mere will of his owner. He knows nothing of the
Words: 11:inspired Apostles through their writings. He has no Sabbath, no Church,
Words: 16:no Bible, no means of grace,--and yet we are told that he is as well off
Words: 14:as the labouring classes of England. It is not enough that the people of
Words: 10:my country should point to their Declaration of Independence which
Words: 13:declares that "all men are created equal." It is not enough that they
Words: 10:should laud to the skies a constitution containing boasting declarations
Words: 14:in favour of freedom. It is not enough that they should extol the genius
Words: 12:of Washington, the patriotism of Henry, or the enthusiasm of Otis. The
Words: 14:time has come when nations are judged by the acts of the present instead
Words: 16:of the past. And so it must be with America. In no place in the United
Words: 10:Kingdom has the American Slave warmer friends than in Newcastle.
Words: 0:
Words: 5:       *       *       *       *       *
Words: 0:
Words: 14:I am now in Sheffield, and have just returned from a visit to James
Words: 12:Montgomery, the poet. In company with James Wall, Esq., I proceeded to
Words: 13:The Mount, the residence of Mr. Montgomery; and our names being sent in,
Words: 14:we were soon in the presence of the "Christian Poet." He held in his
Words: 13:left hand the _Eclectic Review_ for the month, and with the right gave
Words: 14:me a hearty shake, and bade me "Welcome to old England." He was anything
Words: 17:but like the portraits I had seen of him, and the man I had in my mind's
Words: 14:eye. I had just been reading his "Pelican Island," and I eyed the poet
Words: 13:with no little interest. He is under the middle size, his forehead high
Words: 15:and well formed, the top of which was a little bald; his hair of a
Words: 13:yellowish colour, his eyes rather small and deep set, the nose long and
Words: 13:slightly aquiline, his mouth rather small, and not at all pretty. He was
Words: 13:dressed in black, and a large white cravat entirely hid his neck and
Words: 10:chin: his having been afflicted from childhood with salt-rhum, was
Words: 12:doubtless the cause of his chin being so completely buried in the
Words: 12:neckcloth. Upon the whole, he looked more like one of our American
Words: 13:Methodist parsons, than any one I have seen in this country. He entered
Words: 14:freely into conversation with us. He said he should be glad to attend my
Words: 13:lecture that evening, but that he had long since quit going out at
Words: 10:night. He mentioned having heard William Lloyd Garrison some years
Words: 15:before, and with whom he was well pleased. He said it had long been a
Words: 12:puzzle to him, how Americans could hold slaves and still retain their
Words: 14:membership in the churches. When we rose to leave, the old man took my
Words: 14:hand between his two, and with tears in his eyes said, "Go on your
Words: 11:Christian mission, and may the Lord protect and prosper you. Your
Words: 11:enslaved countrymen have my sympathy, and shall have my prayers." Thus
Words: 14:ended our visit to the Bard of Sheffield. Long after I had quitted the
Words: 13:presence of the poet, the following lines of his were ringing in my
Words: 1:ears:--
Words: 0:
Words: 5:   "Wanderer, whither dost thou roam?
Words: 5:    Weary wanderer, old and grey,
Words: 6:    Wherefore has thou left thine home,
Words: 6:    In the sunset of thy day.
Words: 5:    Welcome wanderer as thou art,
Words: 5:    All my blessings to partake;
Words: 6:    Yet thrice welcome to my heart,
Words: 5:    For thine injured people's sake.
Words: 5:    Wanderer, whither would'st thou roam?
Words: 5:    To what region far away?
Words: 7:    Bend thy steps to find a home,
Words: 6:    In the twilight of thy day.
Words: 5:    Where a tyrant never trod,
Words: 6:    Where a slave was never known--
Words: 5:    But where Nature worships God
Words: 4:    In the wilderness alone."
Words: 0:
Words: 0:
Words: 12:Mr. Montgomery seems to have thrown his entire soul into his meditations
Words: 13:on the wrongs of Switzerland. The poem from which we have just quoted,
Words: 12:is unquestionably one of his best productions, and contains more of the
Words: 13:fire of enthusiasm than all his other works. We feel a reverence almost
Words: 12:amounting to superstition, for the poet who deals with nature. And who
Words: 13:is more capable of understanding the human heart than the poet? Who has
Words: 10:better known the human feelings than Shakspere; better painted than
Words: 12:Milton, the grandeur of Virtue; better sighed than Byron over the subtle
Words: 12:weaknesses of Hope? Who ever had a sounder taste, a more exact
Words: 14:intellect than Dante? or who has ever tuned his harp more in favour of
Words: 5:Freedom, than our own Whittier?
Words: 0:
Words: 0:
Words: 0:

Words: 2:LETTER XII.
Words: 0:
Words: 10:_Kirkstall Abbey--Mary the Maid of the Inn--Newstead Abbey: Residence of
Words: 8:Lord Byron--Parish Church of Hucknall--Burial Place of Lord
Words: 6:Byron--Bristol: "Cook's Folly"--Chepstow Castle and Abbey--Tintern

Words: 2:                    _January 29_.
Words: 0:
Words: 11:In passing through Yorkshire, we could not resist the temptation it
Words: 12:offered, to pay a visit to the extensive and interesting ruin of
Words: 11:Kirkstall Abbey, which lies embosomed in a beautiful recess of Airedale,
Words: 12:about three miles from Leeds. A pleasant drive over a smooth road,
Words: 12:brought us abruptly in sight of the Abbey. The tranquil and pensive
Words: 13:beauty of the desolate Monastery, as it reposes in the lap of pastoral
Words: 10:luxuriance, and amidst the touching associations of seven centuries, is
Words: 11:almost beyond description when viewed from where we first beheld it.
Words: 13:After arriving at its base, we stood for some moments under the mighty
Words: 13:arches that lead into the great hall, gazing at its old grey walls
Words: 14:frowning with age. At the distance of a small field, the Aire is seen
Words: 15:gliding past the foot of the lawn on which the ruin stands, after it has
Words: 12:left those precincts, sparkling over a weir with a pleasing murmur. We
Words: 12:could fully enter into the feelings of the Poet when he says:--
Words: 0:
Words: 5:   "Beautiful fabric! even in decay
Words: 6:      And desolation, beauty still is thine;
Words: 8:    As the rich sunset of an autumn day,
Words: 7:      When gorgeous clouds in glorious hues combine
Words: 7:    To render homage to its slow decline,
Words: 7:      Is more majestic in its parting hour:
Words: 6:    Even so thy mouldering, venerable shrine
Words: 6:      Possesses now a more subduing power,
Words: 11:    Than in thine earlier sway, with pomp and pride thy dower."
Words: 0:
Words: 0:
Words: 14:The tale of "Mary, the Maid of the Inn," is supposed, and not without
Words: 12:foundation, to be connected with this Abbey. "Hark to Rover," the name
Words: 16:of the house where the key is kept, was, a century ago, a retired inn or
Words: 12:pot-house, and the haunt of many a desperate highwayman and poacher. The
Words: 13:anecdote is so well known, that it is scarcely necessary to relate it.
Words: 5:It, however, is briefly this:--
Words: 0:
Words: 12:"One stormy night, as two travellers sat at the inn, each having
Words: 11:exhausted his news, the conversation was directed to the Abbey, the
Words: 14:boisterous night, and Mary's heroism; when a bet was at last made by one
Words: 16:of them, that she would not go and bring back from the nave a slip of
Words: 11:the alder-tree growing there. Mary, however, did go; but having nearly
Words: 12:reached the tree, she heard a low, indistinct dialogue; at the same
Words: 10:time, something black fell and rolled towards her, which afterwards
Words: 13:proved to be a hat. Directing her attention to the place whence the
Words: 11:conversation proceeded, she saw, from behind a pillar, two men carrying
Words: 13:a murdered body: they passed near the place where she stood, a heavy
Words: 13:cloud was swept from off the face of the moon, and Mary fell
Words: 10:senseless--one of the murderers was her intended husband! She was
Words: 12:awakened from her swoon, but--her reason had fled for ever." Mr. Southey
Words: 14:wrote a beautiful poem founded on this story, which will be found in his
Words: 11:published works. We spent nearly three hours in wandering through these
Words: 12:splendid ruins. It is both curious and interesting to trace the early
Words: 11:history of these old piles, which become the resort of thousands,
Words: 12:nine-tenths of whom are unaware either of the classic ground on which
Words: 13:they tread, or of the peculiar interest thrown around the spot by the
Words: 4:deeds of remote ages.
Words: 0:
Words: 13:During our stay in Leeds, we had the good fortune to become acquainted
Words: 12:with Wilson Armistead, Esq. This gentleman is well known as an able
Words: 12:writer against Slavery. His most elaborate work is "A Tribute for the
Words: 13:Negro." This is a volume of 560 pages, and is replete with facts
Words: 11:refuting the charges of inferiority brought against the Negro race. Few
Words: 12:English gentlemen have done more to hasten the day of the American
Words: 5:slave's liberation, than Wilson Armistead.
Words: 0:
Words: 5:       *       *       *       *       *
Words: 0:
Words: 13:We have just paid a visit to Newstead Abbey, the far-famed residence of
Words: 11:Lord Byron. I posted from Hucknall over to Newstead one pleasant
Words: 11:morning, and, being provided with a letter of introduction to Colonel
Words: 14:Wildman, I lost no time in presenting myself at the door of the Abbey.
Words: 11:But, unfortunately for me, the Colonel was at Mansfield, in attendance
Words: 12:at the Assizes--he being one of the County Magistrates. I did not
Words: 13:however lose the object of my visit, as every attention was paid in
Words: 14:showing me about the premises. I felt as every one must, who gazes for
Words: 13:the first time upon these walls, and remembers that it was here, even
Words: 12:amid the comparative ruins of a building once dedicated to the sacred
Words: 13:cause of Religion and her twin sister, Charity, that the genius of Byron
Words: 11:was first developed. Here that he paced with youthful melancholy the
Words: 11:halls of his illustrious ancestors, and trode the walks of the
Words: 8:long-banished monks. The housekeeper--a remarkably good looking and
Words: 9:polite woman--showed us through the different apartments, and explained
Words: 12:in the most minute manner every object of interest connected with the
Words: 11:interior of the building. We first visited the Monks' Parlour, which
Words: 11:seemed to contain nothing of note, except a very fine stained
Words: 11:window--one of the figures representing St. Paul, surmounted by a cross.
Words: 10:We passed through Lord Byron's Bedroom, the Haunted Chamber, the
Words: 11:Library, and the Eastern Corridor, and halted in the Tapestry Bedroom,
Words: 13:which is truly a magnificent apartment, formed by the Byrons for the use
Words: 13:of King Charles II. The ceiling is richly decorated with the Byron arms.
Words: 11:We next visited the grand Drawing-room, probably the finest in the
Words: 10:building. This saloon contains a large number of splendid portraits,
Words: 12:among which is the celebrated portrait of Lord Byron, by Phillips. In
Words: 15:this room we took into our hand the Skull-cup, of which so much has been
Words: 11:written, and that has on it a short inscription, commencing with--"Start
Words: 12:not--nor deem my spirit fled." Leaving this noble room, we descended by
Words: 13:a few polished oak steps into the West Corridor, from which we entered
Words: 12:the grand Dining Hall, and through several other rooms, until we reached
Words: 14:the Chapel. Here we were shown a stone coffin which had been found near
Words: 12:the high altar, when the workmen were excavating the vault, intended by
Words: 13:Lord Byron for himself and his dog. The coffin contained the skeleton of
Words: 14:an Abbot, and also the identical skull from which the cup, of which I
Words: 13:have made mention, was made. We then left the building, and took a
Words: 12:stroll through the grounds. After passing a pond of cold crystal water,
Words: 16:we came to a dark wood in which are two leaden statues of Pan, and a
Words: 12:female satyr--very fine specimens as works of art. We here inspected the
Words: 14:tree whereon Byron carved his own name and that of his sister, with the
Words: 14:date, all of which are still legible. However, the tree is now dead, and
Words: 14:we were informed that Colonel Wildman intended to have it cut down so as
Words: 10:to preserve the part containing the inscription. After crossing an
Words: 11:interesting and picturesque part of the gardens, we arrived within the
Words: 12:precincts of the ancient Chapel, near which we observed a neat marble
Words: 13:monument, and which we supposed to have been erected to the memory of
Words: 14:some of the Byrons; but, on drawing near to it, we read the following
Words: 1:inscription:--
Words: 0:
Words: 3:                "Near this spot
Words: 6:        are deposited the remains of one
Words: 5:      who possessed beauty without vanity,
Words: 3:          strength without insolence,
Words: 3:            courage without ferocity,
Words: 9:    and all the virtues of man without his vices.
Words: 7:  This praise, which would be unmeaning flattery,
Words: 5:        if inscribed over human ashes,
Words: 9:      is but a just tribute to the memory of
Words: 3:              BOATSWAIN, a dog,
Words: 7:    Who was born at Newfoundland, May, 1803,
Words: 8:  and died at Newstead Abbey, November 18, 1808."
Words: 0:
Words: 0:
Words: 14:By a will which his Lordship executed in 1811, he directed that his own
Words: 14:body should be buried in a vault in the garden, near his faithful dog.
Words: 11:This feeling of affection to his dumb and faithful follower, commendable
Words: 13:in itself, seems here to have been carried beyond the bounds of reason
Words: 2:and propriety.
Words: 0:
Words: 15:In another part of the grounds we saw the oak tree planted by the poet
Words: 12:himself. It has now attained a goodly size, considering the growth of
Words: 14:the oak, and bids fair to become a lasting memento to the Noble Bard,
Words: 14:and to be a shrine to which thousands of pilgrims will resort in future
Words: 14:ages, to do homage to his mighty genius. This tree promises to share in
Words: 10:after times the celebrity of Shakspere's mulberry, and Pope's willow.
Words: 14:Near by, and in the tall trees, the rooks were keeping up a tremendous
Words: 10:noise. After seeing everything of interest connected with the great
Words: 13:poet, we entered our chaise, and left the premises. As we were leaving,
Words: 13:I turned to take a farewell look at the Abbey, standing in solemn
Words: 13:grandeur, the long ivy clinging fondly to the rich tracery of a former
Words: 13:age. Proceeding to the little town of Hucknall, we entered the old grey
Words: 12:Parish Church, which has for ages been the last resting-place of the
Words: 14:Byrons, and where repose the ashes of the Poet, marked only by a neat
Words: 13:marble slab, bearing the date of the poet's birth, death, and the fact
Words: 14:that the tablet was placed there by his sister. This closed my visit to
Words: 8:the interesting scenes associated with Byron's strange eventful
Words: 12:history--scenes that ever acquire a growing charm as the lapse of years
Words: 13:softens the errors of the man, and confirms the genius of the poet.

Words: 2:                    _May 10_.
Words: 0:
Words: 14:It was on a lovely morning that I found myself on board the little
Words: 12:steamer _Wye_, passing out of Bristol harbour. In going down the river,
Words: 13:we saw on our right, the stupendous rocks of St. Vincent towering some
Words: 14:four or five hundred feet above our heads. By the swiftness of our fairy
Words: 12:steamer, we were soon abreast of Cook's Folly, a singular tower, built
Words: 14:by a man from whom it takes its name, and of which the following
Words: 12:romantic story is told:--"Some years since a gentleman, of the name of
Words: 13:Cook, erected this tower, which has since gone by the name of 'Cook's
Words: 12:Folly.' A son having been born, he was desirous of ascertaining, by
Words: 12:means of astrology, if he would live to enjoy his property. Being
Words: 11:himself a firm believer, like the poet Dryden, that certain information
Words: 11:might be obtained from the above science, he caused the child's
Words: 13:horoscope to be drawn, and found, to his dismay, that in his third,
Words: 12:sixteenth, or twenty-first year, he would be in danger of meeting with
Words: 12:some fearful calamity or sudden death, to avert which he caused the
Words: 12:turret to be constructed, and the child placed therein. Secure, as he
Words: 11:vainly thought, there he lived, attended by a faithful servant, their
Words: 13:food and fuel being conveyed to them by means of a pully-basket, until
Words: 14:he was old enough to wait upon himself. On the eve of his twenty-first
Words: 11:year, his parent's hopes rose high, and great were the rejoicings
Words: 13:prepared to welcome the young heir to his home. But, alas! no human
Words: 14:skill could avert the dark fate which clung to him. The last night he
Words: 15:had to pass alone in the turret, a bundle of faggots was conveyed to him
Words: 14:as usual, in which lay concealed a viper, which clung to his hand. The
Words: 14:bite was fatal; and, instead of being borne in triumph, the dead body of
Words: 14:his only son was the sad spectacle which met the sight of his father."
Words: 0:
Words: 12:We crossed the channel and soon entered the mouth of that most
Words: 13:picturesque of rivers, the Wye. As we neared the town of Chepstow the
Words: 13:old Castle made its appearance, and a fine old ruin it is. Being
Words: 11:previously provided with a letter of introduction to a gentleman in
Words: 14:Chepstow, I lost no time in finding him out. This gentleman gave me a
Words: 12:cordial reception, and did what Englishmen seldom ever do, lent me his
Words: 14:saddle horse to ride to the Abbey. While lunch was in preparation I took
Words: 13:a stroll through the Castle which stood near by. We entered the Castle
Words: 12:through the great door-way and were soon treading the walls that had
Words: 12:once sustained the cannon and the sentinel, but were now covered with
Words: 13:weeds and wild flowers. The drum and fife had once been heard within
Words: 14:these walls--the only music now is the cawing of the rook and daw. We
Words: 12:paid a hasty visit to the various apartments, remaining longest in those
Words: 12:of most interest. The room in which Martin the Regicide was imprisoned
Words: 13:nearly twenty years, was pointed out to us. The Castle of Chepstow is
Words: 12:still a magnificent pile, towering upon the brink of a stupendous cliff,
Words: 14:on reaching the top of which, we had a splendid view of the surrounding
Words: 11:country. Time, however, compelled us to retrace our steps, and after
Words: 14:partaking of a lunch, we mounted a horse for the first time in ten
Words: 12:years, and started for Tintern Abbey. The distance from Chepstow to the
Words: 14:Abbey is about five miles, and the road lies along the banks of the
Words: 14:river. The river is walled in on either side by hills of much beauty,
Words: 13:clothed from base to summit with the richest verdure. I can conceive of
Words: 12:nothing more striking than the first appearance of the Abbey. As we
Words: 15:rounded a hill, all at once we saw the old ruin standing before us in
Words: 10:all its splendour. This celebrated ecclesiastical relic of the olden
Words: 12:time is doubtless the finest ruin of its kind in Europe. Embosomed
Words: 13:amongst hills, and situated on the banks of the most fairy-like river in
Words: 12:the world, its beauty can scarcely be surpassed. We halted at the
Words: 12:"Beaufort Arms," left our horse, and sallied forth to view the Abbey.
Words: 14:The sun was pouring a flood of light upon the old grey walls, lighting
Words: 14:up its dark recesses, as if to give us a better opportunity of viewing
Words: 12:it. I gazed with astonishment and admiration at its many beauties, and
Words: 11:especially at the superb gothic windows over the entrance door. The
Words: 11:beautiful gothic pillars, with here and there a representation of a
Words: 10:praying priest, and mailed knights, with saints and Christian martyrs,
Words: 10:and the hundreds of Scriptural representations, all indicate that this
Words: 12:was a place of considerable importance in its palmy days. The once
Words: 12:stone floor had disappeared, and we found ourselves standing on a floor
Words: 13:of unbroken green grass, swelling back to the old walls, and looking so
Words: 13:verdant and silken that it seemed the very floor of fancy. There are
Words: 13:more romantic and wilder places than this in the world, but none more
Words: 10:beautiful. The preservation of these old abbeys should claim the
Words: 13:attention of those under whose charge they are, and we felt like joining
Words: 5:with the poet and saying:--
Words: 0:
Words: 4:                           "O ye who dwell
Words: 7:    Around yon ruins, guard the precious charge
Words: 8:    From hands profane! O save the sacred pile--
Words: 8:    O'er which the wing of centuries has flown
Words: 5:    Darkly and silently, deep-shadowing all
Words: 6:    Its pristine honours--from the ruthless grasp
Words: 3:    Of future violation."
Words: 0:
Words: 0:
Words: 10:In contemplating these ruins more closely, the mind insensibly reverts
Words: 12:to the period of feudal and regal oppression, when structures like that
Words: 10:of Tintern Abbey necessarily became the scenes of stirring and
Words: 10:highly-important events. How altered is the scene! Where were formerly
Words: 9:magnificence and splendour; the glittering array of priestly prowess;
Words: 11:the crowded halls of haughty bigots, and the prison of religious
Words: 12:offenders; there is now but a heap of mouldering ruins. The oppressed
Words: 12:and the oppressor have long since lain down together in the peaceful
Words: 10:grave. The ruin, generally speaking, is unusually perfect, and the
Words: 10:sculpture still beautifully sharp. The outward walls are nearly entire,
Words: 15:and are thickly clad with ivy. Many of the windows are also in a good
Words: 12:state of preservation; but the roof has long since fallen in. The
Words: 9:feathered songsters were fluttering about, and pouring forth their
Words: 14:artless lays as a tribute of joy; while the lowing of the herds, the
Words: 15:bleating of flocks, and the hum of bees upon the farm near by, all burst
Words: 13:upon the ear, and gave the scene a picturesque sublimity that can be
Words: 10:easier imagined than described. Most assuredly Shakspere had such ruins
Words: 5:in view when he exclaimed--
Words: 0:
Words: 6:   "The cloud-capp'd towers, the gorgeous palaces,
Words: 7:    The solemn temples, the great globe itself,
Words: 7:    Yea, all which it inherit, shall dissolve--
Words: 8:    And, like the baseless fabric of a vision,
Words: 5:    Leave not a wreck behind."
Words: 0:
Words: 0:
Words: 13:In the afternoon we returned to Bristol, and I spent the greater part
Words: 12:of the next day in examining the interior of Redcliffe Church. Few
Words: 12:places in the West of England have greater claims upon the topographer
Words: 11:and historian than the church of St. Mary's, Redcliffe. Its antiquity,
Words: 10:the beauty of its architecture, and above all the interesting
Words: 10:circumstances connected with its history, entitle it to peculiar notice.
Words: 13:It is also associated with the enterprise of genius; for its name has
Words: 11:been blended with the reputation of Rowley, of Canynge, and of
Words: 13:Chatterton; and no lover of poetry and admirer of art can visit it
Words: 12:without a degree of enthusiasm. And when the old building shall have
Words: 11:mouldered into ruins, even these will be trodden with veneration as
Words: 12:sacred to the recollection of genius of the highest order. Ascending a
Words: 13:winding stair, we were shown into the Treasury Room. The room forms an
Words: 8:irregular octagon, admitting light through narrow unglazed apertures
Words: 11:upon the broken and scattered fragments of the famous Rowleian chests,
Words: 14:that with the rubble and dust of centuries cover the floor. It is here
Words: 12:creative fancy pictures forth the sad image of the spirit of the
Words: 12:spot--the ardent boy, flushed and fed by hope, musing on the brilliant
Words: 11:deception he had conceived--whose daring attempt has left his name unto
Words: 9:the intellectual world as a marvel and a mystery.
Words: 0:
Words: 14:That a boy under twelve years of age should write a series of poems,
Words: 12:imitating the style of the fifteenth century, and palm these poems off
Words: 15:upon the world as the work of a monk, is indeed strange; and that these
Words: 10:should become the object of interesting contemplation to the literary
Words: 11:world, and should awaken inquiries, and exercise the talents of a
Words: 12:Southey, a Bryant, a Miller, a Mathias, and others, savours more of
Words: 13:romance than reality. I had visited the room in a garret in High
Words: 14:Holborn, where this poor boy died. I had stood over a grave in the
Words: 13:burial-ground of the Lane Workhouse, which was pointed out to me as the
Words: 14:last resting-place of Chatterton; and now I was in the room where it was
Words: 11:alleged he obtained the manuscripts that gave him such notoriety. We
Words: 12:descended and viewed other portions of the church. The effect of the
Words: 11:chancel, as seen behind the pictures, is very singular, and suggestive
Words: 13:of many swelling thoughts. We look at the great east window, it is
Words: 11:unadorned with its wonted painted glass; we look at the altar-screen
Words: 13:beneath, on which the light of day again falls, and behold the injuries
Words: 14:it has received at the hands of time. There is a dreary mournfulness in
Words: 14:the scene which fastens on the mind, and is in unison with the time-worn
Words: 12:mouldering fragments that are seen all around us. And this dreariness is
Words: 13:not removed by our tracing the destiny of man on the storied pavements
Words: 14:or on the graven brass, that still bears upon its surface the names of
Words: 13:those who obtained the world's regard years back. This old pile is not
Words: 14:only an ornament to the city, but it stands a living monument to the
Words: 13:genius of its founder. Bristol has long sustained a high position as a
Words: 9:place from which the American Abolitionists have received substantial
Words: 10:encouragement in their arduous labours for the emancipation of the
Words: 13:slaves of that land; and the writer of this received the best evidence
Words: 12:that in this respect the character of the people had not been
Words: 8:exaggerated, especially as regards the "Clifton Ladies' Anti-Slavery
Words: 1:Society."
