Words: 6:_Aberdeen--Passage by Steamer--Edinburgh--Visit to the College--William
Words: 3:and Ellen Craft._
Words: 0:
Words: 0:
Words: 13:I have visited few places where I found more warm friends than in
Words: 8:Aberdeen. This is the Granite City of Scotland.
Words: 0:
Words: 12:Aberdeen reminds one of Boston, especially in a walk down Union Street,
Words: 12:which is said to be one of the finest promenades in Europe.
Words: 0:
Words: 15:The town is situated on a neck of land between the rivers Dee and Don,
Words: 13:and is the most important place in the north of Scotland. During our
Words: 14:third day in the city, we visited among other places the Old Bridge of
Words: 13:Don, which is not only resorted to on account of its antique celebrity
Words: 12:and peculiar appearance, but also because of the notoriety that it has
Words: 10:gained by Lord Byron's poem of the "Bridge of Don."
Words: 0:
Words: 14:An engagement to be in Edinburgh and vicinity, cut short our stay in the
Words: 14:north. The very mild state of the weather, and a wish to see something
Words: 12:of the coast between Aberdeen and Edinburgh, induced us to make the
Words: 3:journey by water.
Words: 0:
Words: 12:On Friday evening, the 14th, after delivering a lecture before the Total
Words: 12:Abstinence Society, in company with William and Ellen Craft, I went on
Words: 12:board the steamer bound for Edinburgh. On reaching the vessel, we found
Words: 10:the drawing-room almost entirely at our service, and prejudice against
Words: 11:colour being unknown, we had no difficulty in getting the best
Words: 11:accommodation which the steamer could furnish. This is so unlike the
Words: 9:pro-slavery, negro-hating spirit of America, that the Crafts seemed
Words: 13:almost bewildered by the transition. I had been in the saloon but a
Words: 12:short time, when, looking at the newspapers on the table, I discovered
Words: 15:the _North Star_. It was like meeting with a friend in a strange land. I
Words: 17:looked in vain on the margin for the name of its owner, but as I did not
Words: 16:feel at liberty to take it, and as it appeared to be alone, I laid the
Words: 8:_Liberator_ by its side to keep it company.
Words: 0:
Words: 14:The night was a glorious one. The sky was without a speck; and the
Words: 14:clear, piercing air had a brilliancy I have seldom seen. The moon was in
Words: 10:its zenith--the steamer and surrounding objects were beautiful in the
Words: 14:extreme. The boat got under weigh at a little past twelve, and we were
Words: 15:soon out at sea. The "Queen" is a splendid craft, and without the aid of
Words: 15:sails, was able to make fifteen miles within the hour. I was up the next
Words: 13:morning before the sun, and found the sea as on the previous night--as
Words: 13:calm and smooth as a mirror. It was a delightful morning, more like
Words: 14:April than February; and the sun, as it rose, seemed to fire every peak
Words: 14:of the surrounding hills. On our left, lay the Island of May, while to
Words: 13:the right was to be seen the small fishing town of Anstruther, twenty
Words: 12:miles distant from Edinburgh. Beyond these, on either side, was a range
Words: 11:of undulating blue mountains, swelling as they retired, into a bolder
Words: 10:outline and a loftier altitude, until they terminated some twenty-five
Words: 15:or thirty miles in the dim distance. A friend at my side pointed out a
Words: 13:place on the right, where the remains of an old castle or look-out
Words: 13:house, used in the time of the border wars, once stood, and which
Words: 8:reminded us of the barbarism of the past.
Words: 0:
Words: 12:But these signs are fast disappearing. The plough and roller have passed
Words: 13:over many of these foundations, and the time will soon come, when the
Words: 12:antiquarian will look in vain for those places that history has pointed
Words: 12:out to him, as connected with the political and religious struggles of
Words: 14:the past. The steward of the vessel came round to see who of the
Words: 13:passengers wished for breakfast, and as the keen air of the morning had
Words: 13:given me an appetite, and there being no prejudice on the score of
Words: 15:colour, I took my seat at the table and gave ample evidence that I was
Words: 14:not an invalid. On returning to the deck again, I found we had entered
Words: 13:the Forth, and that "Modern Athens" was in sight; and, far above every
Words: 13:other object, with its turrets almost lost in the clouds, could be seen
Words: 12:Edinburgh Castle. After landing, a pleasant ride over one of the finest
Words: 12:roads in Scotland, with a sprinkling of beautiful villas on either side,
Words: 7:brought us once more to Cannon's Hotel.
Words: 0:
Words: 13:In a city like Edinburgh, there is always something to keep the public
Words: 13:alive, but during our three days' stay in the town, on this occasion,
Words: 11:there were topics under discussion which seemed to excite the people,
Words: 13:although I had been told that the Scotch were not excitable. Indeed all
Words: 13:Edinburgh seemed to have gone mad about the Pope. If his Holiness should
Words: 15:think fit to pay a visit to his new dominions, I would advise him to
Words: 7:keep out of reach of the Scotch.
Words: 0:
Words: 14:In company with the Crafts, I visited the Calton Hill, from which we had
Words: 12:a delightful view of the city and surrounding country. I had an
Words: 12:opportunity during my stay in the city, of visiting the Infirmary, and
Words: 12:was pleased to see among the two or three hundred students, three
Words: 13:coloured young men, seated upon the same benches with those of a fairer
Words: 13:complexion, and yet there appeared no feeling on the part of the whites
Words: 9:towards their coloured associates, except of companionship and respect.
Words: 12:One of the cardinal truths, both of religion and freedom, is the
Words: 13:equality and brotherhood of man. In the sight of God and all just
Words: 10:institutions, the whites can claim no precedence or privilege, on
Words: 13:account of their being white; and if coloured men are not treated as
Words: 12:they should be in the educational institutions in America, it is a
Words: 11:pleasure to know that all distinction ceases by crossing the broad
Words: 12:Atlantic. I had scarcely left the lecture room of the Institute and
Words: 14:reached the street, when I met a large number of the students on their
Words: 14:way to the college, and here again were seen coloured men arm in arm
Words: 11:with whites. The proud American who finds himself in the splendid
Words: 12:streets of Edinburgh, and witnesses such scenes as these, can but behold
Words: 13:in them the degradation of his own country, whose laws would make slaves
Words: 12:of these same young men, should they appear in the streets of
Words: 4:Charleston or New Orleans.
Words: 0:
Words: 14:After all, our country is the most despotic in the wide world, and to
Words: 15:expose and hold it up to the scorn and contempt of other nations, is the
Words: 14:duty of every coloured man who would be true to himself and his race.
Words: 0:
Words: 0:
Words: 12:During my stay in Edinburgh, I accepted an invitation to breakfast with
Words: 10:the great champion of Philosophical Phrenology. Few foreigners are more
Words: 11:admired in America, than the author of "The Constitution of Man."[B]
Words: 14:Although not far from 70 years of age, I found him apparently as active
Words: 15:and as energetic as many men of half that age. He was much pleased with
Words: 15:Mr. and Mrs. Craft, who formed a part of the breakfast party. It may be
Words: 13:a pleasure to the friends of these two fugitive slaves, to know that
Words: 14:they are now the inmates of a good school where they are now being
Words: 12:educated. For this, they are mainly indebted to that untiring friend of
Words: 12:the Slave, John B. Estlin, Esq., of Bristol, whose zeal and co-operation
Words: 11:with the American Abolitionists, have gained for him an undying name
Words: 9:with the friends of freedom in the New World.

Words: 4:                    EDINBURGH, _January 1, 1851_.
Words: 0:
Words: 13:You will see by the date of this that I am spending my
Words: 10:New-Year's-Day in the Scottish Capital, in company with our friend,
Words: 13:William Craft. I came by invitation to attend a meeting of the Edinburgh
Words: 3:Ladies' Emancipation Society.
Words: 0:
Words: 12:The meeting was held on Monday evening last, at which William Craft
Words: 13:gave, for the first time, since his arrival in this country, a history
Words: 12:of his escape from Georgia, two years ago, together with his recent
Words: 3:flight from Boston.
Words: 0:
Words: 12:Craft's reception was one of deep enthusiasm, and his story was well
Words: 13:told, and made a powerful impression on the audience. I would that the
Words: 11:slaveholders, Hughes and Knight, could have been present and heard the
Words: 11:thundering applause with which our friend was received on the following
Words: 10:evening. Craft attended a meeting of the Edinburgh Total Abstinence
Words: 11:Society, before which I lectured, and his appearance here was also
Words: 12:hailed with much enthusiasm. Our friend bids fair to become a favourite
Words: 3:with the Scotch.
Words: 0:
Words: 12:Much regret was expressed that Ellen was not present. She was detained
Words: 12:in Liverpool by indisposition. But Mrs. Craft has so far recovered, that
Words: 5:we expect her here to-morrow.
Words: 0:
Words: 12:The appearance of these two fugitives in Great Britain, at this time,
Words: 12:and under the circumstances, will aid our cause, and create a renewed
Words: 10:hatred to the abominable institution of American slavery. I have
Words: 13:received letters from a number of the friends of the slave, in which

Words: 14:But I must give you my impression of this fine city. Edinburgh is the
Words: 13:most picturesque of all the towns which I have visited since my arrival
Words: 12:in the father-land. Its situation has been compared to that of Athens,
Words: 15:but it is said that the modern Athens is superior to the ancient. I was
Words: 13:deeply impressed with the idea that I had seen the most beautiful of
Words: 9:cities, after beholding those fashionable resorts, Paris and Versailles.
Words: 14:I have seen nothing in the way of public grounds to compare with the
Words: 12:gardens of Versailles, or the _Champs Elysees_ at Paris; and as for
Words: 15:statuary, the latter place is said to take the lead of the rest of the
Words: 1:world.
Words: 0:
Words: 11:The general appearance of Edinburgh prepossesses one in its favour. The
Words: 13:town being built upon the brows of a large terrace, presents the most
Words: 11:wonderful perspective. Its first appearance to a stranger, and the first
Words: 12:impression, can scarcely be but favourable. In my first walk through the
Words: 13:town, I was struck with the difference in the appearance of the people
Words: 11:from the English. But the difference between the Scotch and the
Words: 10:Americans, is very great. The cheerfulness depicted in the countenances
Words: 13:of the people here, and their free and easy appearance, is very striking
Words: 15:to a stranger. He who taught the sun to shine, the flowers to bloom, the
Words: 12:birds to sing, and blesses us with rain, never intended that his
Words: 11:creatures should look sad. There is a wide difference between the
Words: 13:Americans and any other people which I have seen. The Scotch are healthy
Words: 7:and robust, unlike the long-faced, sickly-looking Americans.
Words: 0:
Words: 13:While on our journey from London to Paris, to attend the Peace Congress,
Words: 12:I could not but observe the marked difference between the English and
Words: 11:American delegates. The former looked as if their pockets had been
Words: 12:filled with sandwiches, made of good bread and roast beef, while the
Words: 11:latter appeared as if their pockets had been filled with Holloway's
Words: 5:Pills, and Mrs. Kidder's Cordial.
Words: 0:
Words: 15:I breakfasted this morning in a room in which the Poet Burns, as I was
Words: 11:informed, had often sat. The conversation here turned upon Burns. The
Words: 15:lady of the house pointed to a scrap of poetry which was in a frame
Words: 14:hanging on the wall, written, as she said, by the Poet, on hearing the
Words: 12:people rejoicing in a church over the intelligence of a victory. I
Words: 8:copied it and will give it to you:--
Words: 0:
Words: 6:   "Ye hypocrites! are these your pranks,
Words: 7:    To murder men and give God thanks?
Words: 7:    For shame! give o'er, proceed no further,
Words: 7:    God won't accept your thanks for murder."
Words: 0:
Words: 0:
Words: 14:The fact that I was in the room where Scotland's great national poet had
Words: 15:been a visitor, caused me to feel that I was on classic, if not hallowed
Words: 13:ground. On returning from our morning visit, we met a gentleman with a
Words: 14:coloured lady on each arm. Craft remarked in a very dry manner, "If they
Words: 13:were in Georgia, the slaveholders would make them walk in a more hurried
Words: 14:gait than they do." I said to my friend, that if he meant the
Words: 10:pro-slavery prejudice would not suffer them to walk peaceably through
Words: 13:the streets, they need go no further than the pro-slavery cities of New
Words: 10:York and Philadelphia. When walking through the streets, I amused
Words: 12:myself, by watching Craft's countenance; and in doing so, imagined I saw
Words: 11:the changes experienced by every fugitive slave in his first month's
Words: 11:residence in this country. A sixteen months' residence has not yet
Words: 5:familiarized me with the change.

Words: 5:                    LAUREL BANK, _Jan. 18, 1851_.
Words: 0:
Words: 11:Dear Douglass,--I remained in Edinburgh a day or two after the
Words: 14:date of my last letter, which gave me an opportunity of seeing some of
Words: 13:the lions in the way of public buildings, &c., in company with our
Words: 13:friend Wm. Craft. I paid a visit to the Royal Institute, and inspected
Words: 11:the very fine collection of paintings, statues, and other productions of
Words: 13:art. The collection in the Institute is not to be compared to the
Words: 13:British Museum at London, or the Louvre at Paris, but is probably the
Words: 13:best in Scotland. Paintings from the hands of many of the masters, such
Words: 12:as Sir A. Vandyke, Tiziano, Vercellio and Van Dellen, were hanging on
Words: 13:the wall, and even the names of Reubens, and Titian, were attached to
Words: 13:some of the finer specimens. Many of these represent some of the nobles,
Words: 11:and distinguished families of Rome, Athens, Greece, &c. A beautiful one
Words: 12:representing a group of the Lomellini family of Genoa, seemed to attract
Words: 7:the attention of most of the visitors.
Words: 0:
Words: 13:In visiting this place, we passed close by the monument of Sir Walter
Words: 14:Scott. This is the most exquisite thing of the kind that I have seen
Words: 14:since coming to this country. It is said to be the finest monument in
Words: 13:Europe. There sits the author of "Waverley," with a book and pencil in
Words: 13:hand, taking notes. A beautiful dog is seated by his side. Whether this
Words: 13:is meant to represent his favourite dog, Camp, at whose death the Poet
Words: 15:shed so many tears, we were not informed; but I was of opinion that it
Words: 12:might be the faithful Percy, whose monument stands in the grounds at
Words: 13:Abbotsford. Scott was an admirer of the canine tribe. One may form a
Words: 11:good idea of the appearance of this distinguished writer, when living,
Words: 12:by viewing this remarkable statue. The statue is very beautiful, but not
Words: 15:equal to the one of Lord Byron, which was executed to be placed by the
Words: 10:side of Johnson, Milton, and Addison, in Poets' Corner, Westminster
Words: 13:Abbey; but the Parliament not allowing it a place there, it now stands
Words: 13:in one of the Colleges at Cambridge. While viewing the statue of Byron,
Words: 14:I thought he, too, should have been represented with a dog by his side,
Words: 14:for he, like Scott, was remarkably fond of dogs, so much so that he
Words: 10:intended to have his favourite, Boatswain, interred by his side.
Words: 0:
Words: 14:We paid a short visit to the monuments of Burns and Allan Ramsay, and
Words: 13:the renowned old Edinburgh Castle. The Castle is now used as a barrack
Words: 13:for Infantry. It is accessible only from the High Street, and must have
Words: 12:been impregnable before the discovery of gunpowder. In the wars with the
Words: 13:English, it was twice taken by stratagem; once in a very daring manner,
Words: 13:by climbing up the most inaccessible part of the rock upon which it
Words: 13:stands, and where a foe was least expected, and putting the guard to
Words: 12:death; and another time, by a party of soldiers disguising themselves as
Words: 9:merchants, and obtaining admission inside the Castle gates. They
Words: 11:succeeded in preventing the gates from being closed, until reinforced by
Words: 13:a party of men under Sir Wm. Douglas, who soon overpowered the occupants
Words: 3:of the Castle.
Words: 0:
Words: 13:We could not resist the temptation held out to see the Palace of
Words: 12:Holyrood. It was in this place that the beautiful, but unfortunate Mary,
Words: 13:Queen of Scots, resided for a number of years. On reaching the palace,
Words: 15:we were met at the door by an elderly looking woman, with a red face,
Words: 13:garnished with a pair of second-hand curls, the whole covered with a cap
Words: 15:having the widest border that I had seen for years. She was very kind in
Words: 11:showing us about the premises, especially as we were foreigners, no
Words: 12:doubt expecting an extra fee for politeness. The most interesting of the
Words: 14:many rooms in this ancient castle, is the one which was occupied by the
Words: 9:Queen, and where her Italian favourite, Rizzio, was murdered.
Words: 0:
Words: 12:But by far the most interesting object which we visited while in
Words: 10:Edinburgh, was the house where the celebrated Reformer, John Knox,
Words: 12:re-resided. It is a queer-looking old building, with a pulpit on the
Words: 12:outside, and above the door are the nearly obliterated remains of the
Words: 9:following inscription:--"Lufe. God. Above. Al. And. your. Nichbour. As
Words: 11:you. Self." This was probably traced under the immediate direction of
Words: 13:the great Reformer. Such an inscription put upon a house of worship at
Words: 13:the present day, would be laughed at. I have given it to you,
Words: 7:punctuation and all, just as it stands.
Words: 0:
Words: 10:The general architecture of Edinburgh is very imposing, whether we
Words: 12:regard the picturesque disorder of the buildings, in the Old Town, or
Words: 12:the symmetrical proportions of the streets and squares in the New. But
Words: 13:on viewing this city which has the reputation of being the finest in
Words: 13:Europe, I was surprised to find that it had none of those sumptuous
Words: 10:structures, which like St. Paul's, or Westminster Abbey, York Minster,
Words: 10:and some other of the English provincial Cathedrals, astonish the
Words: 10:beholder alike by their magnitude and their architectural splendour. But
Words: 14:in no city which I have visited in the kingdom, is the general standard
Words: 7:of excellence better maintained than in Edinburgh.
Words: 0:
Words: 15:I am not sure, my dear friend, whether or not I mentioned in my last
Words: 13:letter the attendance of Wm. Craft and myself at a splendid Soiree of
Words: 11:the Edinburgh Temperance Society, and our being voted in life members,
Words: 13:in the most enthusiastic manner, by the whole audience. I will here give
Words: 14:you a part of the speech of the President, as reported in the _Christian
Words: 9:News_. This should cause the pro-slavery whites, and especially
Words: 12:negro-hating Sons of Temperance, who refuse the coloured man a place in
Words: 12:their midst, to feel ashamed of their unchristian conduct. Here it is,
Words: 5:let them judge for themselves:--
Words: 0:
Words: 14:"A great feature in our meeting to-night, is that we have beside us two
Words: 9:individuals, who, according to the immaculate laws of immaculate
Words: 10:Yankeedom, have been guilty of the tremendous crime of stealing
Words: 11:themselves. (Applause.) Mr. Craft, who sits beside me, has stolen his
Words: 12:good wife, and Mrs. Craft has stolen her worthy husband; and our
Words: 13:respected friend, Mr. Brown, has cast a covetous eye on his own person.
Words: 12:In the name of the Temperance reformers of Edinburgh--in the name of
Words: 12:universal Scotland, I would welcome these two victims of the white man's
Words: 11:pride, ambition, selfishness, and cupidity. I welcome them as our equals
Words: 11:in every respect. (Great applause.) What a humiliating thought it will
Words: 13:be, surely, for our American friends on the other side of the water,
Words: 14:when they hear (and we shall endeavour to let them hear) that the very
Words: 14:man whom they consider not worthy to sit in a third class carriage along
Words: 15:with a white man, and that too in a district of country where the very
Words: 9:aristocracy deal in cheap cheese--(great applause) traffic in tallow
Words: 12:candles, and spend their nights and days among raw hides and train
Words: 11:oil--(applause)--what a humbling thought it will be for them to know
Words: 14:that these very men in the centre of educated Scotland, in the midst of
Words: 13:educated Edinburgh, are thought fit to hold even the first rank upon our
Words: 11:aristocratic platform. Let us, then, my friends, lift our voices this
Words: 11:evening in one swelling chorus for the down-trodden slave. Let us
Words: 12:publish abroad the fact to the world, that the sympathies of Scotland
Words: 13:are with the bondsman everywhere. Let us unite our voices to cry, Down
Words: 10:with the iniquitous Slave Bill!--Down with the aristocracy of the
Words: 8:skin!--Perish forever the deepest-dyed, the hardest-hearted system of
Words: 9:abomination under heaven!--Perish the sum of all villanies! Perish
Words: 4:American slavery. (Great applause.)"
Words: 0:
Words: 13:But I must leave the good and hospitable people of the Scottish Capital
Words: 14:for the present. I have taken an elaborate stock of notes, and may speak
Words: 3:of Edinburgh again.
Words: 0:
Words: 14:I left William and Ellen Craft (the latter of whom has just come to
Words: 13:Edinburgh), and took the Glasgow train, and after a ride of two hours
Words: 11:through a beautiful country, with its winding hills on either side--its
Words: 10:fertile fields, luxuriant woods, and stately mansions lying around us,
Words: 14:arrived in the muddy, dirty, smoky, foggy city of Glasgow. As I had had
Words: 11:a standing invitation from a distinguished gentleman with whom I became
Words: 12:acquainted in London, to partake of his hospitality, should I ever visit
Words: 11:Glasgow, and again received a note while in Edinburgh renewing the
Words: 11:invitation, I proceeded to his residence at Partick, three miles from
Words: 14:Glasgow. This is one of the loveliest spots which I have yet seen. Our
Words: 14:mansion is on the side of Laurel Bank, a range of the Kilpatrick hills.
Words: 8:We have a view of the surrounding country.
Words: 0:
Words: 14:On Monday evening, Jan. 6, a public meeting was held in the City Hall,
Words: 13:to extend a welcome to the American fugitive slaves. The hall, one of
Words: 13:the largest in the kingdom, was filled at an early hour. At the
Words: 10:appointed time, Alex. Hastie, Esq., M.P., entered the great room,
Words: 11:followed by the fugitives and most of the leading abolitionists, amid
Words: 11:rapturous applause. With a Member of Parliament in the chair, and
Words: 12:almost any number of clergymen on the platform, the meeting had an
Words: 11:influential appearance. From report, I had imbibed the opinion that the
Words: 13:Scotch were not easily moved, but if I may judge from the enthusiasm
Words: 11:which characterised the City Hall demonstration, I should place them but
Words: 11:little behind the English. After an excellent speech from the Chairman,
Words: 9:and spirited addresses from several clergymen, William Craft was
Words: 13:introduced to the meeting, and gave an account of the escape of himself
Words: 11:and wife from slavery, and their subsequent flight from Boston. Any
Words: 13:description of mine would give but a poor idea of the intense feeling
Words: 13:that pervaded the meeting. I think all who were there, left the hall
Words: 11:after hearing that noble fugitive, with a greater abhorrence of American
Words: 5:slavery than they previously entertained.

Words: 2:LETTER XIV.
Words: 0:
Words: 6:_Stirling--Dundee--Dr. Dick--Geo. Gilfillan--Dr. Dick at home._
Words: 0:
Words: 0:
Words: 5:                    PERTH, SCOTLAND, _Jan. 31, 1851_.
Words: 0:
Words: 13:I am glad once more to breathe an atmosphere uncontaminated by the fumes
Words: 12:and smoke of a city with its population of three hundred thousand
Words: 12:inhabitants. In company with our friends Wm. and Ellen Craft, I left
Words: 13:Glasgow on the afternoon of the 23d inst., for Dundee, a beautiful town
Words: 14:situated on the banks of the river Tay. One like myself, who has spent
Words: 16:the best part of an eventful life in cities, and who prefers, as I do, a
Words: 12:country to a town life, feels a greater degree of freedom when
Words: 11:surrounded by forest trees, or country dwellings, and looking upon a
Words: 11:clear sky, than when walking through the thronged thoroughfares of a
Words: 12:city, with its dense population, meeting every moment a new or strange
Words: 13:face which one has never seen before, and never expects to see again.
Words: 13:Although I had met with one of the warmest public receptions with which
Words: 14:I have been greeted since my arrival in the country, and had had an
Words: 12:opportunity of shaking hands with many noble friends of the slave, whose
Words: 15:names I had often seen in print, yet I felt glad to see the tall
Words: 12:chimneys and smoke of Glasgow receding in the distance, as our 'iron
Words: 11:horse' was taking us with almost lightning speed from the commercial
Words: 3:capital of Scotland.
Words: 0:
Words: 13:The distance from Glasgow to Dundee is some seventy or eighty miles, and
Words: 13:we passed through the finest country which I have seen in this portion
Words: 12:of the Queen's dominions. We passed through the old town of Stirling,
Words: 13:which lies about thirty miles distant from Glasgow, and is a place much
Words: 14:frequented by those who travel for pleasure. It is built on the brow of
Words: 14:a hill, and the Castle from which it most probably derived its name, may
Words: 13:be seen from a distance. Had it not been for a "professional" engagement
Words: 14:the same evening at Dundee, I would most assuredly have halted to take a
Words: 5:look at the old building.
Words: 0:
Words: 14:The Castle is situated or built on an isolated rock, which seems as if
Words: 14:Nature had thrown it there for that purpose. It was once the retreat of
Words: 11:the Scottish Kings, and famous for its historical associations. Here the
Words: 13:"Lady of the Lake," with the magic ring, sought the monarch to intercede
Words: 13:for her father; here James II. murdered the Earl of Douglas; here the
Words: 12:beautiful but unfortunate Mary was made Queen; and here John Knox, the
Words: 11:Reformer, preached the coronation sermon of James VI. The Castle Hill
Words: 12:rises from the valley of the Forth, and makes an imposing and
Words: 12:picturesque appearance. The windings of the noble river till lost in the
Words: 8:distance, present pleasing contrasts, scarcely to be surpassed.
Words: 0:
Words: 12:The speed of our train, after passing Stirling, brought before us, in
Words: 12:quick succession, a number of fine valleys and farm houses. Every spot
Words: 13:seemed to have been arrayed by Nature for the reception of the cottage
Words: 13:of some happy family. During this ride, we passed many sites where the
Words: 10:lawns were made, the terraces defined and levelled, the groves
Words: 11:tastefully clumped, the ancient trees, though small when compared to our
Words: 11:great forest oaks, were beautifully sprinkled here and there, and in
Words: 12:everything the labour of art seemed to have been anticipated by Nature.
Words: 11:Cincinnatus could not have selected a prettier situation for a farm,
Words: 10:than some which presented themselves, during this delightful journey. At
Words: 13:last we arrived at the place of our destination, where our friends were
Words: 4:in waiting for us.
Words: 0:
Words: 14:As I have already forwarded to you a paper containing an account of the
Words: 12:Dundee meeting, I shall leave you to judge from these reports the
Words: 12:character of the demonstration. Yet I must mention a fact or two
Words: 13:connected with our first evening's visit to this town. A few hours after
Words: 14:our arrival in the place, we were called upon by a gentleman whose name
Words: 12:is known wherever the English language is spoken--one whose name is on
Words: 12:the tongue of every student and school-boy in this country and America,
Words: 13:and what lives upon their lips will live and be loved for ever.
Words: 0:
Words: 15:We were seated over a cup of strong tea, to revive our spirits for the
Words: 11:evening, when our friend entered the room, accompanied by a gentleman,
Words: 11:small in stature, and apparently seventy-five years of age, yet he
Words: 13:appeared as active as one half that age. Feeling half drowsy from riding
Words: 15:in the cold, and then the sudden change to a warm fire, I was rather
Words: 14:inclined not to move on the entrance of the stranger. But the name of
Words: 13:Thomas Dick, LL.D., roused me in a moment, from my lethargy; I could
Words: 11:scarcely believe that I was in the presence of the "Christian
Words: 15:Philosopher." Dr. Dick is one of the men to whom the age is indebted. I
Words: 15:never find myself in the presence of one to whom the world owes so much
Words: 14:as Dr. Dick, without feeling a thrilling emotion, as if I were in the
Words: 13:land of spirits. Dr. Dick had come to our lodgings to see and
Words: 11:congratulate Wm. and Ellen Craft upon their escape from the republican
Words: 13:Christians of the United States; and as he pressed the hand of the
Words: 13:"white slave," and bid her "welcome to British soil," I saw the silent
Words: 14:tear stealing down the cheek of this man of genius. How I wished that
Words: 9:the many slaveholders and pro-slavery professed Christians of America,
Words: 13:who have read and pondered the philosophy of this man, could have been
Words: 11:present. Thomas Dick is an abolitionist--one who is willing that the
Words: 11:world should know that he hates the "peculiar institution." At the
Words: 12:meeting that evening, Dr. Dick was among the most prominent. But this
Words: 12:was not the only distinguished man who took part on that occasion.
Words: 0:
Words: 12:Another great mind was on the platform, and entered his solemn protest
Words: 14:in a manner long to be remembered by those present. This was the Rev.
Words: 12:George Gilfillan, well known as the author of the "Portraits of Literary
Words: 12:Men." Mr. Gilfillan is an energetic speaker, and would have been the
Words: 13:lion of the evening, even if many others who are more distinguished as
Words: 13:platform orators had been present. I think it was Napoleon who said that
Words: 13:the enthusiasm of others abated his own. At any rate, the spirit with
Words: 13:which each speaker entered upon his duty for the evening, abated my own
Words: 14:enthusiasm for the time being. The last day of our stay in Dundee, I
Words: 14:paid a visit, by invitation, to Dr. Dick, at his residence in the little
Words: 12:village of Broughty Ferry. We found the great astronomer in his parlour
Words: 15:waiting for us. From the parlour we went to the new study, and here I
Words: 15:felt more at ease, for I went to see the Philosopher in his study, and
Words: 14:not in his drawing-room. But even this room had too much the look of
Words: 13:nicety to be an author's _sanctum_; and I inquired and was soon informed
Words: 13:by Mrs. Dick, that I should have a look at the "_old study_."
Words: 0:
Words: 13:During a sojourn of eighteen months in Great Britain, I have had the
Words: 10:good fortune to meet with several distinguished literary characters, and
Words: 13:have always managed, while at their places of abode, to see the table
Words: 13:and favourite chair. Wm. and Ellen Craft were seeing what they could see
Words: 12:through a microscope, when Mrs. Dick returned to the room, and intimated
Words: 13:that we could now see the old literary workshop. I followed, and was
Words: 13:soon in a room about fifteen feet square, with but one window, which
Words: 14:occupied one side of the room. The walls of the other three sides were
Words: 12:lined with books. And many of these looked the very personification of
Words: 15:age. I took my seat in the "_old arm chair_;" and here, thought I, is
Words: 12:the place and the seat in which this distinguished man sat, while
Words: 13:weaving the radiant wreath of renown which now in his old age surrounds
Words: 13:him, and whose labours will be more appreciated by future ages than the
Words: 1:present.
Words: 0:
Words: 15:I took a farewell of the author of the "Solar System," but not until I
Words: 12:had taken a look through the great telescope in the observatory. This
Words: 13:instrument, through which I tried to see the heavens, was not the one
Words: 11:invented by Galileo, but an improvement upon the original. On leaving
Words: 15:this learned man, he shook hands with us, and bade us "God speed" in our
Words: 13:mission; and I left the philosopher, feeling I had not passed an hour
Words: 12:more agreeably, with a literary character, since the hour which I spent
Words: 12:with Poet Montgomery a few months since. And, by-the-bye, there is a
Words: 10:resemblance between the poet and the philosopher. In becoming acquainted
Words: 15:with great men, I have become a convert to the opinion, that a big nose
Words: 14:is an almost necessary appendage to the form of a man with a giant
Words: 13:intellect. If those whom I have seen be a criterion, such is certainly
Words: 13:the case. But I have spun out this too long, and must close.

Words: 2:LETTER XV.
Words: 0:
Words: 7:_Melrose Abbey--Abbotsford--Dryburgh Abbey--The Grave of Sir Walter
Words: 5:Scott--Hawick--Gretna Green--Visit to the Lakes._
Words: 0:
Words: 0:
Words: 4:                    YORK, _March 26, 1851_.
Words: 0:
Words: 15:I closed my last letter in the ancient town of Melrose, on the banks of
Words: 13:the Tweed, and within a stone's throw of the celebrated ruins from which
Words: 13:the town derives its name. The valley in which Melrose is situated, and
Words: 11:the surrounding hills, together with the Monastery, have so often been
Words: 13:made a theme for the Scottish bards, that this has become the most
Words: 12:interesting part of Scotland. Of the many gifted writers who have taken
Words: 14:up the pen, none have done more to bring the Eildon Hills and Melrose
Words: 13:Abbey into note, than the author of "Waverley." But who can read his
Words: 13:writings without a regret, that he should have so woven fact and fiction
Words: 11:together, that it is almost impossible to discriminate between the one
Words: 3:and the other.
Words: 0:
Words: 13:We arrived at Melrose in the evening, and proceeded to the chapel where
Words: 13:our meeting was to be held, and where our friends, the Crafts, were
Words: 12:warmly greeted. On returning from the meeting, we passed close by the
Words: 12:ruins of Melrose, and, very fortunately, it was a moonlight night. There
Words: 11:is considerable difference of opinion among the inhabitants of the place
Words: 15:as regards the best time to view the Abbey. The author of the "Lay of
Words: 4:the Last Minstrel," says:--
Words: 0:
Words: 7:   "If thou wouldst view fair Melrose aright,
Words: 7:    Go visit it by the pale moonlight:
Words: 7:    For the gay beams of lightsome day
Words: 7:    Gild but to flout the ruins gray."
Words: 0:
Words: 0:
Words: 11:In consequence of this admonition, I was informed that many persons
Words: 14:remain in town to see the ruins by moonlight. Aware that the moon did
Words: 15:not send its rays upon the old building every night in the year, I asked
Words: 15:the keeper what he did on dark nights. He replied that he had a large
Words: 15:lantern, which he put upon the end of a long pole, and with this he
Words: 12:succeeded in lighting up the ruins. This good man laboured hard to
Words: 13:convince me that his invention was nearly, if not quite as good, as
Words: 13:Nature's own moon. But having no need of an application of his invention
Words: 14:to the Abbey, I had no opportunity of judging of its effect. I thought,
Words: 14:however, that he had made a moon to some purpose, when he informed me
Words: 14:that some nights, with his pole and lantern, he earned his four or five
Words: 13:shillings. Not being content with a view by "moonlight alone," I was up
Words: 14:the next morning before the sun, and paid my respects to the Abbey. I
Words: 14:was too early for the keeper, and he handed me the key through the
Words: 13:window, and I entered the rooms alone. It is one labyrinth of gigantic
Words: 11:arches and dilapidated halls, the ivy growing and clinging wherever it
Words: 14:can fasten its roots, and the whole as fine a picture of decay as
Words: 11:imagination could create. This was the favourite resort of Sir Walter
Words: 13:Scott, and furnished him much matter for the "Lay of the Last Minstrel."
Words: 12:He could not have selected a more fitting place for solitary thought
Words: 12:than this ancient abode of monks and priests. In passing through the
Words: 12:cloisters, I could not but remark the carvings of leaves and flowers,
Words: 13:wrought in stone in the most exquisite manner, looking as fresh as if
Words: 13:they were just from the hands of the artist. The lapse of centuries
Words: 12:seems not to have made any impression upon them, or changed their
Words: 14:appearance in the least. I sat down among the ruins of the Abbey. The
Words: 10:ground about was piled up with magnificent fragments of stone,
Words: 11:representing various texts of Scripture, and the quaint ideas of the
Words: 13:priests and monks of that age. Scene after scene swept through my fancy
Words: 13:as I looked upon the surrounding objects. I could almost imagine I saw
Words: 14:the bearded monks going from hall to hall, and from cell to cell. In
Words: 13:visiting these dark cells, the mind becomes oppressed by a sense of the
Words: 11:utter helplessness of the victims who once passed over the thresholds
Words: 13:and entered these religious prisons. There was no help or hope but in
Words: 14:the will that ordered their fate. How painful it is to gaze upon these
Words: 14:walls, and to think how many tears have been shed by their inmates, when
Words: 15:this old Monastery was in its glory. I ascended to the top of the ruin
Words: 13:by a circuitous stairway, whose stone steps were worn deep from use by
Words: 13:many who, like myself, had visited them to gratify a curiosity. From the
Words: 14:top of the Abbey, I had a splendid view of the surrounding hills and
Words: 12:the beautiful valley through which flows the Gala Water and Tweed. This
Words: 9:is unquestionably the most splendid specimen of Gothic architectural
Words: 14:ruin in Scotland. But any description of mine conveys but a poor idea to
Words: 9:the fancy. To be realized, it must be seen.
Words: 0:
Words: 13:During the day, we paid a visit to Abbotsford, the splendid mansion of
Words: 13:the late Sir Walter Scott, Bart. This beautiful seat is situated on the
Words: 15:banks of the Tweed, just below its junction with the Gala Water. It is a
Words: 13:dreary looking spot, and the house from the opposite side of the river
Words: 13:has the appearance of a small, low castle. In a single day's ride
Words: 11:through England, one may see half a dozen cottages larger than
Words: 10:Abbotsford House. I was much disappointed in finding the premises
Words: 11:undergoing repairs and alterations, and that all the trees between the
Words: 15:house and the river had been cut down. This is to be regretted the more,
Words: 13:because they were planted, nearly every one of them, by the same hand
Words: 13:that waved its wand of enchantment over the world. The fountain had been
Words: 15:removed from where it had been placed by the hands of the Poet to the
Words: 15:centre of the yard; and even a small stone that had been placed over the
Words: 12:favourite dog "Percy," had been taken up and thrown among some loose
Words: 13:stones. One visits Abbotsford because of the genius of the man that once
Words: 11:presided over it. Everything connected with the great Poet is of
Words: 11:interest to his admirers, and anything altered or removed, tends to
Words: 11:diminish that interest. We entered the house, and were conducted through

Words: 13:There was the broad skirted blue coat, with its large buttons, the plaid
Words: 13:trousers, the heavy shoes, the black vest and white hat. These were all
Words: 15:in a glass case, and all looked the poet and novelist. But the inside of

Words: 13:dejected, and seemed as if she longed to leave this secluded spot and

Words: 12:plant. I don't think the Scotch could do better than to purchase
Words: 12:Abbotsford, while it has some imprint of the great magician, and secure
Words: 13:its preservation; for I am sure that, a hundred years hence, no place
Words: 13:will be more frequently visited in Scotland than the home of the late
Words: 11:Sir Walter Scott. After sauntering three hours about the premises, I
Words: 14:left, but not without feeling that I had been well paid for my trouble
Words: 3:in visiting Abbotsford.
Words: 0:
Words: 15:In the afternoon of the same day, in company with the Crafts, I took a
Words: 13:drive to Dryburgh Abbey. It is a ruin of little interest, except as
Words: 13:being the burial place of Scott. The poet lies buried in St. Mary's
Words: 14:Aisle. His grave is in the left transept of the cross, and close to
Words: 12:where the high altar formerly stood. Sir Walter Scott chose his own
Words: 14:grave, and he could not have selected a sunnier spot if he had roamed
Words: 15:the wide world over. A shaded window breaks the sun as it falls upon his

Words: 12:shelter the poet's grave from the weather. The author lies between his
Words: 14:wife and eldest son, and there is only room enough for one grave more,
Words: 11:and the son's wife has the choice of being buried here.
Words: 0:
Words: 14:The four o'clock train took us to Hawick; and after a pleasant visit in
Words: 10:this place, and the people registering their names against American

Words: 5:as smooth as a mirror.
Words: 0:

Words: 17:I know not how it is with others, but for my own part, I always form an

Words: 0:
Words: 12:A series of public meetings, one pressing close upon the heel of

Words: 0:

Words: 0:
Words: 15:It is not my intention to lay open the scenes of domestic life at "The

Words: 9:in the East," "The Hour and the Man," &c.
Words: 0:
Words: 13:The dwelling is a cottage of moderate size, built after Miss M.'s own
Words: 15:plan, upon a rise of land from which it derives the name of "The Knoll."
Words: 14:The Library is the largest room in the building, and upon the walls of
Words: 13:it were hung some beautiful engravings and a continental map. On a long

Words: 16:past. One side of the room was taken up with a large case, filled with a
Words: 11:choice collection of books, and everything indicated that it was the
Words: 6:home of genius and of taste.
Words: 0:
Words: 14:The room usually occupied by Miss M., and where we found her on the
Words: 12:evening of our arrival, is rather small and lighted by two large

Words: 9:which she tried in vain to hide from us.
Words: 0:

Words: 13:fellow gained his foothold, and in all probability saved us both from a

Words: 14:resolved that I would never again yield my own judgment to that of any
Words: 6:one, not even to a donkey.
Words: 0:
Words: 12:It seems as if Nature has amused herself in throwing these mountains
Words: 14:together. From the top of the Loughrigg Fell, the eye loses its power in

Words: 0:
Words: 15:We did not see the inside of the house, on account of its being occupied
Words: 15:by a very eccentric man, who will not permit a woman to enter the house,
Words: 15:and it is said that he has been known to run when a female had

Words: 13:in part composed of ladies, we had to share their fate, and therefore
Words: 11:were prevented from seeing the interior of the Dove's Nest. The
Words: 15:exhibitor of such a man would be almost sure of a prize at the great
Words: 1:Exhibition.
Words: 0:
Words: 13:At the head of Grassmere Lake, and surrounded by a few cottages, stands
Words: 11:an old gray, antique-looking Parish Church, venerable with the lapse of
Words: 13:centuries, and the walls partly covered with ivy, and in the rear of
Words: 11:which is the parish burial-ground. After leaving the Dove's Nest, and
Words: 12:having a pleasant ride over the hills and between the mountains, and
Words: 14:just as the sun was disappearing behind them, we arrived at the gate of
Words: 11:Grassmere Church; and alighting and following Miss M., we soon found
Words: 12:ourselves standing over a grave, marked by a single stone, and that,
Words: 14:too, very plain, with a name deeply cut. This announced to us that we
Words: 12:were standing over the grave of William Wordsworth. He chose his own
Words: 14:grave, and often visited the spot before his death. He lies in the most
Words: 12:sequestered spot in the whole grounds, and the simplicity and beauty of
Words: 17:the place was enough to make one in love with it, to be laid so far from
Words: 15:the bustle of the world, and in so sweet a place. The more one becomes
Words: 13:acquainted with the literature of the old world, the more he must love
Words: 14:her poets. Among the teachers of men, none are more worthy of study than
Words: 13:the poets; and, as teachers, they should receive far more credit than is
Words: 13:yielded to them. No one can look back upon the lives of Dante,
Words: 11:Shakspere, Milton, Goethe, Cowper, and many others that we might name,
Words: 11:without being reminded of the sacrifices which they made for mankind,
Words: 12:and which were not appreciated until long after their deaths. We need
Words: 14:look no farther than our own country to find men and women wielding the
Words: 12:pen practically and powerfully for the right. It is acknowledged on all
Words: 12:hands in this country, that England has the greatest dead poets, and
Words: 12:America the greatest living ones. The poet and the true Christian have
Words: 14:alike a hidden life. Worship is the vital element of each. Poetry has in
Words: 14:it that kind of utility which good men find in their Bible, rather than
Words: 13:such convenience as bad men often profess to draw from it. It ennobles
Words: 9:the sentiments, enlarges the affections, kindles the imagination, and
Words: 16:gives to us the enjoyment of a life in the past, and in the future, as
Words: 14:well as in the present. Under its light and warmth, we wake from our
Words: 11:torpidity and coldness, to a sense of our capabilities. This impulse
Words: 12:once given, a great object is gained. Schiller has truly said, "Poetry
Words: 17:can be to a man, what love is to a hero. It can neither counsel him nor
Words: 16:smite him, nor perform any labour for him, but it can bring him up to be
Words: 15:a hero, can summon him to deeds, and arm him with strength for all he
Words: 14:ought to be." I have often read with pleasure the sweet poetry of our
Words: 13:own Whitfield of Buffalo, which has appeared from time to time in the
Words: 14:columns of the _North Star_. I have always felt ashamed of the fact that
Words: 14:he should be compelled to wield the razor instead of the pen for a
Words: 12:living. Meaner poets than James M. Whitfield, are now living by their
Words: 12:compositions; and were he a white man he would occupy a different
Words: 1:position.
Words: 0:
Words: 12:After remaining a short time, and reading the epitaphs of the departed,
Words: 13:we again returned to "The Knoll." Nothing can be more imposing than the
Words: 12:beauty of English park scenery, and especially in the vicinity of the
Words: 11:lakes. Magnificent lawns that extend like sheets of vivid green, with
Words: 13:here and there a sprinkling of fine trees, heaping up rich piles of
Words: 13:foliage, and then the forests with the hare, the deer, and the rabbit,

Words: 11:picturesque beauty to English scenery that is unknown in the United
Words: 12:States. The very labourer with his thatched cottage and narrow slip of

Words: 12:against the wall, and hanging its blossoms about the windows, and the

Words: 14:the day upon his back--all this tells us of the happiness both of rich
Words: 14:and poor in this country. And yet there are those who would have the
Words: 13:world believe that the labourer of England is in a far worse condition
Words: 12:than the slaves of America. Such persons know nothing of the real
Words: 13:condition of the working classes of this country. At any rate, the poor
Words: 17:here, as well as the rich, are upon a level, as far as the laws of the

Words: 14:people, the more one has to admire them. They are so different from the
Words: 11:people of our own country. Hospitality, frankness, and good humour, are
Words: 14:always to be found in an Englishman. After a ramble of three days about

Words: 4:quitted the lake district.

Words: 2:LETTER XVII.
Words: 0:
Words: 6:_A Day in the Crystal Palace._
Words: 0:
Words: 0:
Words: 4:                    LONDON, _June 27th, 1851_.
Words: 0:
Words: 13:Presuming that you will expect from me some account of the great World's
Words: 15:Fair, I take my pen to give you my own impressions, although I am afraid
Words: 14:that anything which I may say about this "Lion of the day," will fall
Words: 14:far short of a description. On Monday last, I quitted my lodgings at an
Words: 13:early hour, and started for the Crystal Palace. This day was fine, such
Words: 12:as we seldom experience in London, with a clear sky, and invigorating
Words: 14:air, whose vitality was as rousing to the spirits as a blast from the
Words: 13:"horn of Astolpho." Although it was not yet 10 o'clock when I entered
Words: 12:Piccadilly, every omnibus was full, inside and out, and the street was
Words: 14:lined with one living stream, as far as the eye could reach, all wending
Words: 12:their way to the "Glass-House." No metropolis in the world presents such
Words: 11:facilities as London for the reception of the Great Exhibition, now
Words: 11:collected within its walls. Throughout its myriads of veins, the stream
Words: 12:of industry and toil pulses with sleepless energy. Every one seems to
Words: 13:feel that this great Capital of the world, is the fittest place wherein
Words: 14:they might offer homage to the dignity of toil. I had already begun to
Words: 12:feel fatigued by my pedestrian excursion as I passed "Apsley House," the
Words: 11:residence of the Duke of Wellington, and emerged into Hyde Park.
Words: 0:
Words: 16:I had hoped that on getting into the Park, I would be out of the crowd
Words: 14:that seemed to press so heavily in the street. But in this I was
Words: 11:mistaken. I here found myself surrounded by and moving with an
Words: 12:overwhelming mass, such as I had never before witnessed. And, away in
Words: 13:the distance, I beheld a dense crowd, and above every other object, was
Words: 14:seen the lofty summit of the Crystal Palace. The drive in the Park was
Words: 9:lined with princely-looking vehicles of every description. The drivers
Words: 13:in their bright red and gold uniforms, the pages and footmen in their
Words: 12:blue trousers and white silk stockings, and the horses dressed up in
Words: 10:their neat, silver-mounted harness, made the scene altogether one of
Words: 13:great splendour. I was soon at the door, paid my shilling, and entered
Words: 14:the building at the south end of the Transept. For the first ten or
Words: 12:twenty minutes I was so lost in astonishment, and absorbed in pleasing
Words: 15:wonder, that I could do nothing but gaze up and down the vista of the
Words: 10:noble building. The Crystal Palace resembles in some respects, the
Words: 13:interior of the cathedrals of this country. One long avenue from east to
Words: 12:west is intersected by a Transept, which divides the building into two
Words: 12:nearly equal parts. This is the greatest building the world ever saw,
Words: 12:before which the Pyramids of Egypt, and the Colossus of Rhodes must
Words: 13:hide their diminished heads. The palace was not full at any time during
Words: 12:the day, there being only 64,000 persons present. Those who love to
Words: 11:study the human countenance in all its infinite varieties, can find
Words: 14:ample scope for the indulgence of their taste, by a visit to the World's
Words: 7:Fair. All countries are there represented--Europeans, Asiatics,
Words: 9:Americans and Africans, with their numerous subdivisions. Even the
Words: 12:exclusive Chinese, with his hair braided, and hanging down his back, has
Words: 13:left the land of his nativity, and is seen making long strides through
Words: 11:the Crystal Palace, in his wooden-bottomed shoes. Of all places of
Words: 10:curious costumes and different fashions, none has ever yet presented
Words: 15:such a variety as this Exhibition. No dress is too absurd to be worn in
Words: 2:this place.
Words: 0:
Words: 13:There is a great deal of freedom in the Exhibition. The servant who
Words: 12:walks behind his mistress through the Park feels that he can crowd
Words: 12:against her in the Exhibition. The Queen and the day labourer, the
Words: 13:Prince and the merchant, the peer and the pauper, the Celt and the
Words: 14:Saxon, the Greek and the Frank, the Hebrew and the Russ, all meet here
Words: 11:upon terms of perfect equality. This amalgamation of rank, this kindly
Words: 10:blending of interests, and forgetfulness of the cold formalities of
Words: 13:ranks and grades, cannot but be attended with the very best results. I
Words: 14:was pleased to see such a goodly sprinkling of my own countrymen in the
Words: 8:Exhibition--I mean coloured men and women--well-dressed, and moving
Words: 10:about with their fairer brethren. This, some of our pro-slavery
Words: 15:Americans did not seem to relish very well. There was no help for it. As
Words: 13:I walked through the American part of the Crystal Palace, some of our

Words: 14:as an English lady was leaning on my arm. But their sneering looks did
Words: 13:not disturb me in the least. I remained the longer in their department,
Words: 12:and criticised the bad appearance of their goods the more. Indeed, the
Words: 12:Americans, as far as appearance goes, are behind every other country in
Words: 12:the Exhibition. The "Greek Slave" is the only production of Art which
Words: 14:the United States has sent. And it would have been more to their credit
Words: 15:had they kept that at home. In so vast a place as the Great Exhibition
Words: 14:one scarcely knows what to visit first, or what to look upon last. After
Words: 13:wandering about through the building for five hours, I sat down in one
Words: 13:of the galleries and looked at the fine marble statue of Virginius, with
Words: 15:the knife in his hand and about to take the life of his beloved and
Words: 12:beautiful daughter, to save her from the hands of Appius Claudius. The

Words: 12:statues in the long avenue. Large statues of Lords Eldon and Stowell,
Words: 12:carved out of solid marble, each weighing above twenty tons, are among
Words: 6:the most gigantic in the building.
Words: 0:
Words: 13:I was sitting with my 400 paged guide-book before me, and looking down

Words: 12:slippers. But oddness in dress, is one of the characteristics of the
Words: 2:Great Exhibition.
Words: 0:
Words: 12:Among the many things in the Crystal Palace, there are some which
Words: 11:receive greater attention than others, around which may always be seen
Words: 13:large groups of the visitors. The first of these is the Koh-i-noor, the
Words: 12:"Mountain of Light." This is the largest and most valuable diamond in
Words: 13:the world, said to be worth £2,000,000 sterling. It is indeed a great
Words: 13:source of attraction to those who go to the Exhibition for the first
Words: 11:time, but it is doubtful whether it obtains such admiration afterwards.
Words: 14:We saw more than one spectator turn away with the idea that after all

Words: 11:precious jewel, and although in a brass-grated cage, strong enough to
Words: 17:hold a lion, I found it to be no larger than the third of a hen's egg.
Words: 9:Two policemen remain by its side day and night.
Words: 0:
Words: 13:The finest thing in the Exhibition, is the "Veiled Vestal," a statue of
Words: 15:a woman carved in marble, with a veil over her face, and so neatly done,
Words: 15:that it looks as if it had been thrown over after it was finished. The
Words: 12:Exhibition presents many things which appeal to the eye and touch the
Words: 12:heart, and altogether, it is so decorated and furnished, as to excite
Words: 8:the dullest mind, and satisfy the most fastidious.
Words: 0:
Words: 9:England has contributed the most useful and substantial articles;
Words: 11:France, the most beautiful; while Russia, Turkey, and the West Indies,
Words: 13:seem to vie with each other in richness. China and Persia are not
Words: 11:behind. Austria has also contributed a rich and beautiful stock. Sweden,
Words: 12:Norway, Denmark, and the smaller states of Europe, have all tried to
Words: 11:outdo themselves in sending goods to the World's Fair. In Machinery,
Words: 12:England has no competitor. In Art, France is almost alone in the
Words: 4:Exhibition, setting aside England.
Words: 0:
Words: 10:In natural productions and provisions, America stands alone in her
Words: 13:glory. There lies her pile of canvassed hams; whether they were wood or
Words: 14:real, we could not tell. There are her barrels of salt, beef, and pork,
Words: 11:her beautiful white lard, her Indian-corn and corn-meal, her rice and

Words: 11:contributors from the United States seemed to have forgotten that this
Words: 13:was an exhibition of Art, or they most certainly would not have sent
Words: 11:provisions. But the United States takes the lead in the contributions,
Words: 12:as no other country has sent in provisions. The finest thing contributed
Words: 14:by our countrymen, is a large piece of silk with an eagle painted upon
Words: 6:it, surrounded by stars and stripes.
Words: 0:
Words: 13:After remaining more than five hours in the great temple, I turned my
Words: 13:back upon the richly laden stalls and left the Crystal Palace. On my
Words: 13:return home I was more fortunate than in the morning, inasmuch as I
Words: 15:found a seat for my friend and myself in an omnibus. And even my ride
Words: 13:in the close omnibus was not without interest. For I had scarcely taken
Words: 13:my seat, when my friend, who was seated opposite me, with looks and
Words: 12:gesture informed me that we were in the presence of some distinguished
Words: 12:person. I eyed the countenances of the different persons, but in vain,
Words: 15:to see if I could find any one who by his appearance showed signs of
Words: 11:superiority over his fellow-passengers. I had given up the hope of
Words: 12:selecting the person of note when another look from my friend directed
Words: 14:my attention to a gentlemen seated in the corner of the omnibus. He was
Words: 12:a tall man with strongly marked features, hair dark and coarse. There
Words: 13:was a slight stoop of the shoulder--that bend which is almost always a
Words: 11:characteristic of studious men. But he wore upon his countenance a
Words: 12:forbidding and disdainful frown, that seemed to tell one that he thought
Words: 14:himself better than those about him. His dress did not indicate a man of
Words: 15:high rank; and had we been in America, I would have taken him for an
Words: 2:Ohio farmer.
Words: 0:
Words: 11:While I was scanning the features and general appearance of the
Words: 12:gentleman, the Omnibus stopped and put down three or four of the
Words: 13:passengers, which gave me an opportunity of getting a seat by the side
Words: 14:of my friend, who, in a low whisper, informed me that the gentleman whom
Words: 14:I had been eyeing so closely, was no less a person than Thomas Carlyle.
Words: 13:I had read his "Hero-worship," and "Past and Present," and had formed a
Words: 12:high opinion of his literary abilities. But his recent attack upon the
Words: 11:emancipated people of the West Indies, and his laborious article in
Words: 12:favour of the re-establishment of the lash and slavery, had created in
Words: 15:my mind a dislike for the man, and I almost regretted that we were in
Words: 14:the same Omnibus. In some things, Mr. Carlyle is right: but in many, he
Words: 12:is entirely wrong. As a writer, Mr. Carlyle is often monotonous and
Words: 12:extravagant. He does not exhibit a new view of nature, or raise
Words: 8:insignificant objects into importance, but generally takes commonplace
Words: 12:thoughts and events, and tries to express them in stronger and statelier
Words: 12:language than others. He holds no communion with his kind, but stands
Words: 14:alone without mate or fellow. He is like a solitary peak, all access to
Words: 13:which is cut off. He exists not by sympathy but by antipathy. Mr.
Words: 13:Carlyle seems chiefly to try how he shall display his own powers, and
Words: 11:astonish mankind, by starting new trains of speculation or by expressing
Words: 16:old ones so as not to be understood. He cares little what he says, so as
Words: 14:he can say it differently from others. To read his works, is one thing;
Words: 13:to understand them, is another. If any one thinks that I exaggerate, let
Words: 15:him sit for an hour over "Sartor Resartus," and if he does not rise from
Words: 14:its pages, place his three or four dictionaries on the shelf, and say I
Words: 14:am right, I promise never again to say a word against Thomas Carlyle. He
Words: 14:writes one page in favour of Reform, and ten against it. He would hang
Words: 14:all prisoners to get rid of them, yet the inmates of the prisons and
Words: 13:"work-houses are better off than the poor." His heart is with the poor;
Words: 14:yet the blacks of the West Indies should be taught, that if they will
Words: 13:not raise sugar and cotton by their own free will, "Quashy should have
Words: 12:the whip applied to him." He frowns upon the Reformatory speakers upon
Words: 14:the boards of Exeter Hall, yet he is the prince of reformers. He hates
Words: 12:heroes and assassins, yet Cromwell was an angel, and Charlotte Corday a
Words: 14:saint. He scorns everything, and seems to be tired of what he is by
Words: 15:nature, and tries to be what he is not. But you will ask, what has
Words: 14:Thomas Carlyle to do with a visit to the Crystal Palace? My only reply
Words: 14:is, "Nothing," and if my remarks upon him have taken up the space that
Words: 13:should have been devoted to the Exhibition, and what I have written not
Words: 14:prove too burdensome to read, my next will be "a week in the Crystal
Words: 1:Palace."

Words: 2:LETTER XVIII.
Words: 0:
Words: 7:_The London Peace Congress--Meeting of Fugitive Slaves--Temperance
Words: 5:Demonstration--The Great Exhibition: last visit._
Words: 0:
Words: 0:
Words: 3:                    LONDON, _August 20_.
Words: 0:
Words: 13:The past six weeks have been of a stirring nature in this great
Words: 10:metropolis. It commenced with the Peace Congress, the proceedings of
Words: 12:which have long since reached you. And although that event has passed
Words: 17:off, it may not be out of place here to venture a remark or two upon its
Words: 1:deliberations.
Words: 0:
Words: 14:A meeting upon the subject of Peace, with the support of the monied and
Words: 11:influential men who rally around the Peace standard, could scarcely have
Words: 12:been held in Exeter Hall without creating some sensation. From all parts
Words: 11:of the world flocked delegates to this practical protest against war.
Words: 13:And among those who took part in the proceedings, were many men whose
Words: 11:names alone would, even on ordinary occasions, have filled the great
Words: 11:hall. The speakers were chosen from among the representatives of the
Words: 11:various countries, without regard to dialect or complexion; and the only
Words: 11:fault which seemed to be found with the Committee's arrangement was,
Words: 12:that in their desire to get foreigners and Londoners, they forgot the
Words: 12:country delegates, so that none of the large provincial towns were at
Words: 11:all represented in the Congress, so far as speaking was concerned.
Words: 10:Manchester, Leeds, Newcastle, and all the important towns in Scotland
Words: 13:and Ireland, were silenced in the great meeting. I need not say that
Words: 14:this was an oversight of the Committee, and one, too, that has done some
Words: 13:injury. Such men as the able Chairman of the late Anti-Corn Law League,
Words: 12:cannot be forgotten in such a meeting, without giving offence to those
Words: 11:who sent him, especially when the Committee brought forward, day after
Words: 10:day, the same speakers, chosen from amongst the metropolitan delegation.
Words: 12:However, the meeting was a glorious one, and will long be remembered
Words: 12:with delight as a step onward in the cause of Peace. Burritt's
Words: 11:Brotherhood Bazaar followed close upon the heels of the Peace Congress;
Words: 11:and this had scarcely closed, when that ever-memorable meeting of the
Words: 10:American Fugitive Slaves took place in the Hall of Commerce.
Words: 0:
Words: 11:The Temperance people made the next reformatory move. This meeting took
Words: 13:place in Exeter Hall, and was made up of delegates from the various
Words: 13:towns in the kingdom. They had come from the North, East, West, and
Words: 12:South. There was the quick-spoken son of the Emerald Isle, with his
Words: 12:pledge suspended from his neck; there, too, the Scot, speaking his broad
Words: 10:dialect; also the representatives from the provincial towns of England
Words: 10:and Wales, who seemed to speak anything but good English.
Words: 0:
Words: 12:The day after the meeting had closed in Exeter Hall, the country
Words: 10:societies, together with those of the metropolis, assembled in Hyde
Words: 13:Park, and then walked to the Crystal Palace. Their number while going to
Words: 11:the Exhibition, was variously estimated at from 15,000 to 20,000, and
Words: 11:was said to have been the largest gathering of Teetotalers ever
Words: 10:assembled in London. They consisted chiefly of the working classes,
Words: 8:their wives and children--clean, well-dressed and apparently happy:
Words: 11:their looks indicating in every way those orderly habits which, beyond
Words: 10:question, distinguish the devotees of that cause above the common
Words: 11:labourers of this country. On arriving at the Exhibition, they soon
Words: 10:distributed themselves among the departments, to revel in its various
Words: 11:wonders, eating their own lunch, and drinking from the Crystal Fountain.
Words: 0:
Words: 15:And now I am at the world's wonder, I will remain here until I finish
Words: 12:this sheet. I have spent fifteen days in the Exhibition, and have
Words: 11:conversed with those who have spent double that number amongst its
Words: 12:beauties, and the general opinion appears to be, that six months would
Words: 15:not be too long to remain within its walls to enable one to examine its
Words: 12:laden stalls. Many persons make the Crystal Palace their home, with the
Words: 13:exception of night. I have seen them come in the morning, visit the
Words: 11:dressing-room, then go to the refreshment room, and sit down to
Words: 14:breakfast as if they had been at their hotel. Dinner and tea would be
Words: 3:taken in turn.
Words: 0:
Words: 12:The Crystal Fountain is the great place of meeting in the Exhibition.
Words: 12:There you may see husbands looking for lost wives, wives for stolen
Words: 10:husbands, mothers for their lost children, and towns-people for their
Words: 12:country friends; and unless you have an appointment at a certain place
Words: 14:at an hour, you might as well prowl through the streets of London to
Words: 14:find a friend, as in the Great Exhibition. There is great beauty in the
Words: 11:"Glass House." Here, in the transept, with the glorious sunlight coming
Words: 11:through that wonderful glass roof, may the taste be cultivated and
Words: 9:improved, the mind edified, and the feelings chastened. Here,
Words: 13:surrounded by noble creations in marble and bronze, and in the midst of
Words: 12:an admiring throng, one may gaze at statuary which might fitly decorate
Words: 8:the house of the proudest prince in Christendom.
Words: 0:
Words: 14:He who takes his station in the gallery, at either end, and looks upon
Words: 11:that wondrous nave, or who surveys the matchless panorama around him
Words: 12:from the intersection of the nave and transept, may be said, without
Words: 12:presumption or exaggeration, to see all the kingdoms of this world and
Words: 13:the glory of them. He sees not only a greater collection of fine
Words: 13:articles, but also a greater as well as more various assemblage of the
Words: 10:human race, than ever before was gathered under one roof.
Words: 0:
Words: 13:One of the beauties of this great international gathering is, that it is
Words: 12:not confined to rank or grade. The million toilers from mine, and
Words: 12:factory, and workshop, and loom, and office, and field, share with their
Words: 11:more wealthy neighbours the feast of reason and imagination spread out
Words: 4:in the Crystal Palace.
Words: 0:
Words: 12:It is strange indeed to see so many nations assembled and represented
Words: 14:on one spot of British ground. In short, it is one great theatre, with
Words: 11:thousands of performers, each playing his own part. England is there,
Words: 10:with her mighty engines toiling and whirring, indefatigable in her
Words: 10:enterprises to shorten labour. India spreads her glitter and paint.
Words: 11:France, refined and fastidious, is there every day, giving the last
Words: 12:touch to her picturesque group; and the other countries, each in their
Words: 14:turn, doing what they can to show off. The distant hum of thousands of
Words: 10:good humoured people, with occasionally a national anthem from some
Words: 12:gigantic organ, together with the noise of the machinery, seems to send
Words: 8:life into every part of the Crystal Palace.
Words: 0:
Words: 13:When you get tired of walking, you can sit down and write your
Words: 14:impressions, and there is the "post" to receive your letter, or if it be
Words: 13:Friday or Saturday, you may, if you choose, rest yourself by hearing a
Words: 11:lecture from Professor Anstead; and then before leaving take your last
Words: 13:look, and see something that you have not before seen. Every thing which
Words: 13:is old in cities, new in colonial life, splendid in courts, useful in
Words: 10:industry, beautiful in nature, or ingenious in invention, is there
Words: 12:represented. In one place we have the Bible translated into one hundred
Words: 11:and fifty languages; in another, we have saints and archbishops painted
Words: 14:on glass; in another, old palaces and the altars of a John Knox, a
Words: 13:Baxter, or some other divines of olden time. In the old Temple of
Words: 13:Delphi, we read that every state of the civilized world had its separate
Words: 11:treasury, where Herodotus, born two thousand years before his time, saw
Words: 13:and observed all kinds of prodigies in gold and silver, brass and iron,
Words: 14:and even in linen. The nations all met there on one common ground, and
Words: 13:the peace of the earth was not a little promoted by their common
Words: 13:interest in the sanctity and splendour of that shrine. As long as the
Words: 13:Exhibition lasts, and its memory endures, we hope and trust that it may
Words: 13:shed the same influence. With this hasty scrap, I take leave of the
Words: 2:Great Exhibition.
