Words: 2:LETTER XIX.
Words: 0:
Words: 8:_Oxford--Martyrs' Monument--Cost of the Burning of the Martyrs--The
Words: 6:Colleges--Dr. Pusey--Energy, the Secret of Success._
Words: 0:
Words: 0:
Words: 4:                    OXFORD, _September 10th, 1851_.
Words: 0:
Words: 14:I have just finished a short visit to the far famed city of Oxford,
Words: 13:which has not unaptly been styled the City of Palaces. Aside from this
Words: 13:being one of the principal seats of learning in the world, it is
Words: 10:distinguished alike for its religious and political changes in times
Words: 13:past. At one time it was the seat of Popery; at another, the
Words: 11:uncompromising enemy of Rome. Here the tyrant, Richard the Third, held
Words: 13:his court, and when James the First, and his son Charles the First,
Words: 13:found their capital too hot to hold them, they removed to their loyal
Words: 11:city of Oxford. The writings of the great Republicans were here
Words: 12:committed to the flames. At one time Popery sent Protestants to the
Words: 13:stake and faggot; at another, a Papist King found no favour with the
Words: 11:people. A noble monument now stands where Cranmer, Ridley, and Latimer,
Words: 11:proclaimed their sentiments and faith, and sealed them with their blood.
Words: 13:And now we read upon the Town Treasurer's book--for three loads of wood,
Words: 13:one load of faggots, one post, two chains and staples, to burn Ridley
Words: 14:and Latimer, £1 5s. 1d. Such is the information one gets by looking over
Words: 8:the records of books written three centuries ago.
Words: 0:
Words: 14:It was a beautiful day on which I arrived at Oxford, and instead of
Words: 14:remaining in my hotel, I sallied forth to take a survey of the beauties
Words: 13:of the city. I strolled into Christ Church Meadows, and there spent the
Words: 11:evening in viewing the numerous halls of learning which surround that
Words: 10:splendid promenade. And fine old buildings they are: centuries have
Words: 13:rolled over many of them, hallowing the old walls, and making them grey
Words: 13:with age. They have been for ages the chosen homes of piety and
Words: 11:philosophy. Heroes and scholars have gone forth from their studies here,
Words: 13:into the great field of the world, to seek their fortunes, and to
Words: 12:conquer and be conquered. As I surveyed the exterior of the different
Words: 13:Colleges, I could here and there see the reflection of the light from
Words: 13:the window of some student, who was busy at his studies, or throwing
Words: 14:away his time over some trashy novel, too many of which find their way
Words: 14:into the trunks or carpet bags of the young men on setting out for
Words: 14:College. As I looked upon the walls of these buildings, I thought as the
Words: 14:rough stone is taken from the quarry to the finisher, there to be made
Words: 13:into an ornament, so was the young mind brought here to be cultivated
Words: 12:and developed. Many a poor unobtrusive young man, with the appearance of
Words: 14:little or no ability, is here moulded into a hero, a scholar, a tyrant,
Words: 13:or a friend of humanity. I never look upon these monuments of education,
Words: 15:without a feeling of regret, that so few of our own race can find a
Words: 14:place within their walls. And this being the fact, I see more and more
Words: 12:the need of our people being encouraged to turn their attention more
Words: 10:seriously to self-education, and thus to take a respectable position
Words: 12:before the world, by virtue of their own cultivated minds and moral
Words: 1:standing.
Words: 0:
Words: 13:Education, though obtained by a little at a time, and that, too, over
Words: 13:the midnight lamp, will place its owner in a position to be respected
Words: 14:by all, even though he be black. I know that the obstacles which the
Words: 13:laws of the land, and of society, place between the coloured man and
Words: 13:education in the United States, are very great, yet if _one_ can break
Words: 13:through these barriers, more can; and if our people would only place the
Words: 10:right appreciation upon education, they would find these obstacles are
Words: 13:easier to be overcome than at first sight appears. A young man once
Words: 12:asked Carlyle, what was the secret of success. His reply was, "Energy;
Words: 14:whatever you undertake, do it with all your might." Had it not been for
Words: 14:the possession of energy, I might now have been working as a servant for
Words: 13:some brainless fellow who might be able to command my labour with his
Words: 13:money, or I might have been yet toiling in chains and slavery. But
Words: 15:thanks to energy, not only for my being to-day in a land of freedom, but
Words: 14:also for my dear girls being in one of the best seminaries in France,
Words: 13:instead of being in an American school, where the finger of scorn would
Words: 12:be pointed at them by those whose superiority rests entirely upon their
Words: 14:having a whiter skin. But I am straying too far from the purpose of
Words: 2:this letter.
Words: 0:
Words: 13:Oxford is indeed one of the finest located places in the kingdom, and
Words: 10:every inch of ground about it seems hallowed by interesting
Words: 10:associations. The University, founded by the good King Alfred, still
Words: 12:throws its shadow upon the side-walk; and the lapse of ten centuries
Words: 12:seems to have made but little impression upon it. Other seats of
Words: 11:learning may be entitled to our admiration, but Oxford claims our
Words: 12:veneration. Although the lateness of the night compelled me, yet I felt
Words: 11:an unwillingness to tear myself from the scene of such surpassing
Words: 14:interest. Few places in any country as noted as Oxford is, but what has
Words: 9:some distinguished person residing within its precincts. And knowing
Words: 14:that the City of Palaces was not an exception to this rule, I resolved
Words: 15:to see some of its lions. Here, of course, is the head quarters of the
Words: 12:Bishop of Oxford, a son of the late William Wilberforce, Africa's noble
Words: 12:champion. I should have been glad to have seen this distinguished pillar
Words: 14:of the Church, but I soon learned that the Bishop's residence was out of
Words: 13:town, and that he seldom visited the city except on business. I then
Words: 13:determined to see one who, although a lesser dignitary in the church, is
Words: 11:nevertheless, scarcely less known than the Bishop of Oxford. This was
Words: 13:the Rev. Dr. Pusey, a divine, whose name is known wherever the religion
Words: 12:of Jesus is known and taught, and the acknowledged head of the
Words: 12:Puseyites. On the second morning of my visit, I proceeded to Christ
Words: 11:Church Chapel, where the rev. gentleman officiates. Fortunately I had an
Words: 12:opportunity of seeing the Dr., and following close in his footsteps to
Words: 13:the church. His personal appearance is anything but that of one who is
Words: 14:the leader of a growing and powerful party in the church. He is rather
Words: 12:under the middle size, and is round shouldered, or rather stoops. His
Words: 13:profile is more striking than his front face, the nose being very large
Words: 14:and prominent. As a matter of course, I expected to see a large nose,
Words: 13:for all great men have them. He has a thoughtful, and somewhat sullen
Words: 13:brow, a firm and somewhat pensive mouth, a cheek pale, thin, and deeply
Words: 12:furrowed. A monk fresh from the cloisters of Tintern Abbey, in its
Words: 11:proudest days, could scarcely have made a more ascetic and solemn
Words: 12:appearance than did Dr. Pusey on this occasion. He is not apparently
Words: 13:above forty-five, or at most fifty years of age, and his whole aspect
Words: 12:renders him an admirable study for an artist. Dr. Pusey's style of
Words: 12:preaching is cold and tame, and one looking at him would scarcely
Words: 11:believe that such an apparently uninteresting man could cause such an
Words: 15:eruption in the Church as he has. I was glad to find that a coloured
Words: 8:young man was among the students at Oxford.
Words: 0:
Words: 13:A few months since, I paid a visit to our countryman, Alexander Crummel,
Words: 11:who is still pursuing his studies at Cambridge--a place, though much
Words: 14:inferior to Oxford as far as appearance is concerned, is yet said to be
Words: 13:greatly its superior as a place of learning. In an hour's walk through
Words: 13:the Strand, Regent, or Piccadilly Streets in London, one may meet half a
Words: 13:dozen coloured young men, who are inmates of the various Colleges in the
Words: 14:metropolis. These are all signs of progress in the cause of the sons of
Words: 13:Africa. Then let our people take courage, and with that courage let them
Words: 12:apply themselves to learning. A determination to excel is the sure road
Words: 16:to greatness, and that is as open to the black man as the white. It was
Words: 11:that which has accomplished the mightiest and noblest triumphs in the
Words: 12:intellectual and physical world. It was that which has made such rapid
Words: 10:strides towards civilization, and broken the chains of ignorance and
Words: 11:superstition, which have so long fettered the human intellect. It was
Words: 10:determination which raised so many worthy individuals from the humble
Words: 12:walks of society, and from poverty, and placed them in positions of
Words: 12:trust and renown. It is no slight barrier that can effectually oppose
Words: 9:the determination of the will--success must ultimately crown its
Words: 13:efforts. "The world shall hear of me," was the exclamation of one whose
Words: 11:name has become as familiar as household words. A Toussaint, once
Words: 12:laboured in the sugar field with his spelling-book in his pocket, amid
Words: 14:the combined efforts of a nation to keep him in ignorance. His name is
Words: 13:now recorded among the list of statesmen of the past. A Soulouque was
Words: 16:once a slave, and knew not how to read. He now sits upon the throne of
Words: 2:an Empire.
Words: 0:
Words: 14:In our own country, there are men who once held the plough, and that
Words: 11:too without any compensation, who are now presiding at the editor's
Words: 12:table. It was determination that brought out the genius of a Franklin,
Words: 12:and a Fulton, and that has distinguished many of the American Statesmen,
Words: 13:who but for their energy and determination would never have had a name
Words: 7:beyond the precincts of their own homes.
Words: 0:
Words: 13:It is not always those who have the best advantages, or the greatest
Words: 11:talents, that eventually succeed in their undertakings; but it is those
Words: 11:who strive with untiring diligence to remove all obstacles to success,
Words: 11:and who, with unconquerable resolution, labour on until the rich reward
Words: 13:of perseverance is within their grasp. Then again let me say to our
Words: 12:young men--Take courage; "There is a good time coming." The darkness of
Words: 10:the night appears greatest just before the dawn of day.

Words: 2:LETTER XX.
Words: 0:
Words: 4:_Fugitive Slaves in England._
Words: 0:
Words: 0:
Words: 14:The love of freedom is one of those natural impulses of the human breast
Words: 11:which cannot be extinguished. Even the brute animals of the creation
Words: 11:feel and show sorrow and affection when deprived of their liberty.
Words: 11:Therefore is a distinguished writer justified in saying, "Man is free,
Words: 13:even were he born in chains." The Americans boast, and justly, too, that
Words: 10:Washington was the hero and model patriot of the American
Words: 11:Revolution--the man whose fame, unequalled in his own day and country,
Words: 14:will descend to the end of time, the pride and honour of humanity. The
Words: 12:American speaks with pride of the battles of Lexington and Bunker Hill;
Words: 13:and when standing in Faneuil Hall, he points to the portraits of Otis,
Words: 11:Adams, Hancock, Quincy, Warren, and Franklin, and tells you that their
Words: 12:names will go down to posterity among the world's most devoted and
Words: 5:patriotic friends of human liberty.
Words: 0:
Words: 14:It was on the first of August, 1851, that a number of men, fugitives
Words: 13:from that boasted land of freedom, assembled at the Hall of Commerce in
Words: 13:the City of London, for the purpose of laying their wrongs before the
Words: 14:British nation, and at the same time, to give thanks to the God of
Words: 13:Freedom for the liberation of their West India brethren, on the first of
Words: 12:August, 1834. Little notice had been given of the intended meeting, yet
Words: 16:it seemed to be known in all parts of the city. At the hour of half-past
Words: 13:seven, for which the meeting had been called, the spacious hall was well
Words: 12:filled, and the fugitives, followed by some of the most noted English
Words: 10:Abolitionists, entered the hall, amid the most deafening applause, and
Words: 13:took their seats on the platform. The appearance of the great hall at
Words: 11:this juncture was most splendid. Besides the committee of fugitives, on
Words: 14:the platform there were a number of the oldest and most devoted of the
Words: 13:Slave's friends. On the left of the chair sat Geo. Thompson, Esq., M.P.;
Words: 15:near him was the Rev. Jabez Burns, D.D.; and by his side the Rev. John
Words: 12:Stevenson, M.A., Wm. Farmer, Esq., R. Smith, Esq.; while on the other
Words: 11:side were the Rev. Edward Mathews, John Cunliff, Esq., Andrew Paton,
Words: 12:Esq., J.P. Edwards, Esq., and a number of coloured gentlemen from the
Words: 12:West Indies. The body of the hall was not without its distinguished
Words: 11:guests. The Chapmans and Westons of Boston, U.S., were there. The
Words: 14:Estlins and Tribes had come all the way from Bristol to attend the great
Words: 13:meeting. The Patons of Glasgow had delayed their departure, so as to be
Words: 13:present. The Massies had come in from Upper Clapton. Not far from the
Words: 11:platform sat Sir Francis Knowles, Bart., still farther back was Samuel
Words: 14:Bowly, Esq., while near the door were to be seen the greatest critic of
Words: 12:the age, and England's best living poet. Macaulay had laid aside the
Words: 13:pen, entered the hall, and was standing near the central door, while not
Words: 10:far from the historian stood the newly-appointed Poet Laureat. The
Words: 14:author of "In Memoriam" had been swept in by the crowd, and was standing
Words: 13:with his arms folded, and beholding for the first time (and probably the
Words: 14:last) so large a number of coloured men in one room. In different parts
Words: 14:of the hall were men and women from nearly all parts of the kingdom,
Words: 13:besides a large number who, drawn to London by the Exhibition, had come
Words: 12:in to see and hear these oppressed people plead their own cause.
Words: 0:
Words: 12:The writer of this sketch was chosen Chairman of the meeting, and
Words: 10:commenced its proceedings by delivering the following address, which we
Words: 8:cut from the columns of the _Morning Advertiser_:--
Words: 0:
Words: 0:
Words: 10:"The Chairman, in opening the proceedings, remarked that, although the
Words: 10:metropolis had of late been inundated with meetings of various
Words: 11:character, having reference to almost every variety of subject, yet that
Words: 12:the subject they were called upon that evening to discuss differed from
Words: 13:them all. Many of those by whom he was surrounded, like himself, had
Words: 11:been victims to the inhuman institution of Slavery, and were in
Words: 11:consequence exiled from the land of their birth. They were fugitives
Words: 13:from their native land, but not fugitives from justice, and they had not
Words: 10:fled from a monarchical, but from a so-called republican government.
Words: 13:They came from amongst a people who declared, as part of their creed,
Words: 15:that all men were born free, but who, while they did so, made slaves of
Words: 13:every sixth man, woman, and child in the country (hear, hear). He must
Words: 13:not, however, forget that one of the purposes for which they were met
Words: 11:that night was to commemorate the emancipation of their brothers and
Words: 14:sisters in the isles of the sea. That act of the British Parliament, and
Words: 13:he might add in this case with peculiar emphasis, of the British nation,
Words: 15:passed on the 12th day of August, 1833, to take effect on the first day
Words: 11:of August, 1834, and which enfranchised 800,000 West Indian slaves, was
Words: 11:an event sublime in its nature, comprehensive and mighty in its
Words: 8:immediate influences and remote consequences, precious beyond expression
Words: 12:to the cause of freedom, and encouraging beyond the measure of any
Words: 13:government on earth to the hearts of all enlightened and just men. This
Words: 12:act was the commencement of a long course of philanthropic and Christian
Words: 14:efforts on the part of some of the best men that the world ever
Words: 13:produced. It was not his intention to go into a discussion or a
Words: 13:calculation of the rise and fall of property, or whether sugar was worth
Words: 13:more or less by the act of emancipation. But the abolition of Slavery
Words: 14:in the West Indies, was a blow struck in the right direction, at that
Words: 12:most inhuman of all traffics, the slave trade--a trade which would never
Words: 13:cease so long as slavery existed, for where there was a market there
Words: 12:would be merchandise; where there was demand there would be a supply;
Words: 12:where there were carcases there would be vultures; and they might as
Words: 15:well attempt to turn the water, and make it run up the Niagara river, as
Words: 14:to change this law. It was often said by the Americans that England was
Words: 10:responsible for the existence of slavery there, because it was
Words: 11:introduced into that country while the colonies were under the British
Words: 14:Crown. If that were the case, they must come to the conclusion that, as
Words: 12:England abolished Slavery in the West Indies, she would have done the
Words: 16:same for the American States if she had had the power to do it; and if
Words: 13:that was so, they might safely say that the separation of the United
Words: 13:States from the mother country was (to say the least) a great misfortune
Words: 13:to one-sixth of the population of that land. England had set a noble
Words: 12:example to America, and he would to heaven his countrymen would follow
Words: 11:the example. The Americans boasted of their superior knowledge, but they
Words: 14:needed not to boast of their superior guilt, for that was set upon a
Words: 14:hill top, and that too, so high, that it required not the lantern of
Words: 12:Diogenes to find it out. Every breeze from the western world brought
Words: 14:upon its wings the groans and cries of the victims of this guilt. Nearly
Words: 12:all countries had fixed the seal of disapprobation on slavery, and when,
Words: 14:at some future age, this stain on the page of history shall be pointed
Words: 10:at, posterity will blush at the discrepancy between American profession
Words: 13:and American practice. What was to be thought of a people boasting of
Words: 9:their liberty, their humanity, their Christianity, their love of
Words: 13:justice, and at the same time keeping in slavery nearly four millions of
Words: 13:God's children, and shutting out from them the light of the Gospel, by
Words: 12:denying the Bible to the slave! (Hear, hear.) No education, no marriage,
Words: 14:everything done to keep the mind of the slave in darkness. There was a
Words: 13:wish on the part of the people of the northern States to shield
Words: 12:themselves from the charge of slave-holding, but as they shared in the
Words: 13:guilt, he was not satisfied with letting them off without their share in
Words: 13:the odium. And now a word about the Fugitive Slave Bill. That measure
Words: 12:was in every respect an unconstitutional measure. It set aside the right
Words: 13:formerly enjoyed by the fugitive of trial by jury--it afforded to him no
Words: 12:protection, no opportunity of proving his right to be free, and it
Words: 11:placed every free coloured person at the mercy of any unprincipled
Words: 13:individual who might wish to lay claim to him. (Hear.) That law is
Words: 11:opposed to the principles of Christianity--foreign alike to the laws of
Words: 13:God and man, it had converted the whole population of the free States
Words: 13:into a band of slave-catchers, and every rood of territory is but so
Words: 12:much hunting ground, over which they might chase the fugitive. But while
Words: 13:they were speaking of slavery in the United States, they must not omit
Words: 13:to mention that there was a strong feeling in that land, not only
Words: 11:against the Fugitive Slave Law, but also against the existence of
Words: 15:slavery in any form. There was a band of fearless men and women in the
Words: 13:city of Boston, whose labours for the slave had resulted in good beyond
Words: 12:calculation. This noble and heroic class had created an agitation in the
Words: 11:whole country, until their principles have taken root in almost every
Words: 12:association in the land, and which, with God's blessing, will, in due
Words: 13:time, cause the Americans to put into practice what they have so long
Words: 12:professed. (Hear, hear.) He wished it to be continually held up before
Words: 12:the country, that the northern States are as deeply implicated in the
Words: 13:guilt of slavery as the South. The north had a population of 13,553,328
Words: 12:freemen; the south had a population of only 6,393,756 freemen; the north
Words: 14:has 152 representatives in the house, the south only 81; and it would be
Words: 13:seen by this, that the balance of power was with the free States.
Words: 13:Looking, therefore, at the question in all its aspects, he was sure that
Words: 14:there was no one in this country but who would find out, that the
Words: 13:slavery of the United States of America was a system the most abandoned
Words: 6:and the most tyrannical. (Hear, hear.)"
Words: 0:
Words: 12:At the close of this address, the Rev. Edward Matthews, last from
Words: 12:Bristol, but who had recently returned from the United States, where he
Words: 13:had been maltreated on account of his fidelity to the cause of freedom,
Words: 12:was introduced, and made a most interesting speech. The next speaker was
Words: 12:George Thompson, Esq., M.P.; and we need only say that his eloquence,
Words: 11:which has seldom or ever been equalled, and never surpassed, exceeded,
Words: 12:on this occasion, the most sanguine expectations of his friends. All who
Words: 10:sat under the thundering anathemas which he hurled against slavery,
Words: 10:seemed instructed, delighted, and animated. No one could scarcely have
Words: 10:remained unmoved by the pensive sympathies that pervaded the entire
Words: 13:assembly. There were many in the meeting who had never seen a fugitive
Words: 14:slave before, and when any of the speakers would refer to those on the
Words: 12:platform, the whole audience seemed moved to tears. No meeting of the
Words: 12:kind held in London for years created a greater sensation than this
Words: 14:gathering of refugees from the "Land of the free, and the home of the
Words: 12:brave." The following appeal, which I had written for the occasion, was
Words: 12:unanimously adopted at the close of the meeting, and thus ended the
Words: 5:great Anti-Slavery demonstration of 1851.
Words: 0:
Words: 0:
Words: 11:AN APPEAL TO THE PEOPLE OF GREAT BRITAIN AND THE WORLD.
Words: 0:
Words: 14:We consider it just, both to the people of the United States and to
Words: 11:ourselves, in making an appeal to the inhabitants of other countries,
Words: 14:against the laws which have exiled us from our native land, to state the
Words: 14:ground upon which we make our appeal, and the causes which impel us to
Words: 14:do so. There are in the United States of America, at the present time,
Words: 14:between three and four millions of persons, who are held in a state of
Words: 14:slavery which has no parallel in any other part of the world; and whose
Words: 11:numbers have, within the last fifty years, increased to a fearful
Words: 13:extent. These people are not only deprived of the rights to which the
Words: 12:laws of Nature and Nature's God entitle them, but every avenue to
Words: 12:knowledge is closed against them. The laws do not recognise the family
Words: 13:relation of a slave, and extend to him protection in the enjoyment of
Words: 8:domestic endearments. Brothers and sisters, parents and children,
Words: 12:husbands and wives, are torn asunder, and permitted to see each other
Words: 14:no more. The shrieks and agonies of the slave are heard in the markets
Words: 12:at the seat of government, and within hearing of the American Congress,
Words: 14:as well as on the cotton, sugar and rice plantations of the far South.
Words: 0:
Words: 13:The history of the negroes in America is but a history of repeated
Words: 13:injuries and acts of oppression committed upon them by the whites. It is
Words: 14:not for ourselves that we make this appeal, but for those whom we have
Words: 2:left behind.
Words: 0:
Words: 10:In their Declaration of Independence, the Americans declare that "all
Words: 12:men are created equal; that they are endowed by their Creator with
Words: 11:certain inalienable rights; that among these are life, liberty, and the
Words: 11:pursuit of happiness." Yet one-sixth of the inhabitants of the great
Words: 13:Republic are slaves. Thus they give the lie to their own professions. No
Words: 12:one forfeits his or her character or standing in society by being
Words: 13:engaged in holding, buying or selling a slave; the details of which, in
Words: 7:all their horror, can scarcely be told.
Words: 0:
Words: 12:Although the holding of slaves is confined to fifteen of the thirty-one
Words: 11:States, yet we hold that the non-slave-holding States are equally guilty
Words: 14:with the slave-holding. If any proof is needed on this point, it will be
Words: 13:found in the passage of the inhuman Fugitive Slave Law, by Congress; a
Words: 13:law which could never have been enacted without the votes of a portion
Words: 12:of the representatives from the free States, and which is now being
Words: 13:enforced, in many of the States, with the utmost alacrity. It was the
Words: 14:passing of this law that exiled us from our native land, and it has
Words: 12:driven thousands of our brothers and sisters from the free States, and
Words: 12:compelled them to seek a refuge in the British possessions in North
Words: 11:America. The Fugitive Slave Law has converted the entire country, North
Words: 11:and South, into one vast hunting-ground. We would respectfully ask you
Words: 12:to expostulate with the Americans, and let them know that you regard
Words: 13:their treatment of the coloured people of that country as a violation of
Words: 11:every principle of human brotherhood, of natural right, of justice, of
Words: 11:humanity, of Christianity, of love to God and love to man.
Words: 0:
Words: 13:It is needless that we should remind you that the religious sects of
Words: 11:America, with but few exceptions, are connected with the sin of
Words: 13:slavery--the churches North as well as South. We would have you tell the
Words: 12:professed Christians of that land, that if they would be respected by
Words: 12:you, they must separate themselves from the unholy alliance with men who
Words: 12:are daily committing deeds which, if done in England, would cause the
Words: 13:perpetrator to be sent to a felon's doom; that they must refuse the
Words: 8:right hand of Christian fellowship, whether individually or
Words: 12:collectively, to those implicated, in any way, in the guilt of slavery.
Words: 0:
Words: 14:We do not ask for a forcible interference on your part, but only that
Words: 13:you will use all lawful and peaceful means to restore to this much
Words: 10:injured race their God-given rights. The moral and religious sentiment
Words: 11:of mankind must be arrayed against slave-holding, to make it infamous,
Words: 16:ere we can hope to see it abolished. We would ask you to set them the
Words: 10:example, by excluding from your pulpits, and from religious communion,
Words: 11:the slave-holding and pro-slavery ministers who may happen to visit this
Words: 13:country. We would even go further, and ask you to shut your doors
Words: 12:against either ministers or laymen, who are at all guilty of upholding
Words: 13:and sustaining this monster sin. By the cries of the slave, which come
Words: 16:from the fields and swamps of the far South, we ask you to do this! By
Words: 13:that spirit of liberty and equality of which you all admire, we would
Words: 14:ask you to do this. And by that still nobler, higher, and holier spirit
Words: 15:of our beloved Saviour, we would ask you to stamp upon the head of the
Words: 13:slaveholder, with a brand deeper than that which marks the victim of his
Words: 10:wrongs, the infamy of theft, adultery, man-stealing, piracy, and murder,
Words: 13:and, by the force of public opinion, compel him to "unloose the heavy
Words: 7:burden, and let the oppressed go free."

Words: 0:
Words: 2:LETTER XXI.
Words: 0:
Words: 5:_A Chapter on American Slavery._
Words: 0:
Words: 0:
Words: 13:The word Englishman is but another name for an American, and the word
Words: 11:American is but another name for an Englishman--England is the father,
Words: 12:America the son. They have a common origin and identity of language;
Words: 12:they hold the same religious and political opinions; they study the same
Words: 10:histories, and have the same literature. Steam and mechanical ingenuity
Words: 12:have brought the two countries within nine days sailing of each other.
Words: 11:The Englishman on landing at New-York finds his new neighbours speaking
Words: 13:the same language which he last heard on leaving Liverpool, and he sees
Words: 14:the American in the same dress that he had been accustomed to look upon
Words: 13:at home, and soon forgets that he is three thousand miles from his
Words: 11:native land, and in another country. The American on landing at
Words: 11:Liverpool, and taking a walk through the great commercial city, finding
Words: 10:no difficulty in understanding the people, supposes himself still in
Words: 14:New-York; and if there seems any doubt in his own mind, growing out of
Words: 14:the fact that the people have a more healthy look, seem more polite, and
Words: 12:that the buildings have a more substantial appearance than those he had
Words: 13:formerly looked upon, he has only to imagine, as did Rip Van Winkle,
Words: 8:that he has been asleep these hundred years.
Words: 0:
Words: 13:If the Englishman who has seen a Thompson silenced in Boston, or a
Words: 11:Macready mobbed in New-York, upon the ground that they were foreigners,
Words: 13:should sit in Exeter Hall and hear an American orator until he was
Words: 12:hoarse, and wonder why the American is better treated in England than
Words: 13:the Englishman in America, he has only to attribute it to John Bull's
Words: 12:superior knowledge of good manners, and his being a more law-abiding man
Words: 12:than brother Jonathan. England and America has each its reforms and its
Words: 13:reformers, and they have more or less sympathy with each other. It has
Words: 12:been said that one generation commences a reform in England, and that
Words: 13:another generation finishes it. I would that so much could be said with
Words: 12:regard to the great object of reform in America--the system of slavery!
Words: 0:
Words: 15:No evil was ever more deeply rooted in a country than is slavery in the
Words: 12:United States. Spread over the largest and most fertile States in the
Words: 12:Union, with decidedly the best climate, and interwoven, as it is, with
Words: 9:the religious, political, commercial, and social institutions of the
Words: 12:country, it is scarcely possible to estimate its influence. This is the
Words: 11:evil which claims the attention of American Reformers, over and above
Words: 13:every other evil in the land, and thanks to a kind providence, the
Words: 12:American slave is not without his advocates. The greatest enemy to the
Words: 11:Anti-Slavery Society, and the most inveterate opposer of the men whose
Words: 14:names stand at the head of the list as officers and agents of that
Words: 11:association, will, we think, assign to William Lloyd Garrison, the first
Words: 12:place in the ranks of the American Abolitionists. The first to proclaim
Words: 12:the doctrine of immediate emancipation to the slaves of America, and on
Words: 12:that account an object of hatred to the slave-holding interest of the
Words: 13:country, and living for years with his life in danger, he is justly
Words: 13:regarded by all, as the leader of the Anti-Slavery movement in the New
Words: 12:World. Mr. Garrison is at the present time but little more than
Words: 14:forty-five years of age, and of the middle size. He has a high and
Words: 13:prominent forehead, well developed, with no hair on the top of the head,
Words: 13:having lost it in early life; with a piercing eye, a pleasant, yet
Words: 10:anxious countenance, and of a most loveable disposition; tender, and
Words: 11:blameless in his family affections, devoted to his friends; simple and
Words: 8:studious, upright, guileless, distinguished, and worthy, like the
Words: 10:distinguished men of antiquity, to be immortalized by another Plutarch.
Words: 13:How many services never to be forgotten, has he not rendered to the
Words: 14:cause of the slave, and the welfare of mankind! As a speaker, he is
Words: 14:forcible, clear, and logical, yet he will not rank with the many who are
Words: 15:less known. As a writer, he is regarded as one of the finest in the
Words: 10:United States, and certainly the most prominent in the Anti-Slavery
Words: 12:cause. Had Mr. Garrison wished to serve himself, he might, with his
Words: 14:great talents, long since, have been at the head of either of the great
Words: 11:political parties. Few men can withstand the allurements of office, and
Words: 12:the prize-money that accompanies them. Many of those who were with him
Words: 12:fifteen years ago, have been swept down with the current of popular
Words: 15:favour, either in Church or State. He has seen a Cox on the one hand,
Words: 13:and a Stanton on the other, swept away like so much floating wood
Words: 12:before the tide. When the sturdiest characters gave way, when the finest
Words: 11:geniuses passed one after another under the yoke of slavery, Garrison
Words: 13:stood firm to his convictions, like a rock that stands stirless amid the
Words: 12:conflicting agitation of the waves. He is not only the friend and
Words: 14:advocate of freedom with his pen and his tongue, but to the oppressed of
Words: 15:every clime he opens his purse, his house, and his heart: yet he is not
Words: 14:a man of money. The fugitive slave, fresh from the whips and chains, who
Words: 12:is turned off by the politician, and experiences the cold shoulder of
Words: 14:the divine, finds a bed and a breakfast under the hospitable roof of Mr.
Words: 2:Lloyd Garrison.
Words: 0:
Words: 13:The party of which he is the acknowledged head, is one of no
Words: 11:inconsiderable influence in the United States. No man has more bitter
Words: 12:enemies or stauncher friends than he. There are those among his friends
Words: 13:who would stake their all upon his veracity and integrity; and we are
Words: 11:sure that the coloured people throughout America, bond and free, in
Words: 13:whose cause he has so long laboured, will, with one accord, assign the
Words: 10:highest niche in their affection to the champion of universal
Words: 12:emancipation. Every cause has its writers and its orators. We have drawn
Words: 12:a hasty and imperfect sketch of the greatest writer in the Anti-Slavery
Words: 11:field: we shall now call attention to the most distinguished public
Words: 12:speaker. The name of Wendell Phillips is but another name for eloquence.
Words: 12:Born in the highest possible position in America, Mr. Phillips has all
Words: 13:the advantages that birth can give to one in that country. Educated at
Words: 11:the first University, graduating with all the honours which the College
Words: 14:could bestow on him, and studying the law and becoming a member of the
Words: 13:bar, he has all the accomplishments that these advantages can give to a
Words: 14:man of a great mind. Nature has treated him as a favourite. His stature
Words: 10:is not tall, but handsome; his expressive countenance paints and
Words: 10:reflects every emotion of his soul. His gestures are wonderfully
Words: 13:graceful, like his delivery. There is a fascination in the soft gaze of
Words: 13:his eyes, which none can but admire. Being a great reader, and endowed
Words: 12:by nature with a good memory, he supplies himself with the most
Words: 10:complicated dates and historical events. Nothing can equal the variety
Words: 14:of his matter. I have heard him more than twenty different times on the
Words: 13:same subject, but never heard the same speech. He is personal, but there
Words: 11:is nothing offensive in his personalities. He extracts from a subject
Words: 14:all that it contains, and does it as none but Wendell Phillips can. His
Words: 11:voice is beautifully musical, and it is calculated to attract wherever
Words: 15:it is heard. He is a man of calm intrepidity, of a patriotic and warm
Words: 11:heart, with manners the most affable, temper the most gentle, a
Words: 11:rectitude of principle entirely natural, a freedom from ambition, and a
Words: 12:modesty quite singular. As Napoleon kept the Old Guard in reserve, to
Words: 13:turn the tide in battle, so do the Abolitionists keep Mr. Phillips in
Words: 11:reserve when opposition is expected in their great gatherings. We have
Words: 11:seen the meetings turned into a bedlam, by the mobocratic slave-holding
Words: 12:spirit, and when the speakers had one after another left the platform
Words: 12:without a hearing, and the chairman had lost all control of the
Words: 11:assembly, the appearance of this gentleman upon the platform would turn
Words: 14:the tide of events. He would not beg for a hearing, but on the
Words: 13:contrary, he would lash them as no preceding speaker had done. If, by
Words: 12:their groans and yells, they stifled his voice, he would stand unmoved
Words: 15:with his arms folded, and by the very eloquence of his looks put them to
Words: 11:silence. His speeches against the Fugitive Slave Law, and his withering
Words: 11:rebukes of Daniel Webster and other northern men who supported that
Words: 12:measure, are of the most splendid character, and will compare in point
Words: 11:of composition with anything ever uttered by Chatham or Sheridan in
Words: 11:their palmiest days. As a public speaker, Mr. Phillips is, without
Words: 12:doubt, the first in the United States. Considering his great talent, his
Words: 14:high birth, and the prospects which lay before him, and the fact that he
Words: 12:threw everything aside to plead the slave's cause, we must be convinced
Words: 13:that no man has sacrificed more upon the altar of humanity than Wendell
Words: 1:Phillips.
Words: 0:
Words: 13:Within the past ten years, a great impetus has been given to the
Words: 11:anti-slavery movement in America by coloured men who have escaped from
Words: 13:slavery. Coming as they did from the very house of bondage, and being
Words: 12:able to speak from sad experience, they could speak as none others
Words: 1:could.
Words: 0:
Words: 14:The gentleman to whom we shall now call attention is one of this class,
Words: 13:and doubtless the first of his race in America. The name of Frederick
Words: 12:Douglass is well known throughout this country as well as America. Born
Words: 15:and brought up as a slave, he was deprived of a mother's care and of
Words: 12:early education. Escaping when he was little more than twenty years of
Words: 13:age, he was thrown upon his own resources in the free states, where
Words: 12:prejudice against colour is but another name for slavery. But during all
Words: 12:this time he was educating himself as well as circumstances would admit.
Words: 12:Mr. Douglass commenced his career as a public speaker some ten years
Words: 10:since, as an agent of the American or Massachusetts Anti-Slavery
Words: 11:Societies. He is tall and well made. His vast and well-developed
Words: 12:forehead announces the power of his intellect. His voice is full and
Words: 11:sonorous. His attitude is dignified, and his gesticulation is full of
Words: 12:noble simplicity. He is a man of lofty reason, natural, and without
Words: 11:pretension, always master of himself, brilliant in the art of exposing
Words: 13:and of abstracting. Few persons can handle a subject with which they are
Words: 12:familiar better than Mr. Douglass. There is a kind of eloquence issuing
Words: 14:from the depth of the soul, as from a spring, rolling along its copious
Words: 10:floods, sweeping all before it, overwhelming by its very force,
Words: 9:carrying, upsetting, engulphing its adversaries, and more dazzling and
Words: 14:more thundering than the bolt which leaps from crag to crag. This is the
Words: 13:eloquence of Frederick Douglass. He is one of the greatest mimics of the
Words: 15:age. No man can put on a sweeter smile or a more sarcastic frown than
Words: 15:he: you cannot put him off his guard. He is always in good humour. Mr.
Words: 12:Douglass possesses great dramatic powers; and had he taken up the sock
Words: 14:and buskin, instead of becoming a lecturer, he would have made as fine a
Words: 6:Coriolanus as ever trod the stage.
Words: 0:
Words: 12:However, Mr. Douglass was not the first coloured man that became a
Words: 12:lecturer, and thereby did service to the cause of his countrymen. The
Words: 11:earliest and most effective speaker from among the coloured race in
Words: 10:America, was Charles Lennox Remond. In point of eloquence, this
Words: 10:gentleman is not inferior to either Wendell Phillips or Frederick
Words: 13:Douglass. Mr. Remond is of small stature, and neat figure, with a head
Words: 12:well developed, but a remarkably thin face. As an elocutionist, he is,
Words: 12:without doubt, the first on the anti-slavery platform. He has a good
Words: 10:voice, a pleasing countenance, a prompt intelligence, and when speaking,
Words: 13:is calculated to captivate and carry away an audience by the very force
Words: 14:of his eloquence. Born in the freest state of the Union, and of most
Words: 12:respectable parents, he prides himself not a little on his birth and
Words: 12:descent. One can scarcely find fault with this, for, in the United
Words: 11:States, the coloured man is deprived of the advantages which parentage
Words: 14:gives to the white man. Mr. Remond is a descendant of one of those
Words: 14:coloured men who stood side by side with white men on the plains of
Words: 11:Concord and Lexington, in the battles that achieved the independence of
Words: 13:the colonies from the mother country, in the war of the Revolution. Mr.
Words: 12:Remond has felt deeply, (probably more so than any other coloured man),
Words: 13:the odious prejudice against colour. On this point he is sensitive to a
Words: 16:fault. If any one will sit for an hour and hear a lecture from him on
Words: 13:this subject, if he is not converted, he will at least become convinced,
Words: 10:that the boiling cauldron of anti-slavery discussion has never thrown
Words: 11:upon its surface a more fiery spirit than Charles Lennox Remond.
Words: 0:
Words: 13:There are some men who neither speak nor write, but whose lives place
Words: 14:them in the foremost ranks in the cause which they espouse. One of these
Words: 13:is Francis Jackson. He was one of the earliest to give countenance and
Words: 13:support to the anti-slavery movement. In the year 1835, when a mob of
Words: 12:more than 5000 merchants and others, in Boston, broke up an anti-slavery
Words: 11:meeting of females, at which William Lloyd Garrison and George Thompson
Words: 14:were to deliver addresses, and when the Society had no room in which to
Words: 13:hold its meetings (having been driven from their own room by the mob),
Words: 10:Francis Jackson, with a moral courage scarcely ever equalled, came
Words: 12:forward and offered his private dwelling to the ladies, to hold their
Words: 11:meeting in. The following interesting passage occurs in a letter from
Words: 14:him to the Secretary of the Society a short time after, on receiving a
Words: 6:vote of thanks from its members:--
Words: 0:
Words: 15:"If a large majority of this community choose to turn a deaf ear to the
Words: 11:wrongs which are inflicted upon their countrymen in other portions of
Words: 13:the land--if they are content to turn away from the sight of oppression,
Words: 10:and 'pass by on the other side'--so it must be.
Words: 0:
Words: 14:"But when they undertake in any way to impair or annul my right to
Words: 11:speak, write, and publish upon any subject, and more especially upon
Words: 12:enormities, which are the common concern of every lover of his country
Words: 16:and his kind--so it must not be--so it shall not be, if I for one can
Words: 14:prevent it. Upon this great right let us hold on at all hazards. And
Words: 12:should we, in its exercise, be driven from public halls to private
Words: 11:dwellings, one house at least shall be consecrated to its preservation.
Words: 14:And if, in defence of this sacred privilege, which man did not give me,
Words: 16:and shall not (if I can help it) take from me, this roof and these walls
Words: 14:shall be levelled to the earth, let them fall if they must; they cannot
Words: 14:crumble in a better cause. They will appear of very little value to me
Words: 9:after their owner shall have been whipt into silence."
Words: 0:
Words: 12:There are among the contributors to the Anti-Slavery cause, a few who
Words: 13:give with a liberality which has never been surpassed by the donors to
Words: 10:any benevolent association in the world, according to their means--the
Words: 6:chief of these is Francis Jackson.
Words: 0:
Words: 13:In the month of May, 1844, while one evening strolling up Broadway, New
Words: 14:York, I saw a crowd making its way into the Minerva Rooms, and, having
Words: 12:no pressing engagement, I followed, and was soon in a splendid hall,
Words: 11:where some twelve or fifteen hundred persons were seated, and listening
Words: 12:to rather a strange-looking man. The speaker was tall and slim, with
Words: 13:long arms, long legs, and a profusion of auburn or reddish hair hanging
Words: 13:in ringlets down his shoulders; while a huge beard of the same colour
Words: 14:fell upon his breast. His person was not at all improved by his dress.
Words: 13:The legs of his trousers were shorter than those worn by smaller men:
Words: 13:the sleeves of his coat were small and short, the shirt collar turned
Words: 13:down in Byronic style, beard and hair hid his countenance, so that no
Words: 11:redeeming feature could be found there; yet there was one redeeming
Words: 11:quality about the man--that was the stream of fervid eloquence which
Words: 14:escaped from his lips. I inquired his name, and was informed that it was
Words: 12:Charles C. Burleigh. Nature has been profuse in showering her gifts upon
Words: 13:Mr. Burleigh, but all has been bestowed upon his head and heart. There
Words: 13:is a kind of eloquence which weaves its thread around the hearer, and
Words: 11:gradually draws him into its web, fascinating him with its gaze,
Words: 15:entangling him as the spider does the fly, until he is fast: such is the
Words: 12:eloquence of C.C. Burleigh. As a debater he is unquestionably the first
Words: 13:on the Anti-slavery platform. If he did not speak so fast, he would
Words: 12:equal Wendell Phillips; if he did not reason his subject out of
Words: 12:existence, he would surpass him. However, one would have to travel over
Words: 15:many miles, and look in the faces of many men, before he would find one
Words: 13:who has made more personal sacrifices, or done more to bring about the
Words: 10:Emancipation of the American Slaves, than Mr. Charles C. Burleigh.
Words: 0:
Words: 11:Whoever the future historian of the Anti-Slavery movement may be, he
Words: 13:will not be able to compile a correct history of this great struggle,
Words: 12:without consulting the writings of Edmund Quincy, a member of one of
Words: 10:the wealthiest, patriotic, and aristocratic families in New England: the
Words: 14:prestige of his name is a passport to all that the heart could wish.
Words: 12:Descended from a family, whose name is connected with all that was
Words: 13:glorious in the great American Revolution, the son of one who has again
Words: 11:and again represented his native State, in the National Congress, he
Words: 10:too, like Wendell Phillips, threw away the pearl of political
Words: 11:preferment, and devoted his distinguished talents to the cause of the
Words: 13:Slave. Mr. Quincy is better known in this country as having filled the
Words: 11:editorial chair of _The Liberator_, during the several visits of its

Words: 12:digging among the tombs of the friends of mankind, and of universal

Words: 8:because the engraver failed to do his duty.
Words: 0:
Words: 14:Were we sent out to find a man who should excel all others in

Words: 13:so that they would appear new, and bringing them into a meeting and

Words: 0:
Words: 12:Most men who inherit large fortunes, do little or nothing to benefit
Words: 12:mankind. A few, however, spend their means in the best possible manner:
Words: 13:one of the latter class is Gerrit Smith. The name of this gentleman
Words: 12:should have been brought forward among those who are first mentioned in
Words: 14:this chapter. Some eight or ten years ago, Mr. Smith was the owner of
Words: 13:large tracts of land, lying in twenty-nine counties in the State of New
Words: 14:York, and came to the strange conclusion to give the most of it away.
Words: 11:Consequently, three thousand lots of land, containing from thirty to one
Words: 11:hundred acres each, were given to coloured men residing in the
Words: 9:State--the writer of this being one of the number.
Words: 0:
Words: 13:Although universal suffrage is enjoyed by the whites in the State of New
Words: 11:York, a property-qualification is imposed on coloured men; and this act
Words: 14:of Mr. Smith's not only made three thousand men the owners of land, but
Words: 11:created also three thousand voters. The ability to give, and the
Words: 14:willingness to do so, is not by any means the greatest quality of this
Words: 12:gentleman. As a public speaker, Mr. Smith has few equals; and certainly
Words: 14:no man in his State has done more to forward the cause of Negro
Words: 3:Emancipation than he.
Words: 0:
Words: 12:We have already swelled the pages of this chapter beyond what we
Words: 13:intended when we commenced, but yet we have called attention to only one
Words: 11:branch of American Reformers. The Temperance Reformers are next to be
Words: 12:considered. This cause has many champions, and yet none who occupy a
Words: 10:very prominent position before the world. The first temperance newspaper
Words: 11:published in the United States, was edited by William Lloyd Garrison.
Words: 11:Gerrit Smith has also done much in promulgating temperance views. But
Words: 15:the most noted man in the movement at the present time, and the one best
Words: 14:known to the British public, is John B. Gough. This gentleman was at one
Words: 13:time an actor on the stage, and subsequently became an inebriate of the
Words: 10:most degraded kind. He was, however, reclaimed through the great
Words: 11:Washingtonian movement that swept over the United States a few years
Words: 14:since. In stature, Mr. Gough is tall and slim, with black hair, which he
Words: 13:usually wears too long. As an orator, he is considered among the first
Words: 13:in the United States. Having once been an actor, he throws all his
Words: 12:dramatic powers into his addresses. He has a facility of telling strange
Words: 11:and marvellous stories which can scarcely be surpassed; and what makes
Words: 11:them still more interesting, he always happens to be an eyewitness.
Words: 14:While speaking, he acts the drunkard, and does it in a style which could
Words: 14:not be equalled on the boards of the Lyceum or Adelphi. No man has
Words: 12:obtained more signatures to the temperance pledge than he. After all, it
Words: 14:is a question whether he has ever been of any permanent service to this
Words: 14:reform or not. Mr. Gough has more than once fallen from his position as
Words: 14:a teetotaler; more than once he has broken his pledge, and when found by
Words: 11:his friends, was in houses of a questionable character. However, some
Words: 15:are of opinion that these defects have been of use to him; for when he
Words: 11:has made his appearance after one of these debaucheries, the people
Words: 13:appear to sympathize more with him, and some thought he spoke better. If
Words: 13:we believe that a person could enjoy good health with water upon the
Words: 12:brain, we would be of opinion that Mr. Gough's cranium contained a
Words: 12:greater quantity than that of any other living man. When speaking before
Words: 14:an audience, he can weep when he pleases; and the tears shed on these
Words: 11:occasions are none of your make-believe kind--none of your small drops
Words: 12:trickling down the cheeks one at a time;--but they come in great
Words: 14:showers, so as even to sprinkle upon the paper which he holds in his
Words: 13:hand. Of course, he is not alone in shedding tears in his meetings,
Words: 12:many of his hearers usually join him; especially the ladies, as these
Words: 14:showers are intended for them. However, no one can sit for an hour and
Words: 13:hear John. B. Gough, without coming to the conclusion that he is nothing
Words: 5:more than a theatrical mountebank.
Words: 0:
Words: 12:The ablest speaker on the subject of Peace, is Charles Sumner. Standing
Words: 13:more than six feet in height, and well proportioned, Mr. Sumner makes a
Words: 11:most splendid and commanding appearance before an assembly. It is not
Words: 10:his looks alone that attract attention--his very countenance indicates a
Words: 12:superior mind. Born in the upper circle, educated in the first College
Words: 14:in the country, and finally becoming a member of the Bar, he is well
Words: 12:qualified to take the highest possible position as a public speaker. As
Words: 13:an orator, Charles Sumner has but one superior in the United States, and
Words: 12:that is Wendell Phillips. Mr. Sumner is an able advocate for the
Words: 14:liberation of the American Slaves as well as of the cause of Peace, and
Words: 8:has rendered great aid to the abolition movement.
Words: 0:
Words: 13:The name of Elihu Burritt, for many reasons, should be placed at the
Words: 14:head of the Peace Movement. No man was ever more devoted to one idea
Words: 16:than he is to that of peace. If he is an advocate of Temperance, it is
Words: 13:because it will promote peace. If he opposes Slavery, it is upon the
Words: 14:grounds of peace. Ask him why he wants an "Ocean Penny Postage," he will
Words: 12:tell you to engender the principles of peace. Everything with him hinges
Words: 13:upon the doctrine of peace. As a speaker, Mr. Burritt does not rank
Words: 13:amongst the first. However, his speeches are of a high order, some think
Words: 13:them too high, and complain that he is too much of a cloud-traveller,
Words: 12:and when he descends from these aerial flights and cloudy thrones, they
Words: 13:are unwilling to admit that he can be practical. If Mr. Burritt should
Words: 14:prove as good a statesman as a theorist, he would be an exception to
Words: 13:most who belong to the aerial school. As a writer he stands deservedly
Words: 14:high. In his "Sparks from the Anvil," and "Voice from the Forge," are to

Words: 13:His "Drunkard's Wife" is the most splendid thing of the kind in the
Words: 12:language. His stature is of the middle size, head well developed, with
Words: 10:eyes deeply set, and a prepossessing countenance, though not handsome;
Words: 11:he wears an exterior of remarkable austerity, and everything about him
Words: 13:is grave, even to his smile. Being well versed in the languages, ancient
Words: 12:and modern, he does not lack variety or imagination, either in his
Words: 11:public addresses or private conversation; yet it would be difficult to
Words: 13:find a man with a better heart, or sweeter spirit, than Elihu Burritt.

Words: 2:LETTER XXII.
Words: 0:
Words: 5:_A Narrative of American Slavery._
Words: 0:
Words: 0:
Words: 10:Although the first slaves, introduced into the American Colonies from
Words: 13:the coast of Africa, were negroes of a very dark complexion with woolly
Words: 13:hair, and it was thought that slavery would be confined to the blacks,
Words: 12:yet the present slave population of America is far from being black.
Words: 11:This change in colour, is attributable, solely to the unlimited power
Words: 12:which the slave owner exercises over his victim. There being no lawful
Words: 11:marriage amongst slaves, and no encouragement to slave women to be
Words: 13:virtuous and chaste, there seems to be no limits to the system of
Words: 11:amalgamation carried on between master and slave. This accounts for the
Words: 13:fact, that most persons who go from Europe, or from the Free States,
Words: 11:into Carolina or Virginia, are struck with the different shades of
Words: 12:colour amongst the slaves. On a plantation employing fifty slaves, it is
Words: 13:not uncommon to see one third of them mulattoes, and some of these
Words: 2:nearly white.
Words: 0:
Words: 14:In the year 1831, there resided in the state of Virginia, a slave who
Words: 15:was so white, that no one would suppose for a moment that a drop of
Words: 12:African blood coursed through his veins. His skin was fair, hair soft,
Words: 12:straight, fine and white; his eyes blue, nose prominent, lips thin; his
Words: 12:head well formed, forehead high and prominent; and he was often taken
Words: 15:for a white free person, by those who did not know him. This made his
Words: 12:condition as a slave still more intolerable; for one so white, seldom
Words: 13:ever receives fair treatment at the hands of his fellow slaves; and the
Words: 12:whites usually regard such slaves as persons, who, if not often flogged
Words: 12:and otherwise ill treated, to remind them of their condition, would soon
Words: 12:"forget" that they were slaves, and "think themselves as good as white
Words: 11:folks." During that year, an insurrection broke out amongst the slave
Words: 10:population, known as the Southampton Rebellion, or the "Nat Turner
Words: 10:Insurrection." Five or six hundred slaves, believing in the doctrine
Words: 13:that "all men are created equal," armed with such weapons as they could
Words: 12:get, commenced a war for freedom. Amongst these was George, the white
Words: 14:slave of whom we have spoken. He had been employed as a house servant,
Words: 12:and had heard his master and visiters speak of the down-trodden and
Words: 13:oppressed Poles; he heard them talk of going to Greece to fight for
Words: 10:Grecian liberty, and against the oppressors of that ill-fated people.
Words: 14:George, fired with the love of freedom, and zeal for the cause of his
Words: 9:enslaved countrymen, joined the insurrection. The result of that
Words: 12:struggle for liberty is well known. The slaves were defeated, and those
Words: 12:who were not taken prisoners, took refuge in the dismal swamps. These
Words: 11:were ordered to surrender; but instead of doing so, they challenged
Words: 12:their proud oppressors to take them, and immediately renewed the war. A
Words: 11:ferocious struggle now commenced between the parties; but not until the
Words: 12:United States troops were called in, did they succeed in crushing a
Words: 13:handful of men and women who were fighting for freedom. The negroes were
Words: 13:hunted with dogs, and many who were caught were burnt alive; while some
Words: 10:were hung, and others flogged and banished from the State.
Words: 0:
Words: 13:Among those who were sentenced to be hanged, was George. He was placed
Words: 14:in prison to await the day of execution, which would give him ten days
Words: 15:to prepare for his doom. George was the son of a member of the American
Words: 11:Congress, his mother being a servant in the principal hotel in
Words: 12:Washington, where members of Congress usually put up. After the birth of
Words: 14:George, his mother was sold to a negro trader, and he to a Virginian,
Words: 14:who sent agents through the country to buy up young slaves to raise for
Words: 12:the market. George was only about nineteen years of age, when he
Words: 9:unfortunately became connected with the insurrection. Mr. Green, who
Words: 11:owned George, was a comparatively good master, and prided himself on
Words: 12:treating his slaves better than most men. This gentleman was also the
Words: 12:owner of a girl who was perfectly white, with straight hair and
Words: 13:prominent features. This girl was said to be the daughter of her own
Words: 12:master. A feeling of attachment sprang up between Mary and George, which
Words: 13:proved to be more than mere friendship, and upon which we base the
Words: 4:burden of this narrative.
Words: 0:
Words: 13:After poor George had been sentenced to death and cast into prison, Mary
Words: 12:begged and obtained leave to visit George, and administer to him the
Words: 13:comforts of religion, as she was a member of a religious body, while
Words: 12:George was not. As George had been a considerable favourite with Mrs.
Words: 12:Green, Mary had no difficulty in obtaining permission to pay a daily
Words: 15:visit to him, to whom she had pledged her heart and hand. At one of
Words: 12:these meetings, and only four days from the time fixed for the
Words: 12:execution, while Mary was seated in George's cell, it occurred to her
Words: 14:that she might yet save him from a felon's doom. She revealed to him
Words: 11:the secret that was then occupying her thoughts, viz., that George
Words: 11:should exchange clothes with her, and thus attempt his escape in
Words: 12:disguise. But he would not for a single moment listen to the
Words: 12:proposition. Not that he feared detection; but he would not consent to
Words: 12:place an innocent and affectionate girl in a position where she might
Words: 11:have to suffer for him. Mary pleaded, but in vain--George was
Words: 12:inflexible. The poor girl left her lover with a heavy heart, regretting
Words: 6:that her scheme had proved unsuccessful.
Words: 0:
Words: 13:Towards the close of the next day, Mary again appeared at the prison
Words: 15:door for admission, and was soon by the side of him whom she so ardently
Words: 12:loved. While there, the clouds which had overhung the city for some
Words: 12:hours, broke, and the rain fell in torrents amid the most terrific
Words: 10:thunder and lightning. In the most persuasive manner possible, Mary
Words: 11:again importuned George to avail himself of her assistance to escape
Words: 12:from an ignominious death. After assuring him that she not being the
Words: 11:person condemned, would not receive any injury, he at last consented,
Words: 13:and they began to exchange apparel. As George was of small stature, and
Words: 12:both were white, there was no difficulty in his passing out without
Words: 11:detection: and as she usually left the cell weeping, with handkerchief
Words: 15:in hand, and sometimes at her face, he had only to adopt this mode and
Words: 13:his escape was safe. They had kissed each other, and Mary had told
Words: 13:George where he would find a small parcel of provisions which she had
Words: 12:placed in a secluded spot, when the prison-keeper opened the door, and
Words: 13:said, "Come, girl, it is time for you to go." George again embraced
Words: 14:Mary, and passed out of the gaol. It was already dark and the street
Words: 15:lamps were lighted, so that our hero in his new dress had no dread of
Words: 12:detection. The provisions were sought out and found, and poor George was
Words: 14:soon on the road towards Canada. But neither of them had once thought of
Words: 14:a change of dress for George when he should have escaped, and he had
Words: 14:walked but a short distance before he felt that a change of his apparel
Words: 12:would facilitate his progress. But he dared not go amongst even his
Words: 11:coloured associates for fear of being betrayed. However, he made the
Words: 14:best of his way on towards Canada, hiding in the woods during the day,
Words: 11:and travelling by the guidance of the North Star at night.
Words: 0:
Words: 13:One morning, George arrived on the banks of the Ohio river, and found
Words: 13:his journey had terminated, unless he could get some one to take him
Words: 14:across the river in a secret manner, for he would not be permitted to
Words: 14:cross in any of the ferry boats; it being a penalty for crossing a
Words: 13:slave, besides the value of the slave. He concealed himself in the tall
Words: 13:grass and weeds near the river, to see if he could embrace an
Words: 13:opportunity to cross. He had been in his hiding-place but a short time,
Words: 13:when he observed a man in a small boat, floating near the shore,
Words: 14:evidently fishing. His first impulse was to call out to the man and ask
Words: 17:him to take him over to the Ohio side, but the fear that the man was a
Words: 12:slaveholder, or one who might possibly arrest him, deterred him from it.
Words: 13:The man after rowing and floating about for some time fastened the boat
Words: 13:to the root of a tree, and started to a neighbouring farm-house. This
Words: 12:was George's moment, and he seized it. Running down the bank, he
Words: 12:unfastened the boat, jumped in, and with all the expertness of one
Words: 13:accustomed to a boat, rowed across the river and landed on the Ohio
Words: 1:side.
Words: 0:
Words: 13:Being now in a free state, he thought he might with perfect safety
Words: 14:travel on towards Canada. He had, however, gone but a few miles, when he
Words: 12:discovered two men on horseback coming behind him. He felt sure that

Words: 0:
Words: 13:After an absence of nearly two hours, the slave owner returned with an

Words: 0:
Words: 13:George, however, did not forget his promise to use all means in his

Words: 8:and was now on the road to wealth.

Words: 14:In the year 1842, just ten years after George Green (for he adopted his
Words: 11:master's name) arrived in England, he visited France, and spent some
Words: 15:days at Dunkirk. It was towards sunset, on a warm day in the month of

Words: 0:

Words: 14:"The morning after we left Mobile, Devenant met me as I came from my

Words: 14:A celebrated writer has justly said of woman: "A woman's whole life is a
Words: 13:history of the affections. The heart is her world; it is there her
Words: 12:ambition strives for empire; it is there her avarice seeks for hidden
Words: 11:treasures. She sends forth her sympathies on adventure; she embarks her
Words: 13:whole soul in the traffic of affection; and if shipwrecked, her case is
Words: 8:hopeless--for it is a bankruptcy of the heart."
Words: 0:
Words: 13:Mary had every reason to believe that she would never see George again;
Words: 12:and although she confesses that the love she bore him was never
Words: 12:transferred to her first husband, we can scarcely find fault with her
Words: 12:for marrying Mr. Devenant. But the adherence of George Green to the
Words: 12:resolution never to marry, unless to his Mary, is, indeed, a rare
Words: 15:instance of the fidelity of man in the matter of love. We can but blush
Words: 13:for our country's shame, when we recall to mind the fact, that while
Words: 11:George and Mary Green, and numbers of other fugitives from American
Words: 11:slavery, can receive protection from any of the Governments of Europe,
Words: 10:they cannot return to their native land without becoming slaves.
